Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Classification of Religious Matters and the Priority of Purification (Taharah)
Author classifies religious matters and justifies the placement of taharah before prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five categories of religious matters according to the Hanafi classification, explain why taharah is placed before prayer, and identify the proof (dalil) for the priority of prayer over other acts of worship.

Core Idea
Taharah precedes prayer because it is an indispensable condition (shart) that is never waived, and conditions logically precede the conditioned in fiqh ordering.

In opening the Book of Purification, the author first situates taharah within a comprehensive classification of all Islamic sciences. He then explains the standard fiqh ordering: acts of worship come first, prayer is the foremost act of worship, and purification precedes prayer because it is a condition for its validity. Understanding this hierarchy clarifies why every fiqh text begins with taharah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories
List the five categories of religious matters as enumerated by the author: beliefs, acts of worship, transactions, deterrents, and manners.
These categories encompass all aspects of Islamic law and practice.
2
Determine the scope of fiqh
Note that fiqh primarily deals with acts of worship, transactions, and deterrents, excluding beliefs and manners.
Beliefs are covered in theology (kalam) and manners in tasawwuf/akhlaq.
3
Understand the ordering rationale
Trace the reasoning: acts of worship are placed first due to their importance; within worship, prayer is placed first because it follows faith and is established by textual evidence (Quran 2:3 and the hadith 'Islam is built on five'); then purification is placed before prayer as its condition.
The priority is based on both importance and logical sequence.
4
Recognize the proof cited
Identify that the author cites Quran 2:3 (partial) and references the famous hadith 'Islam is built on five' to establish the primacy of prayer.
The hadith is ahad in transmission but its meaning is decisive (qati' al-dalalah).
5
Appreciate the special status of taharah
Note that taharah is singled out because it is never waived by any excuse, unlike other conditions such as facing the qiblah (which is waived in certain situations).
This makes taharah the most important condition for prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة

اعلم أن مدار أمور الدين متعلق بالاعتقادات والعبادات والمعاملات والمزاجر والآداب، فالاعتقادات خمسة أنواع : الإيمان بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله واليوم الآخر، والعبادات خمسة : الصلاة والزكاة والصوم والحج والجهاد والمعاملات خمسة : المعاوضات المالية والمناكحات والمخاصمات والأمانات والتركات والمزاجر خمسة : مزجرة قتل النفس، ومزجرة أخذ المال، ومزجرة هتك الستر ومزجرة هتك العرض، ومزجرة قطع البيضة. والآداب أربعة: الأخلاق والشيم الحسنة والسياسات والمعاشرات، فالعبادات والمعاملات والمزاجر من قبيل ما نحن بصدده دون القسمين الآخرين. وقدم في سائر كتب الفقه العبادات على المعاملات والمزاجر لكونها أهم من غيرها، ثم الصلاة قدمت على غيرها لأنها تالية الإيمان وثابتة بالنص والخبر كقوله تعالى الذين يؤمنون بالغيب ويقيمون الصلاة [البقرة: ٣] وكحديث بني الإسلام على خمس» (۱) . ثم قدمت الطهارة هنا على الصلاة لأنها شرطها والشرط مقدم على المشروط طبعاً فيقدم وضعاً، وخصها بالبداءة دون سائر الشروط لأنها أهم غيرها لأنها لا تسقط بعذر من الأعذار. كذا في المستصفى وغيره. وتعليلهم للأهمية بعدم
(۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الإيمان باب ۲،۱ مسلم في كتاب الإيمان حديث ١٩-٢٢. الترمذي في كتاب الإيمان باب .. النسائي في كتاب الإيمان باب ۱۳.
Book of Purification

Know that the pivot of religious matters is connected to beliefs, acts of worship, transactions, deterrents, and manners. Beliefs are five types: faith in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, and the Last Day. Acts of worship are five: prayer, zakat, fasting, hajj, and jihad. Transactions are five: financial exchanges, marriages, lawsuits, trusts, and inheritances. Deterrents are five: deterrent for taking life, deterrent for taking property, deterrent for violating privacy, deterrent for violating honor, and deterrent for breaking the egg. Manners are four: character, noble qualities, policies, and social dealings. So acts of worship, transactions, and deterrents are among what we are engaged in, unlike the other two sections. In all books of fiqh, acts of worship are placed before transactions and deterrents because they are more important than others. Then prayer is placed before others because it follows faith and is established by text and report, like His statement: 'Those who believe in the unseen and establish prayer' [al-Baqarah:3] and the hadith 'Islam is built on five.' Then purification is placed here before prayer because it is its condition, and the condition is naturally prior to the conditioned, so it is placed first. It is singled out for commencement over other conditions because it is more important than them, as it is not waived by any excuse. This is in al-Mustasfa and others. And their reasoning for its importance is that it is not...
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the Book of Taharah in the sharh. The author first provides a comprehensive classification of all religious sciences into five categories: beliefs (i'tiqadat), acts of worship (ibadat), transactions (mu'amalat), deterrents (mazajir, i.e., penal law), and manners (adab). He notes that fiqh primarily deals with ibadat, mu'amalat, and mazajir, excluding beliefs and manners. Then he explains the standard ordering in fiqh books: ibadat come first due to their importance, and within ibadat, prayer (salah) is prioritized because it is the most fundamental pillar after faith, citing Quran 2:3 and the famous hadith 'Islam is built on five' (referenced in the footnote). Finally, he justifies why the book of purification (taharah) is placed before prayer: it is a condition (shart) for prayer, and the condition logically precedes the conditioned. Taharah is singled out over other conditions (like facing qiblah) because it is never waived by any excuse, making it the most important. This explanation is drawn from al-Mustasfa and other works. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence that continues on the next page.

Key Terms
الطهارة (al-ṭahārah): Ritual purification, both physical and spiritual, necessary for prayer.
المعاوضات المالية (al-mu'āwaḍāt al-māliyyah): Financial exchanges such as sale, hire, etc., a subcategory of transactions.
مزجرة قطع البيضة (mazjarat qaṭ' al-bayḍah): The deterrent for breaking the egg of Islam, i.e., apostasy (riddah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the book of purification (taharah) placed before prayer in fiqh texts according to this passage?
A) Because purification is more important than prayer
B) Because purification is a condition for prayer, and the condition precedes the conditioned
C) Because purification is a pillar of prayer
D) Because purification is the first of the five pillars

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the ordering of topics reflects the logical priority: conditions precede conditioned, and more important acts precede less important ones.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
8 min
Defining 'Kitab' in Hanafi Fiqh Works
Defining 'Kitab' terminologically in fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'kitab' as a collection of independent legal topics and distinguish it from 'bab' and 'fasl'.

Core Idea
A 'kitab' in fiqh is a self-contained collection of independent legal topics, not every written work.

When you open a fiqh book, you see divisions like 'Kitab al-Taharah' or 'Kitab al-Buyu''. What exactly makes a 'kitab' a kitab? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the technical meaning of 'kitab' in the context of Hanafi fiqh works, clarifying that it refers to a collection of independent legal issues, not just any grouping of words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root of 'kitab'
The word 'kitab' comes from the root meaning to gather or collect. Understand that the term originally referred to the act of writing, but in fiqh it indicates a collection of topics.
The author gives examples like 'katabtu al-baghla' (joining with a ring) to show the root meaning of gathering.
2
Define 'kitab' technically in fiqh
Technically, 'kitab' is defined as 'a collection of independent legal topics' (جمع المسائل المستقلة). This excludes mere words or letters, and also excludes chapters (bab) and sections (fasl) which are not independent.
Independence means that the topics do not depend on prior or later material for comprehension.
3
Apply the definition to 'Kitab al-Taharah'
Although 'Kitab al-Taharah' is part of a larger work, it is considered independent because its topics are self-contained. The author refutes the idea that a 'kitab' must encompass all aspects of its subject matter.
The author criticizes al-Siraj al-Wahhaj's claim that 'kitab' in Shari'ah implies comprehensiveness.
4
Distinguish 'kitab' from 'bab' and 'fasl'
A 'bab' (chapter) and 'fasl' (section) are subordinate to a 'kitab'. They depend on the kitab for context. The kitab is the highest level of division in classical fiqh works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
السقوط أصلاً يخصها لأن النية كذلك كما صرح به الزيلعي في آخر نكاح الرقيق، فالأولى أن يزاد بأنها من الشرائط اللازمة للصلاة في كل أوقاتها وهي من خصائص الصلاة، فتخرج النية لأنه لا يشترط استصحابها لكل ركن من أركانها وليست من خصائصها بل من خصائص العبادات كلها.
ثم كتاب الطهارة مركب إضافي لا بد من معرفة جزأيه ولو من وجه؛ فالكتاب لغة مصدر كتاب كتابة وكتبه وكتاباً بمعنى الكتب وهو جمع الحروف، وسمي به المفعول للمبالغة تقول كتبت البغلة إذا جمعت بين رحمها بحلقة أو سير، وكتبت القربة إذا خرزتها كتباً. والكتبة بالضم الخرزة والجمع كُتُب بفتح التاء. والكتيبة الجيش المجتمع، وتكتبت الخيل أو تجمعت. وسميت الكتابة كتابة لأنها جمع الحروف والكلمات وجمعه كُتب بضمتين وكتب بسكون التاء ومدار التركيب على الجمع. قال في المغرب: وقولهم «سمي العقد مكاتبة لأنه ضم حرية اليد إلى حرية الرقبة، أو لأنه جمع بين نجمين فصاعداً ضعيف جداً وإنما الصحيح إن كلا منهما كتب على نفسه أمراً هذا الوفاء وهذا الآداء انتهى. وإنما كان التعليل بالجمع بين النجمين ضعيفاً لأنه ليس بلازم فيها لجوازها حالة، وضعف الوجه الأول ظاهر لأنه بالكتابة قبل الأداء لم تحصل حرية الرقبة فلم يصح الجمع بهذا المعنى.
واحداً
هذا
وفي الاصطلاح جمع المسائل المستقلة، فخرج جمع الحروف والكلمات التي ليست بمسائل، وخرج الباب والفصل لعدم استقلالهما لدخولهما تحت كتاب، وشمل ما كان نوعاً من المسائل ككتاب اللقطة، أو أنواعاً ككتاب البيوع ولا حاجة إلى أن يقال اعتبرت مستقلة ليدخل ما كان تبعاً لغيره ولم يكن مستقلاً بل اعتبر مستقلاً ككتاب الطهارة كما العناية لأن المراد بالاستقلال عدم توقف تصور المسائل على شيء قبلها ولا شيء بعدها.

كتاب الطهارة
۲۱
وهي
عرف أهل الشرع وهو بعيد ويبعده أيضاً أن ظاهره أنه لا يكون كتاباً إلا إذا أحاط بمسائل ما أضيف إليه وشملها والواقع خلافه، فالظاهر ما ذكرناه والطهارة بفتح الطاء الفعل لغة النظافة، وبكسرها الآلة، وبضمها فضل ما يتطهر به واصطلاحاً زوال الحدث أو الخبث، والحدث مانعية شرعية قائمة بالأعضاء إلى غاية استعمال المزيل وهو طبعي كالماء، وشرعي كالتراب والخبث عين مستقذرة شرعاً وكلمة (أو) في الحد ليست لمنع الجمع فلا يفسد بها الحد. وقول بعضهم إنها إزالة الحدث أو الخبث غير جامع لخروج الزوال بدون الإزالة كما إذا وقع المطر على أعضاء الوضوء من غير قصد فإنه طهارة وليس بإزالة لعدم الصنع منه، ولا يرد الوضوء على الوضوء فإنه طهارة بدون الزوال المذكور باعتبار إزالة الآثام الحاصلة لأن تسميته طهارة مجاز والتعريف للحقيقة. وعرفها في السراج الوهاج بما يدخله فقال : إيصال مطهر إلى محل يجب تطهيره أو يندب، ولو عبر بالوصول لكان أولى لما ذكرنا في ما فيه من لزوم الدور وهو توقف مطهر على الطهارة وهي عليه لأنه بعض التعريف. وفي البدائع ما يفيد أن تعرفها بالزوال المذكور توسع ومجاز فقال : الطهارة لغة وشرعاً هي النظافة والتطهير التنظيف وهو إثبات النظافة في المحل فإنها صفة تحدث ساعة فساعة، وإنما يمتنع حدوثها بوجود ضدّها وهو القذر فإذا أزال القذر أي امتنع حدوثه بإزالة العين القذرة تحدث النظافة فكان زوال القدر من باب زوال المانع من حدوث الطهارة لا أن يكون طهارة، وإنما سمي طهارة توسعاً لحدوث الطهارة عند زواله.
الإزالة مع
وأما سبب وجوبها فقيل الحدث والخبث ونسبه الأصوليون إلى أهل الطرد قالوا للدوران وجوداً وعدماً، وعزاه في السراج الوهاج إليهم. وفي الخلاصة أنه أخذ به الإمام السرخسي في الأصل ويبعد صحته عنه لأنه مردود بأن الدوران وجوداً غير موجود لأنه قد يوجد الحدث ولا يجب الوضوء قبل دخول الوقت. كذا في غاية البيان. وقد يدفع بأنه يجب به الوضوء وجوباً موسعاً إلى القيام إلى الصلاة لما نقله السراج الوهاج من أنه لا يأثم بالتأخير عن الحدث بالإجماع، وهكذا في الغسل على ما نبينه فيه إن شاء الله تعالى. فحينئذ لم يتخلف الدوران، ورد أيضاً بأنهما ينقضانها فكيف يوجبانها؟ ودفعه في فتح القدير وغيره بأنهما ينقضان ما كان ويوجبان ما سيكون فلا منافاة وأجاب عنه العلامة السيرامي بأن الحدث :
أو قد أمكن الحقيقي باعتبار أن كلاً منهما كتب على نفسه أمراً يعني وثيقة جمع الحروف فيها، ولهذا قال الشارح بعد ذكر الضعيف : أو لأن كلاً منهما يكتب وثيقة وهذا أظهر . قوله : (بالزوال المذكور) أي بزوال الحدث أو الخبث قوله : قبل دخول الوقت الظاهر أن الصواب إسقاطه أو إبدال لفظة قبل بلفظة بعد ليناسب ما بعده تأمل قوله : (وأجاب عنه العلامة السيرامي) أي عن دفع صاحب فتح القدير فهو تأييد للرد السابق وحاصله لزوم إفضاء الشيء إلى زوال نفسه وذلك باطل. قوله : (لصلوات
The Book of Purification. Its non-falling essentially belongs to it because niyyah is likewise, as al-Zayla'i clarified at the end of Nikah al-Raqiq. So it is better to add that it is among the necessary conditions for prayer in all its times and it is a characteristic of prayer. Thus niyyah is excluded because continuity is not required for every pillar of prayer and it is not a characteristic of prayer but of all acts of worship. Then 'Kitab al-Taharah' is a compound construct; it is necessary to know its two parts from some aspect. Linguistically, 'kitab' is a verbal noun meaning writing; it also means the written thing, and it is derived from the root meaning to gather. It is applied to the object for emphasis. You say 'katabtu al-baghla' when you join its womb with a ring or strap, and 'katabtu al-qirbata' when you stitch it. 'Al-kitbah' (with damma) is the stitch, plural 'kutub' with fatha on ta. 'Al-katibah' is the gathered army, and 'takattabat al-khayl' means they gathered. Writing is called 'kitabah' because it is the collection of letters and words; its plural is 'kutub' with two dammas or 'kutb' with sukun on ta; the root meaning revolves around collection. Al-Mughrib says: 'Their statement that the contract is called mukatabah because it combines the freedom of the hand with the freedom of the neck, or because it combines two or more installments, is very weak. Rather the correct view is that each of them has written an obligation on himself: this one for payment and that one for service.' The justification based on combining two installments is weak because it is not necessary in mukatabah since it is permissible at once. The weakness of the first aspect is obvious because by writing before performance, the freedom of the neck is not achieved, so the combination in this sense is incorrect. In technical terminology, 'kitab' is the collection of independent legal topics. Thus the collection of letters and words that are not legal topics is excluded, and 'bab' (chapter) and 'fasl' (section) are excluded because they are not independent, falling under a book. It includes what is one type of topic, like the book of lost property, or multiple types, like the book of sales. And there is no need to say that they are considered independent to include what is subsidiary to others and not independent but considered independent, like the Book of Purification, as in al-'Inayah, because the intended meaning of independence is that the comprehension of the topics does not depend on anything before or after it. The Book of Purification is likewise, not originality or being subsidiary, and the restriction to legal topics as in al-'Inayah is specific to this context, not a definitional restriction. What is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that it is in the Shari'ah for inclusivity and comprehensiveness is incorrect, because it is not a technical Shar'i term here but a customary term, unless one intends that it is like an ostrich and every bird, then metaphorically used for an iron helmet due to the formal similarity, and it is said: the egg of Islam for conceptual similarity, that it is gathered just as that one gathers the child. End. His statement: 'Because niyyah is likewise' — Al-Nahr says: One could object that we do not concede that niyyah and taharah are not dropped by it; rather they may be dropped by it. As for niyyah, it is in al-Qunyah: if worries befall him, his niyyah by his tongue suffices him. As for taharah, they said about one whose hands are cut off to the elbows and feet to the ankles and has a wound on his face that he prays without wudu and without tayammum and there is no repetition upon him according to the most correct view, as in al-Zahiriyyah. So if this description applies to him after the time has entered, taharah is dropped from him due to this excuse. His statement: 'And the justification based on combining two installments is weak, etc.' — Al-Nahr says: I say it is not hidden that the freedom of the neck, even if not realized, still its absence is the cause, and the principle is installments. So the apparent view is to say: combination in reality is only in physical objects, and what is mentioned of meanings... The custom of the people of the Shari'ah, and it is far-fetched, and it is also far-fetched that its apparent meaning is that it is not a book unless it encompasses the topics of what it is added to and includes them, but the reality is opposed. So the apparent is what we mentioned. And 'taharah' with fathah on the ta is the verbal noun, linguistically meaning cleanliness; with kasrah on the ta it is the instrument; with dammah on the ta it is the leftover with which one purifies oneself. Technically, it is the removal of hadath (impurity) or khabath (filth). Hadath is a Shar'i prevention residing in the limbs until the use of a remover, which is natural like water, or legal like earth. Khabath is an object deemed unclean by the Shari'ah. The word 'or' in the definition is not for preventing combination, so the definition is not invalidated by it. The statement of some that it is the removal of hadath or khabath is not comprehensive because it excludes the occurrence without removal, as when rain falls on the limbs of wudu without intention, for that is taharah but not removal due to lack of deliberate action. Nor does wudu upon wudu refute it, for it is taharah without the mentioned removal, considering the removal of accrued sins because its being called taharah is metaphorical, and the definition is for the real meaning. Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj defined it according to its prerequisite: delivering a purifier to a place that must be purified or is recommended. If he had expressed it as 'arrival' it would have been better due to what we mentioned about the circularity involved: the dependence of a purifier on taharah and taharah on it, because it is part of the definition. In al-Bada'i', there is a suggestion that defining it by the mentioned occurrence is expansion and metaphor: taharah linguistically and legally is cleanliness, and tathir is cleansing, which is establishing cleanliness in the place, for it is a quality that occurs moment by moment. Its occurrence is prevented only by the existence of its opposite, which is filth. When filth is removed — i.e., its occurrence is prevented by removing the filthy substance — cleanliness occurs. So the removal of filth is a case of removal of a preventer of the occurrence of taharah, not that it is taharah itself. It is called taharah only as an expansion because taharah occurs upon its removal. As for the cause of its obligation, it is said: hadath and khabath, and the usul scholars attribute this to the people of 'tard' (correlation) because of the correlation in existence and non-existence. Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj attributed it to them. In al-Khulasah, it is reported that Imam al-Sarakhsi adopted it in al-Usul, but its correctness from him is unlikely because it is refuted by the fact that correlation in existence is not present: hadath may exist and wudu is not obligatory before the time enters. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. It may be countered that wudu is obligatory upon hadath with an extensive obligation until standing for prayer, as al-Siraj al-Wahhaj transmitted that he does not sin by delaying after hadath by consensus. Similarly for ghusl as we will clarify there insha Allah. Thus the correlation is not absent. It is also refuted by the fact that hadath and khabath invalidate taharah, so how do they necessitate it? This was refuted in Fath al-Qadir and others by saying they invalidate what has been and necessitate what will be, so there is no contradiction. 'Allamah al-Sirami responded that hadath... Or it may be possible as true considering that each of them has written an obligation on himself, i.e., a document containing letters. Hence the commentator said after mentioning the weak view: 'Or because each of them writes a document, and this is more apparent.' His statement: 'by the mentioned occurrence' i.e., by removal of hadath or khabath. His statement: 'before the time enters' — the apparent view is to drop it or replace 'before' with 'after' to suit what follows. Ponder. His statement: 'And 'Allamah al-Sirami responded' i.e., to the refutation of the author of Fath al-Qadir; it is a support for the previous rebuttal, and its essence is the necessity of a thing leading to its own elimination, which is invalid. His statement: 'for prayers'
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the opening of the Book of Purification (Kitab al-Taharah). The author first tackles a technical point: the ruling that taharah (ritual purity) and niyyah (intention) are never entirely dropped in any circumstance. He states that taharah and niyyah share this characteristic, as both are necessary conditions for prayer but in different ways. Taharah is a condition that must be present for every part of the prayer, whereas niyyah is a condition for the entire act but not for each individual pillar; moreover, niyyah is a requirement for all acts of worship, not specific to prayer. The commentator then refutes an objection that these can sometimes be dropped, citing examples: for niyyah, one overwhelmed by worries may suffice with verbal intention; for taharah, a person with severe amputations and wounds may pray without any purification with no repetition required. However, he argues that these exceptions do not negate the ruling because the original ruling remains intact under normal circumstances. The discussion then shifts to the definition of the term 'kitab' (book) in the context of fiqh works. Linguistically, 'kitab' derives from the root meaning to collect or gather, and it is used for a written document. Technically, in fiqh, 'kitab' refers to a collection of independent legal topics. The author clarifies that it does not include mere letters or words that are not legal issues, nor does it include chapters (bab) or sections (fasl) which are subordinate. A 'kitab' can cover a single type of topic (e.g., lost property) or multiple types (e.g., sales). The independence of a 'kitab' means that its contents are self-contained and do not depend on prior or subsequent material. The author criticizes the definition from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that 'kitab' in Shari'ah implies comprehensiveness and coverage, arguing that this is not accurate because it is a customary usage and not a technical Shar'i term. He also discusses the nature of the compound 'kitab al-taharah' specifically. The passage then moves to the definition of 'taharah' itself. Linguistically, 'taharah' (with fatha on ta) means cleanliness; with kasra it refers to the instrument of purification; with damma it means the leftover purifying water. Technically, it is defined as the removal of hadath (ritual impurity) or khabath (physical filth). The author clarifies that 'hadath' is a legal prevention residing in the limbs until removed by a natural or legal substance; 'khabath' is something deemed impure by Shari'ah. The word 'or' in the definition does not imply that both conditions must occur together; rather it indicates a disjunction. He then refines the definition: it is not merely 'removal' because sometimes taharah occurs without deliberate removal, as when rainwater falls on one's limbs without intention. That is still valid taharah but not an 'izalah' (removal) because it lacks intention. Similarly, performing wudu over existing wudu (i.e., renewing wudu) is not removal of something, but it is still called taharah metaphorically, as it is believed to remove sins. The actual definition for real taharah is the occurrence of purity without necessarily requiring intentional removal. The author also critiques another definition that defines taharah as 'delivering a purifier to the required place', noting a circularity: the purifier (mutahhir) is defined by taharah, and taharah is defined by the purifier. He prefers the definition of zawaal (occurrence) as a metaphorical extension. Finally, he discusses the cause (sabab) of the obligation of taharah. Some scholars argue that hadath and khabath are the causes because of a correlation (dawaran) — when they exist, purification is required, and when they are absent, it is not. This view is attributed to the 'people of tard' (correlation theorists). The author refutes this by noting that hadath may be present without the obligation of wudu before the prayer time enters. Some attempt to defend the correlation by saying that the obligation is extensive (wujub musa') from the time of hadath until prayer time, so the correlation holds existentially. However, this rebuttal is further challenged: since hadath and khabath actually invalidate existing purity, how can they at the same time necessitate it? The response from Fath al-Qadir is that they invalidate what was (previous purity) and necessitate what will be (future purity), so there is no contradiction. A final objection is that if hadath necessitates purification, then removing hadath would require removing the very thing that necessitated it, leading to a thing causing its own elimination, which is impossible. The commentator concludes by affirming the more apparent view, as stated by al-Mughrib, regarding the definition of mukatabah. The overall lesson provides a rigorous conceptual foundation for the study of taharah in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الكتاب (al-kitāb): In fiqh terminology: a collection of independent legal topics, as opposed to a chapter (bāb) or section (faṣl) which are subordinate.
الباب (al-bāb): A chapter subordinate to a kitab, covering a subset of topics within that kitab.
الفصل (al-faṣl): A section subordinate to a bab, further dividing topics.

Key Takeaway
A 'kitab' in Hanafi fiqh is a collection of independent legal topics, distinguished from subordinate chapters and sections.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Defining 'Taharah' – Linguistic Meaning and Technical Definition
Defining 'Taharah' linguistically and technically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi technical definition of taharah as the occurrence (zawal) of hadath or khabath, and explain why it is not simply 'removal'.

Core Idea
Taharah is the occurrence of purity, not the act of removing; unintentional purification is valid.

What does it mean to be 'pure' in Islamic law? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the linguistic and technical meanings of taharah. This passage delves into the nuances, arguing that taharah is defined as the occurrence (zawal) of ritual impurity or physical filth, not the act of removing it. This distinction has practical implications, such as the validity of purification without intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meanings of 'taharah'
The word 'taharah' has three forms with different vowels: with fatha (الطهارة) the verbal noun meaning cleanliness; with kasra (الطهارة) the instrument; with damma (الطهارة) the leftover that is used for purification.
Only the fatha form is relevant for the legal concept.
2
State the technical definition of taharah
Technically, taharah is defined as 'zawal al-hadath aw al-khabath' – the occurrence of removal of ritual impurity or physical filth. The word 'aw' (or) is not for combination, so purity can be achieved by removing either one.
Hadath is a legal prevention; khabath is physical filth.
3
Understand the Hanafi preference for 'zawal' over 'izalah'
The author argues that defining taharah as 'izalah (removal) is not comprehensive because it excludes cases of unintentional purification, such as rain falling on limbs without intention. Such cases are valid taharah but are not acts of removal.
This shows the Hanafi emphasis on the result (purity) rather than the act.
4
Analyze the debate on the cause (sabab) of obligation
There is dispute over whether hadath and khabath are the causes of the obligation of taharah. Some argue correlations exist; others refute it by noting that hadath can exist without obligation before prayer time.
The author presents and critiques various positions, but the final view is that the obligation is triggered by hadath (for wudu/ghusl) and khabath (for removal of najasah).

كتاب الطهارة
السقوط أصلاً يخصها لأن النية كذلك كما صرح به الزيلعي في آخر نكاح الرقيق، فالأولى أن يزاد بأنها من الشرائط اللازمة للصلاة في كل أوقاتها وهي من خصائص الصلاة، فتخرج النية لأنه لا يشترط استصحابها لكل ركن من أركانها وليست من خصائصها بل من خصائص العبادات كلها.
ثم كتاب الطهارة مركب إضافي لا بد من معرفة جزأيه ولو من وجه؛ فالكتاب لغة مصدر كتاب كتابة وكتبه وكتاباً بمعنى الكتب وهو جمع الحروف، وسمي به المفعول للمبالغة تقول كتبت البغلة إذا جمعت بين رحمها بحلقة أو سير، وكتبت القربة إذا خرزتها كتباً. والكتبة بالضم الخرزة والجمع كُتُب بفتح التاء. والكتيبة الجيش المجتمع، وتكتبت الخيل أو تجمعت. وسميت الكتابة كتابة لأنها جمع الحروف والكلمات وجمعه كُتب بضمتين وكتب بسكون التاء ومدار التركيب على الجمع. قال في المغرب: وقولهم «سمي العقد مكاتبة لأنه ضم حرية اليد إلى حرية الرقبة، أو لأنه جمع بين نجمين فصاعداً ضعيف جداً وإنما الصحيح إن كلا منهما كتب على نفسه أمراً هذا الوفاء وهذا الآداء انتهى. وإنما كان التعليل بالجمع بين النجمين ضعيفاً لأنه ليس بلازم فيها لجوازها حالة، وضعف الوجه الأول ظاهر لأنه بالكتابة قبل الأداء لم تحصل حرية الرقبة فلم يصح الجمع بهذا المعنى.

كتاب الطهارة

وهي
عرف أهل الشرع وهو بعيد ويبعده أيضاً أن ظاهره أنه لا يكون كتاباً إلا إذا أحاط بمسائل ما أضيف إليه وشملها والواقع خلافه، فالظاهر ما ذكرناه والطهارة بفتح الطاء الفعل لغة النظافة، وبكسرها الآلة، وبضمها فضل ما يتطهر به واصطلاحاً زوال الحدث أو الخبث، والحدث مانعية شرعية قائمة بالأعضاء إلى غاية استعمال المزيل وهو طبعي كالماء، وشرعي كالتراب والخبث عين مستقذرة شرعاً وكلمة (أو) في الحد ليست لمنع الجمع فلا يفسد بها الحد. وقول بعضهم إنها إزالة الحدث أو الخبث غير جامع لخروج الزوال بدون الإزالة كما إذا وقع المطر على أعضاء الوضوء من غير قصد فإنه طهارة وليس بإزالة لعدم الصنع منه، ولا يرد الوضوء على الوضوء فإنه طهارة بدون الزوال المذكور باعتبار إزالة الآثام الحاصلة لأن تسميته طهارة مجاز والتعريف للحقيقة. وعرفها في السراج الوهاج بما يدخله فقال : إيصال مطهر إلى محل يجب تطهيره أو يندب، ولو عبر بالوصول لكان أولى لما ذكرنا في ما فيه من لزوم الدور وهو توقف مطهر على الطهارة وهي عليه لأنه بعض التعريف. وفي البدائع ما يفيد أن تعرفها بالزوال المذكور توسع ومجاز فقال : الطهارة لغة وشرعاً هي النظافة والتطهير التنظيف وهو إثبات النظافة في المحل فإنها صفة تحدث ساعة فساعة، وإنما يمتنع حدوثها بوجود ضدّها وهو القذر فإذا أزال القذر أي امتنع حدوثه بإزالة العين القذرة تحدث النظافة فكان زوال القدر من باب زوال المانع من حدوث الطهارة لا أن يكون طهارة، وإنما سمي طهارة توسعاً لحدوث الطهارة عند زواله.
الإزالة مع
وأما سبب وجوبها فقيل الحدث والخبث ونسبه الأصوليون إلى أهل الطرد قالوا للدوران وجوداً وعدماً، وعزاه في السراج الوهاج إليهم. وفي الخلاصة أنه أخذ به الإمام السرخسي في الأصل ويبعد صحته عنه لأنه مردود بأن الدوران وجوداً غير موجود لأنه قد يوجد الحدث ولا يجب الوضوء قبل دخول الوقت. كذا في غاية البيان. وقد يدفع بأنه يجب به الوضوء وجوباً موسعاً إلى القيام إلى الصلاة لما نقله السراج الوهاج من أنه لا يأثم بالتأخير عن الحدث بالإجماع، وهكذا في الغسل على ما نبينه فيه إن شاء الله تعالى. فحينئذ لم يتخلف الدوران، ورد أيضاً بأنهما ينقضانها فكيف يوجبانها؟ ودفعه في فتح القدير وغيره بأنهما ينقضان ما كان ويوجبان ما سيكون فلا منافاة وأجاب عنه العلامة السيرامي بأن الحدث :
أو قد أمكن الحقيقي باعتبار أن كلاً منهما كتب على نفسه أمراً يعني وثيقة جمع الحروف فيها، ولهذا قال الشارح بعد ذكر الضعيف : أو لأن كلاً منهما يكتب وثيقة وهذا أظهر . قوله : (بالزوال المذكور) أي بزوال الحدث أو الخبث قوله : قبل دخول الوقت الظاهر أن الصواب إسقاطه أو إبدال لفظة قبل بلفظة بعد ليناسب ما بعده تأمل قوله : (وأجاب عنه العلامة السيرامي) أي عن دفع صاحب فتح القدير فهو تأييد للرد السابق وحاصله لزوم إفضاء الشيء إلى زوال نفسه وذلك باطل. قوله : (لصلوات
The Book of Purification. Its non-falling essentially belongs to it because niyyah is likewise, as al-Zayla'i clarified at the end of Nikah al-Raqiq. So it is better to add that it is among the necessary conditions for prayer in all its times and it is a characteristic of prayer. Thus niyyah is excluded because continuity is not required for every pillar of prayer and it is not a characteristic of prayer but of all acts of worship. Then 'Kitab al-Taharah' is a compound construct; it is necessary to know its two parts from some aspect. Linguistically, 'kitab' is a verbal noun meaning writing; it also means the written thing, and it is derived from the root meaning to gather. It is applied to the object for emphasis. You say 'katabtu al-baghla' when you join its womb with a ring or strap, and 'katabtu al-qirbata' when you stitch it. 'Al-kitbah' (with damma) is the stitch, plural 'kutub' with fatha on ta. 'Al-katibah' is the gathered army, and 'takattabat al-khayl' means they gathered. Writing is called 'kitabah' because it is the collection of letters and words; its plural is 'kutub' with two dammas or 'kutb' with sukun on ta; the root meaning revolves around collection. Al-Mughrib says: 'Their statement that the contract is called mukatabah because it combines the freedom of the hand with the freedom of the neck, or because it combines two or more installments, is very weak. Rather the correct view is that each of them has written an obligation on himself: this one for payment and that one for service.' The justification based on combining two installments is weak because it is not necessary in mukatabah since it is permissible at once. The weakness of the first aspect is obvious because by writing before performance, the freedom of the neck is not achieved, so the combination in this sense is incorrect. In technical terminology, 'kitab' is the collection of independent legal topics. Thus the collection of letters and words that are not legal topics is excluded, and 'bab' (chapter) and 'fasl' (section) are excluded because they are not independent, falling under a book. It includes what is one type of topic, like the book of lost property, or multiple types, like the book of sales. And there is no need to say that they are considered independent to include what is subsidiary to others and not independent but considered independent, like the Book of Purification, as in al-'Inayah, because the intended meaning of independence is that the comprehension of the topics does not depend on anything before or after it. The Book of Purification is likewise, not originality or being subsidiary, and the restriction to legal topics as in al-'Inayah is specific to this context, not a definitional restriction. What is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that it is in the Shari'ah for inclusivity and comprehensiveness is incorrect, because it is not a technical Shar'i term here but a customary term, unless one intends that it is like an ostrich and every bird, then metaphorically used for an iron helmet due to the formal similarity, and it is said: the egg of Islam for conceptual similarity, that it is gathered just as that one gathers the child. End. His statement: 'Because niyyah is likewise' — Al-Nahr says: One could object that we do not concede that niyyah and taharah are not dropped by it; rather they may be dropped by it. As for niyyah, it is in al-Qunyah: if worries befall him, his niyyah by his tongue suffices him. As for taharah, they said about one whose hands are cut off to the elbows and feet to the ankles and has a wound on his face that he prays without wudu and without tayammum and there is no repetition upon him according to the most correct view, as in al-Zahiriyyah. So if this description applies to him after the time has entered, taharah is dropped from him due to this excuse. His statement: 'And the justification based on combining two installments is weak, etc.' — Al-Nahr says: I say it is not hidden that the freedom of the neck, even if not realized, still its absence is the cause, and the principle is installments. So the apparent view is to say: combination in reality is only in physical objects, and what is mentioned of meanings... The custom of the people of the Shari'ah, and it is far-fetched, and it is also far-fetched that its apparent meaning is that it is not a book unless it encompasses the topics of what it is added to and includes them, but the reality is opposed. So the apparent is what we mentioned. And 'taharah' with fathah on the ta is the verbal noun, linguistically meaning cleanliness; with kasrah on the ta it is the instrument; with dammah on the ta it is the leftover with which one purifies oneself. Technically, it is the removal of hadath (impurity) or khabath (filth). Hadath is a Shar'i prevention residing in the limbs until the use of a remover, which is natural like water, or legal like earth. Khabath is an object deemed unclean by the Shari'ah. The word 'or' in the definition is not for preventing combination, so the definition is not invalidated by it. The statement of some that it is the removal of hadath or khabath is not comprehensive because it excludes the occurrence without removal, as when rain falls on the limbs of wudu without intention, for that is taharah but not removal due to lack of deliberate action. Nor does wudu upon wudu refute it, for it is taharah without the mentioned removal, considering the removal of accrued sins because its being called taharah is metaphorical, and the definition is for the real meaning. Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj defined it according to its prerequisite: delivering a purifier to a place that must be purified or is recommended. If he had expressed it as 'arrival' it would have been better due to what we mentioned about the circularity involved: the dependence of a purifier on taharah and taharah on it, because it is part of the definition. In al-Bada'i', there is a suggestion that defining it by the mentioned occurrence is expansion and metaphor: taharah linguistically and legally is cleanliness, and tathir is cleansing, which is establishing cleanliness in the place, for it is a quality that occurs moment by moment. Its occurrence is prevented only by the existence of its opposite, which is filth. When filth is removed — i.e., its occurrence is prevented by removing the filthy substance — cleanliness occurs. So the removal of filth is a case of removal of a preventer of the occurrence of taharah, not that it is taharah itself. It is called taharah only as an expansion because taharah occurs upon its removal. As for the cause of its obligation, it is said: hadath and khabath, and the usul scholars attribute this to the people of 'tard' (correlation) because of the correlation in existence and non-existence. Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj attributed it to them. In al-Khulasah, it is reported that Imam al-Sarakhsi adopted it in al-Usul, but its correctness from him is unlikely because it is refuted by the fact that correlation in existence is not present: hadath may exist and wudu is not obligatory before the time enters. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. It may be countered that wudu is obligatory upon hadath with an extensive obligation until standing for prayer, as al-Siraj al-Wahhaj transmitted that he does not sin by delaying after hadath by consensus. Similarly for ghusl as we will clarify there insha Allah. Thus the correlation is not absent. It is also refuted by the fact that hadath and khabath invalidate taharah, so how do they necessitate it? This was refuted in Fath al-Qadir and others by saying they invalidate what has been and necessitate what will be, so there is no contradiction. 'Allamah al-Sirami responded that hadath... Or it may be possible as true considering that each of them has written an obligation on himself, i.e., a document containing letters. Hence the commentator said after mentioning the weak view: 'Or because each of them writes a document, and this is more apparent.' His statement: 'by the mentioned occurrence' i.e., by removal of hadath or khabath. His statement: 'before the time enters' — the apparent view is to drop it or replace 'before' with 'after' to suit what follows. Ponder. His statement: 'And 'Allamah al-Sirami responded' i.e., to the refutation of the author of Fath al-Qadir; it is a support for the previous rebuttal, and its essence is the necessity of a thing leading to its own elimination, which is invalid. His statement: 'for prayers'
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the opening of the Book of Purification (Kitab al-Taharah). The author first tackles a technical point: the ruling that taharah (ritual purity) and niyyah (intention) are never entirely dropped in any circumstance. He states that taharah and niyyah share this characteristic, as both are necessary conditions for prayer but in different ways. Taharah is a condition that must be present for every part of the prayer, whereas niyyah is a condition for the entire act but not for each individual pillar; moreover, niyyah is a requirement for all acts of worship, not specific to prayer. The commentator then refutes an objection that these can sometimes be dropped, citing examples: for niyyah, one overwhelmed by worries may suffice with verbal intention; for taharah, a person with severe amputations and wounds may pray without any purification with no repetition required. However, he argues that these exceptions do not negate the ruling because the original ruling remains intact under normal circumstances. The discussion then shifts to the definition of the term 'kitab' (book) in the context of fiqh works. Linguistically, 'kitab' derives from the root meaning to collect or gather, and it is used for a written document. Technically, in fiqh, 'kitab' refers to a collection of independent legal topics. The author clarifies that it does not include mere letters or words that are not legal issues, nor does it include chapters (bab) or sections (fasl) which are subordinate. A 'kitab' can cover a single type of topic (e.g., lost property) or multiple types (e.g., sales). The independence of a 'kitab' means that its contents are self-contained and do not depend on prior or subsequent material. The author criticizes the definition from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that 'kitab' in Shari'ah implies comprehensiveness and coverage, arguing that this is not accurate because it is a customary usage and not a technical Shar'i term. He also discusses the nature of the compound 'kitab al-taharah' specifically. The passage then moves to the definition of 'taharah' itself. Linguistically, 'taharah' (with fatha on ta) means cleanliness; with kasra it refers to the instrument of purification; with damma it means the leftover purifying water. Technically, it is defined as the removal of hadath (ritual impurity) or khabath (physical filth). The author clarifies that 'hadath' is a legal prevention residing in the limbs until removed by a natural or legal substance; 'khabath' is something deemed impure by Shari'ah. The word 'or' in the definition does not imply that both conditions must occur together; rather it indicates a disjunction. He then refines the definition: it is not merely 'removal' because sometimes taharah occurs without deliberate removal, as when rainwater falls on one's limbs without intention. That is still valid taharah but not an 'izalah' (removal) because it lacks intention. Similarly, performing wudu over existing wudu (i.e., renewing wudu) is not removal of something, but it is still called taharah metaphorically, as it is believed to remove sins. The actual definition for real taharah is the occurrence of purity without necessarily requiring intentional removal. The author also critiques another definition that defines taharah as 'delivering a purifier to the required place', noting a circularity: the purifier (mutahhir) is defined by taharah, and taharah is defined by the purifier. He prefers the definition of zawaal (occurrence) as a metaphorical extension. Finally, he discusses the cause (sabab) of the obligation of taharah. Some scholars argue that hadath and khabath are the causes because of a correlation (dawaran) — when they exist, purification is required, and when they are absent, it is not. This view is attributed to the 'people of tard' (correlation theorists). The author refutes this by noting that hadath may be present without the obligation of wudu before the prayer time enters. Some attempt to defend the correlation by saying that the obligation is extensive (wujub musa') from the time of hadath until prayer time, so the correlation holds existentially. However, this rebuttal is further challenged: since hadath and khabath actually invalidate existing purity, how can they at the same time necessitate it? The response from Fath al-Qadir is that they invalidate what was (previous purity) and necessitate what will be (future purity), so there is no contradiction. A final objection is that if hadath necessitates purification, then removing hadath would require removing the very thing that necessitated it, leading to a thing causing its own elimination, which is impossible. The commentator concludes by affirming the more apparent view, as stated by al-Mughrib, regarding the definition of mukatabah. The overall lesson provides a rigorous conceptual foundation for the study of taharah in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الطهارة (al-tahārah): Linguistically: cleanliness. Technically: occurrence (zawāl) of hadath or khabath.
الحدث (al-ḥadath): A Shar'i prevention residing in the limbs until removed by water or earth.
الخبث (al-khabath): Physical filth deemed impure by Shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi technical definition presented in this passage, which of the following is considered taharah?
A) Deliberately washing the face during wudu
B) Rain falling on one's limbs without any intention
C) Performing ghusl for janabah with intention
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Taharah is defined as the occurrence of purity (zawal), not the act of removal, which validates purification even without deliberate intention.

Definition of Taharah: Linguistic and Technical
Defines taharah linguistically and technically, critiquing alternative definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi technical definition of taharah and distinguish it from alternative definitions.

Core Idea
Taharah is the departure of impurity, not the act of removal.

Precise definitions are the foundation of fiqh. In this lesson, we examine how Hanafi scholars define taharah (purity) linguistically and technically, and why they reject definitions that equate taharah with the act of removal (izālah). Understanding this distinction clarifies when purity is achieved even without intentional action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meanings
Note the three forms: with fatha (action), kasra (instrument), damma (surplus water).
This shows lexical richness but the technical definition is what matters for fiqh.
2
Memorize the technical definition
Taharah is 'zawāl al-ḥadath aw al-khabath' – the departure of ritual impurity or physical filth.
The word 'aw' (or) does not prevent combination; it includes both.
3
Understand the components
Hadath is a legal preventer residing in the limbs; khabath is a substance deemed filthy by Islamic law.
These are the two types of impurity that taharah addresses.
4
Critique the removal definition
The author argues that 'izālah' (removal) is not comprehensive because taharah can occur without intentional removal, e.g., rain falling on limbs during wudu.
This shows the Hanafi concern for cases where purity is achieved passively.
5
Refine the concept: state vs. act
From al-Badā'i', taharah is the attribute of cleanliness that occurs when the opposite (filth) departs; the departure is merely removal of the preventer.
This reinforces that taharah is a state, not an action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطهارة بفتح الطاء الفعل لغة النظافة، وبكسرها الآلة، وبضمها فضل ما يتطهر به واصطلاحاً زوال الحدث أو الخبث، والحدث مانعية شرعية قائمة بالأعضاء إلى غاية استعمال المزيل وهو طبعي كالماء، وشرعي كالتراب والخبث عين مستقذرة شرعاً وكلمة (أو) في الحد ليست لمنع الجمع فلا يفسد بها الحد. وقول بعضهم إنها إزالة الحدث أو الخبث غير جامع لخروج الزوال بدون الإزالة كما إذا وقع المطر على أعضاء الوضوء من غير قصد فإنه طهارة وليس بإزالة لعدم الصنع منه، ولا يرد الوضوء على الوضوء فإنه طهارة بدون الزوال المذكور باعتبار إزالة الآثام الحاصلة لأن تسميته طهارة مجاز والتعريف للحقيقة. وعرفها في السراج الوهاج بما يدخله فقال : إيصال مطهر إلى محل يجب تطهيره أو يندب، ولو عبر بالوصول لكان أولى لما ذكرنا في ما فيه من لزوم الدور وهو توقف مطهر على الطهارة وهي عليه لأنه بعض التعريف. وفي البدائع ما يفيد أن تعرفها بالزوال المذكور توسع ومجاز فقال : الطهارة لغة وشرعاً هي النظافة والتطهير التنظيف وهو إثبات النظافة في المحل فإنها صفة تحدث ساعة فساعة، وإنما يمتنع حدوثها بوجود ضدّها وهو القذر فإذا أزال القذر أي امتنع حدوثه بإزالة العين القذرة تحدث النظافة فكان زوال القدر من باب زوال المانع من حدوث الطهارة لا أن يكون طهارة، وإنما سمي طهارة توسعاً لحدوث الطهارة عند زواله.
And taharah (with fatha on the ta) is the action, linguistically: cleanliness; (with kasra) the instrument; (with damma) the surplus of what one purifies with. Technically: the departure of hadath or khabath. Hadath is a legal preventer residing in the limbs until the use of the remover, which is natural like water or legal like earth. Khabath is a substance deemed filthy by law. The word 'or' in the definition is not for preventing combination, so the definition is not invalidated by it. The statement of some that it is the removal of hadath or khabath is not comprehensive because it excludes departure without removal, as when rain falls on the limbs of wudu without intention – that is taharah but not removal due to lack of action. Nor does wudu upon wudu refute this, for it is taharah without the mentioned departure due to the removal of sins, but calling it taharah is metaphorical; the definition applies to the real meaning. And al-Siraj al-Wahhaj defined it in terms of its entry, saying: Conveying a purifier to a place that is obligatory or recommended to purify. But if he had said 'arrival' it would be better, due to the circularity in the former: a purifier depends on taharah and taharah depends on it, since it is part of the definition. In al-Bada'i', it is indicated that defining it by the mentioned departure is broad and metaphorical: he said taharah linguistically and legally is cleanliness, and tat'hīr is cleaning, which is establishing cleanliness in the place, for it is an attribute that occurs moment by moment, and its occurrence is only prevented by the existence of its opposite, which is filth. So when filth is removed, i.e. its occurrence is prevented by removing the filthy substance, cleanliness occurs. Thus the departure of filth is of the category of removal of a preventer of the occurrence of taharah, not that it is taharah itself. It is only called taharah broadly because taharah occurs at its departure.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling. The author first gives the linguistic meanings of taharah: with fatha on ta' it means the act of purification; with kasra it means the instrument used; with damma it means the extra water or whatever remains after purification. Technically, he defines taharah as the departure (zawāl) of either hadath (ritual impurity) or khabath (physical filth). He defines hadath as a legal hinderance residing in the limbs until the removal is used; the remove can be natural (water) or legal (earth for tayammum). Khabath is a substance considered filthy by Islamic law. He clarifies that the word 'or' in the definition does not mean the two cannot combine, so the definition is sound. He then refutes the alternative definition that taharah is the removal (izālah) of hadath or khabath because there are cases where taharah occurs without intentional removal, such as rain falling on the limbs during wudu without one's intent — purity is achieved but it is not an act of removal. Similarly, renewing wudu (wudu on wudu) is called taharah even though there is no removal because the previous purity is still intact; that usage is metaphorical. He then cites al-Siraj al-Wahhaj's definition: conveying a purifier to a place that must or should be purified. The author criticizes this for circularity because 'purifier' depends on taharah and taharah on it. Finally, he presents the view from al-Bada'i' that defining taharah as departure is broad and metaphorical; rather, taharah is the attribute of cleanliness that occurs when the opposite (filth) is removed, and the removal of filth is merely removing a preventer. This passage shows the Hanafi concern for precise definitions in fiqh.

Key Terms
الطهارة (al-ṭahārah): Technically, the departure of hadath or khabath.
الحدث (al-ḥadath): A legal preventer residing in the limbs that requires removal for worship.
الخبث (al-khabath): A substance deemed filthy by Islamic law, such as blood or urine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is 'removal of hadath or khabath' not a comprehensive definition of taharah?
A) Because removal requires intention, but taharah can occur without intention
B) Because hadath and khabath are not real
C) Because the word 'or' prevents combination
D) Because removal is only for physical filth

Key Takeaway
Taharah is the departure of impurity, not the act of removal; the state of purity can be achieved without intentional action.

The Cause of Obligation of Taharah
Ruling with proof exploring the legal cause of taharah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the sabab of taharah, distinguish it from three alternative opinions, and apply it to a conditional divorce scenario.

Core Idea
The cause of taharah is entry of prayer time, not hadath itself — a broad obligation that becomes specific.

What is it that actually makes taharah obligatory? Is it the moment you break your wudu, or the moment it becomes time to pray? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq navigates through several opinions, refuting them one by one, before settling on the Hanafi position: the cause is the entry of prayer time. Understanding this distinction is crucial not only for fiqh but also for specific legal consequences like conditional divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opinions presented
List the four opinions on the sabab of taharah mentioned in the text: (1) hadath and khabath, (2) iqamat al-salah, (3) iradat al-salah, (4) dukhul al-waqt (the preferred view).
The first three are attributed to various scholars and groups; the fourth is the relied-upon Hanafi view.
2
Analyze the refutation of each opinion
For each opinion, extract the refutation given in the passage. Note how the author uses logical arguments (e.g., correlation fails, one wudu suffices, sin implication) to dismiss the first three views.
Pay attention to the defenses offered by proponents of those views and how they are countered.
3
Understand the preferred view and its reasoning
Focus on the conclusion that the cause is entry of prayer time, which imposes a broad obligation (wajib musawwa') that becomes specific (mudayyaq) as time narrows. Note the evidence: consensus on no sin for delaying wudu, analogy with prayer, and the fact that one wudu suffices for multiple prayers.
The concept of broad vs. specific obligation is a distinctive Hanafi contribution.
4
Apply the ruling to a practical case
Consider the conditional divorce example: 'If taharah is obligatory upon you, you are divorced.' Under the Hanafi view, divorce does not occur upon hadath but only when the time for prayer enters and the obligation becomes specific. Contrast this with the alternative views.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) mentioned in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما سبب وجوبها فقيل الحدث والخبث ونسبه الأصوليون إلى أهل الطرد قالوا للدوران وجوداً وعدماً، وعزاه في السراج الوهاج إليهم. وفي الخلاصة أنه أخذ به الإمام السرخسي في الأصل ويبعد صحته عنه لأنه مردود بأن الدوران وجوداً غير موجود لأنه قد يوجد الحدث ولا يجب الوضوء قبل دخول الوقت. كذا في غاية البيان. وقد يدفع بأنه يجب به الوضوء وجوباً موسعاً إلى القيام إلى الصلاة لما نقله السراج الوهاج من أنه لا يأثم بالتأخير عن الحدث بالإجماع، وهكذا في الغسل على ما نبينه فيه إن شاء الله تعالى. فحينئذ لم يتخلف الدوران، ورد أيضاً بأنهما ينقضانها فكيف يوجبانها؟ ودفعه في فتح القدير وغيره بأنهما ينقضان ما كان ويوجبان مفض إلى زوال نفسه ا.هـ. وفي فتح القدير والأولى أن يقال : السببية إنما تثبت بدليل الجعل وقد يدفع بأنه موجود لما رواه في الكشف الكبير عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام وضوء عن وحرف عن يدل على السببية كقوله «أدوا عمن تمونون» ولذا كان الرأس بوصف المؤنة والولاية سبباً لوجوب صدقة الفطر. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن الدليل لما دل على عدم صلاحية الحدث للسببية كان دخول «عن» على الحدث باعتبار أنه شبيه بالسبب بالنظر إلى التوقف والتكرر دليل السببية عند الصلاحية وهي منتفية فلا تدل. وقيل سببها إقامة الصلاة فهو وإن صححه في الخلاصة فقد نسبه في العناية إلى أهل الظاهر، وصرح في غاية البيان بفساده لصحة الاكتفاء بوضوء واحد لصلوات ما دام متطهراً. وقد يدفع بأن الإقامة سبب بشرط الحدث فلا يلزم ما ذكر خصوصاً أنه ظاهر الآية. وقيل سببها إرادة الصلاة وهو وإن صححه في الكشف وغيره مردود بأن مقتضاه أنه إذا أراد الصلاة ولم يتوضأ أثم ولو لم يصل، والواقع خلافه لأنه لم يقل به أحد كما أشار إليه في فتح القدير . وقد يدفع بما ذكره الزيلعي في باب الظهار بأنه إذا أراد الصلاة وجبت عليه الطهارة فإذا رجع وترك التنفل سقطت الطهارة لأن وجوبها لأجلها وفي العناية سببها وجوب الصلاة لا وجودها لأن وجودها مشروط بها فكان متأخراً عنها والمتأخر لا يكون سبباً للمتقدم اهـ . يعني الأصل أن يكون وجودها هو السبب بدليل الإضافة نحو طهارة الصلاة وهي عندهم القدير لما علمت أن أصل الوجوب كاف للسببية إلا أنه مشكل لعدم شموله سبب الطهارة للصلاة النافلة إذ لا وجوب هنا ليكون دام متطهراً) مع أن ظاهره أنه لا يكفيه ذلك بل كلما قام إلى الصلاة يلزمه الوضوء. قوله: (وظاهره أنه بدخول الوقت تجب الطهارة (الخ قال الشيخ علاء الدين بن الحصكفي في الدر المختار على تنوير الأبصار واعلم أن أثر الخلاف يظهر في نحو التعاليق نحو إن وجب عليك طهارة فأنت طالق دون الإثم للإجماع على عدمه بالتأخير عن الحدث. ذكره في التوشيح. وبه اندفع ما في السراج من إثبات الثمرة من جهة الإثم بل وجوبها موسع بدخول الوقت كالصلاة، فإذا ضاق الوقت صار الوجوب فيهما (۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الوضوء باب ٣٤ ابن ماجه في كتاب الطهارة باب ٧٤. الدارمي في كتاب الطهارة باب ٣.
As for the cause of its obligation, it is said: hadath and khabath. The usuliyyun attribute this to the people of rejection, saying for the correlation in existence and non-existence. Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj attributed it to them. In al-Khulasah, it is taken by Imam al-Sarakhsi in al-Asl, but its correctness from him is unlikely because it is refuted: the correlation in existence does not exist because hadath may occur and wudu is not obligatory before the entry of time. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. It may be responded that wudu is obligatory due to it with a broad obligation until standing for prayer, as al-Siraj al-Wahhaj reported that there is no sin in delaying from hadath by consensus, and similarly in ghusl as we will explain. So then the correlation does not fail. It was also refuted that they (hadath and khabath) nullify taharah, so how can they obligate it? Fath al-Qadir and others responded: they nullify what was and obligate what will be, so there is no contradiction. Al-Sayrami answered: hadath leads to obligation and obligation leads to existence, and what leads to what leads to a thing leads to that thing, so hadath leads to existence of taharah, and its existence leads to removal of hadath, so hadath leads to its own removal. In Fath al-Qadir, it is better to say: causality is established only by evidence of divine declaration, and it may be responded that it exists because of what is narrated in al-Kashf al-Kabir from him (peace be upon him): 'wudu' from' and the particle 'an' indicates causality, like his statement 'Pay for those you support', and thus the head due to description of expenditure and authority is a cause for the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr. It can be answered: since evidence indicates that hadath is not suitable for causality, the entry of 'an' on hadath is only because it resembles a cause in terms of dependency and repetition; evidence of causality depends on suitability, which is absent, so it does not indicate. It is said its cause is the establishment of prayer, and although it was authenticated in al-Khulasah, al-Inayah attributed it to the literalists, and Ghayat al-Bayan declared its invalidity because one wudu suffices for prayers as long as one remains purified. It may be responded that establishment is a cause conditioned by hadath, so the objection does not follow, especially since it is apparent from the verse. It is said its cause is the intention of prayer, although authenticated in al-Kashf and others, it is refuted because it would imply that if one intends prayer and does not perform wudu, he sins even if he does not pray, but that is not the case as no one said it, as indicated in Fath al-Qadir. It may be responded as mentioned by al-Zayla'i in the chapter on zihar: if one intends prayer, taharah becomes obligatory, so if he goes back and abandons the supererogatory prayer, taharah falls because its obligation is for that purpose. In al-Inayah, its cause is the obligation of prayer, not its existence, because its existence is conditioned by it, so it is posterior, and the posterior cannot be a cause for the prior. Meaning originally existence should be the cause, as indicated by the idafah like 'taharah of prayer', which among them is a sign of causality, but a preventer prevents that. And it is apparent that at the entry of time, taharah becomes obligatory, but it is a broad obligation like the obligation of prayer, so when time becomes narrow, the obligation becomes specified. Then there is no need to make its cause the obligation of performing prayer as in Fath al-Qadir, because the basic obligation is sufficient for causality, except it is problematic because it does not include the cause of taharah for supererogatory prayer, where there is no obligation. [Yet] its apparent meaning is that it does not suffice; rather, every time one stands for prayer, wudu is required. His statement: 'And it is apparent that at the entry of time taharah becomes obligatory...' al-Shaykh 'Ala' al-Din ibn al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar said: Know that the effect of the disagreement appears in matters like conditionals: 'If taharah becomes obligatory upon you, then you are divorced', without sin, due to consensus on the absence of sin for delaying from hadath. Mentioned in al-Tawshih. And with this, what is in al-Siraj regarding the establishment of consequence in terms of sin is refuted. Rather, its obligation is broad at the entry of time like prayer, so when time becomes narrow, the obligation becomes specified in both. (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari in Kitab al-Wudu, Bab 34; Ibn Majah in Kitab al-Taharah, Bab 74; al-Darimi in Kitab al-Taharah, Bab 3.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the cause (sabab) that obligates taharah. It presents multiple opinions found among Hanafi and other scholars. The first opinion is that hadath and khabath themselves are the cause. This is attributed to some and refuted because correlation fails: hadath exists but wudu is not obligatory before the prayer time enters. A response argues that the obligation is broad from the onset of hadath until prayer time, so correlation holds. A further refutation is that hadath and khabath nullify taharah, so how can they be the cause? Answers are given, including that they nullify the previous state and obligate a future one, or that hadath ultimately leads to its own removal. The second opinion is that the establishment of prayer (iqamat al-salah) is the cause, but this is invalidated because one wudu suffices for multiple prayers. A defense says it is the cause conditioned on hadath. The third opinion is that the intention of prayer (iradat al-salah) is the cause, but it is refuted because it would imply sin for not intending wudu even if not praying. The preferred view, as indicated by the conclusion, is that the cause is the entry of prayer time (dukhul al-waqt), which makes taharah obligatory with a broad obligation (wajib musawwa') similar to the obligation of prayer itself. This broad obligation becomes specific (mudayyaq) when time becomes narrow. This view is supported by the consensus that there is no sin for delaying wudu after hadath until prayer time. The passage also notes a practical consequence: in conditional divorce, if one says 'if taharah is obligatory upon you, you are divorced', it does not take effect immediately upon hadath because the obligation is not immediate; it only takes effect when the obligation becomes specific due to time constraint. This is a highly technical discussion of legal causality in Hanafi usul al-fiqh applied to a specific fiqh ruling.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): The legal cause that makes a ruling obligatory; in this context, what triggers the obligation of taharah.
الوجوب الموسع (al-wujūb al-mūsawwa‘): An obligation that is not immediate but has a broader timeframe, like the obligation of taharah from entry of time until the time becomes narrow.
الوجوب المضيق (al-wujūb al-muḍayyaq): An obligation that becomes specific and immediate when the time window narrows, such as taharah at the end of prayer time.
الحدث (al-ḥadath): A state of ritual impurity that nullifies wudu, e.g., passing wind, sleep, etc.
إقامة الصلاة (iqāmat al-ṣalāh): The act of standing for prayer or performing the prayer.
إرادة الصلاة (irādat al-ṣalāh): The intention to pray.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this passage, what is the sabab (cause) for the obligation of taharah?
A) The occurrence of hadath
B) The intention to pray
C) The entry of prayer time
D) The establishment of prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that the cause of taharah is the entry of prayer time, not the hadath itself, resulting in a broad obligation that becomes specific as time narrows.

Taharah — Purification

The Cause (Sabab) that Obligates Wudu: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute over sabab of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four views on the cause of wudu's obligation within the Hanafi school and explain why the preferred view is intention (iradah) to pray.

Core Idea
The obligation of wudu is triggered by the intention to pray, not by impurity or time alone.

The passage before us is a deep intra-Hanafi investigation into what exactly makes wudu obligatory. While it may seem that hadath (impurity) is the obvious cause, Hanafi scholars proposed multiple views: hadath itself, the establishment of prayer, the intention to pray, and the obligation of prayer. This lesson walks through each view's evidence and the final tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The core question: what is the sabab (legal cause) that makes wudu obligatory? The answer determines when a person becomes liable to perform wudu and the consequences of delay.
Distinguish sabab from shart and mani.
2
List the four opinions
The passage presents four intra-Hanafi views: (1) hadath itself, (2) iqamah al-salah (establishing prayer), (3) iradah al-salah (intention to pray), (4) wujub al-salah (obligation of prayer).
Note that the author attributes some views to specific sources.
3
Examine evidence for each view
For each opinion, note the dalil (proof) as presented: hadath relies on the hadith with particle 'an'; iqamah on the Quranic verse; iradah on practical implications; wujub on logical priority.
Focus on the strength of the evidence and the rebuttals.
4
Trace the rebuttals and counter-rebuttals
The passage refutes hadath as unfit for causality, rejects iqamah as dependent on hadath, counters iradah with the sin objection (which is resolved), and critiques wujub for not covering nafl prayers.
Notice the back-and-forth argumentation characteristic of fiqh discourse.
5
Determine the preferred view and its thamarah
The author concludes that the cause is iradah (intention) for both fard and nafl prayers. The practical consequence (thamarah) appears in conditional divorces and in the absence of sin for delaying wudu after hadath.
This is the mufta bihi position among later Hanafis.

الصلاة لا وجودها لأن وجودها مشروط بها فكان متأخراً عنها والمتأخر لا يكون سبباً للمتقدم اهـ. يعني الأصل أن يكون وجودها هو السبب بدليل الإضافة نحو طهارة الصلاة وهي عندهم من إمارة السببية لكن منع مانع من ذلك، وظاهره أنه بدخول الوقت تجب الطهارة لكنه وجوب موسع كوجوب الصلاة فإذا ضاق الوقت صار الوجوب فيهما مضيقاً وحينئذ فلا حاجة إلى جعل سببها وجوب أداء الصلاة كما في فتح القدير لما علمت أن أصل الوجوب كاف By mere possibility and it is absent. Hadath leads to obligation, obligation to existence, and that which leads to that which leads to something leads to that thing. So hadath leads to the existence of taharah, and its existence leads to the removal of hadath, so hadath leads to its own removal. End quote. In Fath al-Qadir, it is better to say: causality is only established by evidence of making (ja'l). It may be rebutted that it exists because of what was narrated in al-Kashf al-Kabir from him (peace be upon him): 'Pay on behalf of those you support', and the particle 'an' indicates causality. Hence the head, by virtue of being the one who bears expenses and guardianship, is a cause for the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr. It can be answered that since evidence indicates the inability of hadath to be a cause, the entry of 'an' upon hadath, considering it resembles a cause in terms of dependency and recurrence, is evidence of causality when there is fitness, but it is absent, so it does not indicate. It was said its cause is the establishment of prayer (iqamah al-salah). Although it was corrected in al-Khulasah, it was attributed in al-Inayah to the literalists (ahl al-zahir), and declared invalid in Ghayat al-Bayan due to the validity of sufficing with one wudu for multiple prayers as long as one remains in a state of purity. It may be rebutted that iqamah is a cause conditional on hadath, so the mentioned criticism does not apply, especially since it is apparent from the verse. It was said its cause is the intention of prayer. Although it was corrected in al-Kashf and elsewhere, it is rejected because its implication is that if one intends prayer and does not perform wudu, he sins even if he does not actually pray, but reality contradicts this as no one has said it, as indicated in Fath al-Qadir. It may be rebutted by what al-Zayla'i mentioned in the chapter of Zihar: when one intends prayer, taharah is obligatory, but if he retracts and leaves optional prayer, taharah is dropped because its obligation is for its sake. In al-Inayah, its cause is the obligation of prayer, not its existence, because its existence is conditional upon it, so it is posterior to it, and the posterior cannot be a cause for the prior. End quote. Meaning, originally its existence is the cause, as indicated by the idafah like 'taharah of prayer', which according to them is a sign of causality, but a preventer prevented that. Apparently, with the entry of time, taharah becomes obligatory, but it is a broad obligation like the obligation of prayer, so when time becomes narrow, the obligation in both becomes narrow. Hence there is no need to make its cause the obligation of performing prayer, as in Fath al-Qadir, because we know that the original obligation suffices for causality, except that it is problematic because it does not include the cause of taharah for supererogatory prayer, since there is no obligation to be continuously purified, while apparently that is not sufficient; rather, every time he stands for prayer, wudu is required. His statement: (And apparently, with the entry of time, taharah is obligatory) etc. Sheikh Ala al-Din ibn al-Haskafi said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar on Tanwir al-Absar: Know that the effect of the difference appears in conditional statements like 'If taharah becomes obligatory upon you, you are divorced', but not in sin, due to consensus that it is not sinful for delaying after hadath. Mentioned in al-Tawshih. With this, what was in al-Siraj regarding proving the fruit from the aspect of sin is refuted; rather, its obligation is broad with the entry of time like prayer, so when time becomes narrow, the obligation in both becomes narrow. His statement: (So apparently the cause is intention in obligatory and supererogatory prayer) and its obligation is lifted by abandoning the intention of prayer, or it is the intention that is connected to commencing, so what was mentioned is not valid against it.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sabab (cause) that triggers the obligation of wudu. The discussion begins by refuting the view that the mere possibility of needing wudu creates obligation. Then various opinions are presented: (1) hadath (ritual impurity) itself causes obligation, based on a hadith using the particle 'an' which indicates causality; however, opponents argue that hadath is not fit to be a cause. (2) The establishment of prayer (iqamah al-salah) is the cause, but this is attributed to literalists and criticized because one wudu suffices for multiple prayers. (3) The intention (iradah) of prayer is the cause, but this would imply sin for intending without actually praying, which is not held. (4) The obligation of prayer (wujub al-salah) is the cause, but then supererogatory prayers, which are not obligatory, would not require wudu, yet they do. The author notes that the effect of this difference appears in conditional divorce cases, not in sin (since there is consensus that delaying wudu after hadath is not sinful). The preferred view (zahir) is that the cause is the intention to pray, for both obligatory and supererogatory prayers, and that obligation lifts if intention is abandoned. This clarifies that the obligation is broad upon entry of time and narrows when time is about to expire.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): The cause that triggers the obligation of wudu; the legal cause that makes wudu obligatory.
الحدث (al-hadath): Ritual impurity that necessitates wudu (minor hadath) or ghusl (major hadath).
الإرادة (al-iradah): Intention or desire to perform an action; here specifically intention to pray.
إقامة الصلاة (iqamah al-salah): Establishment of prayer; the act of performing prayer.
وجوب الصلاة (wujub al-salah): The obligation of prayer; the fact that a prayer is obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The entry of prayer time
B) The hadath (impurity)
C) The intention to pray (iradah)
D) The establishment of prayer (iqamah)

Key Takeaway
The intention to pray is the operative cause that triggers the obligation of wudu, affecting both fard and nafl prayers.

Pillars (Arkan) of Taharah
Enumerates the pillars of ritual purification for minor/major hadath and najasah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pillars of purification for minor hadath, major hadath, and najasah, and identify the consequence of omitting a pillar.

Core Idea
Purification has four sets of pillars; missing any one invalidates the whole act.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates the essential pillars (arkan) of taharah in the Hanafi school. Unlike recommended actions, these pillars are indispensable: if any is omitted, the purification is legally invalid (batil). Knowing these ensures your worship is correctly performed from the start.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of purification
Determine whether the case involves minor hadath, major hadath, or najasah (visible or non-visible).
2
Recall the corresponding pillar
For each category, state the pillar(s) as given in the text.
Minor hadath: washing face, hands, feet, wiping quarter of head; major hadath: full body wash; visible najasah: removal; non-visible: three washes with wringing/drying.
3
Apply the consequence of omission
If any pillar is missed, the purification is invalid and must be repeated from the beginning.
This applies to all four categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأركانها في الحدث الأصغر غسل الأعضاء الثلاثة ومسح ربع الرأس، وفي الأكبر غسل جميع البدن وفي النجاسة الحقيقية المرئية إزالة عينها، وفي غير المرئية غسل محلها ثلاثاً والعصر في كل مرة إن كان مما ينعصر والتجفيف في كل ما لا ينعصر.
And its pillars in minor hadath are washing the three limbs and wiping a quarter of the head; in major hadath, washing the entire body; in real visible najasah, removing its substance; in non-visible najasah, washing the place three times and wringing each time if it is something that can be wrung, and drying each time for what cannot be wrung.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing the arkan (pillars) of taharah (purification) in the Hanafi school. The term 'arkan' here refers to the essential parts of purification; if omitted, the purification is invalid. For minor hadath (e.g., after using the toilet), the pillars are washing the three limbs (face, two hands including elbows, and two feet including ankles) and wiping a quarter of the head. For major hadath (e.g., after sexual intercourse), the pillar is washing the entire body (ghusl). For actual physical impurity (najasah) that is visible, the pillar is removing the substance itself; if non-visible (like urine that dries), the pillar is washing the affected area three times, with wringing after each wash for liquid impurities, and drying for non-wringable impurities. This distinction is crucial for validity.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): An integral part of an act; omission invalidates the act entirely.
العصر (al-'asr): Wringing or squeezing the washed garment to remove impurity-laden water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Washing three times with drying each time
C) Washing once with drying
D) Rubbing the area until dry

Key Takeaway
Purification has four distinct sets of pillars; omitting any one invalidates the act entirely.

Taharah — Purification
