The Ruling and Tools of Taharah
Defines the hukm and alat of taharah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hukm of taharah as defined by the Hanafi school, identify its primary tools, and explain why reward is not inherent to the hukm.

Core Idea
Taharah permits what is forbidden; reward depends on intention, but intention is not a condition for validity.

This foundational passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq defines what taharah (purification) does legally: it makes permissible acts that are otherwise prohibited, such as prayer. It immediately clarifies a crucial nuance: while taharah is required, its inherent hukm is only permissibility, not reward. Reward comes from intention, and in the Hanafi school, intention is a sunnah, not a condition for the purification to be valid. The passage also introduces the materials (alat) used for purification: water, earth (for tayammum), and substances annexed to them. Understanding this distinction between the legal effect and the spiritual reward is essential for correct fiqh reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the act and its ruling
Identify what is being ruled upon: taharah (purification). The ruling (hukm) is that it makes permissible (istibaha) what is not permissible without it. Write down the hukm in your own words.
This is the legal effect — the permission to perform acts like salah or touching the Quran.
2
Separate the hukm from reward
The text emphasizes that reward (thawab) is not part of the hukm of taharah. Explain why: reward depends on intention (niyyah), and niyyah is not a condition (shart) for the validity of taharah in the Hanafi school. So purification performed without intention is valid but carries no reward.
This is a key Hanafi position that differs from the Shafi'i school.
3
Identify the tools (alat)
List the tools mentioned: water (for wudu/ghusl), earth (for tayammum), and what is annexed to them (malhaq bihima, such as snow or other pure substances). Note that the details of types will come later.
Alat here refers to the physical means of purification.
4
Understand the cross-madhhab nuance
The statement 'they did not mention that reward is from its ruling' implies that some might assume reward is automatic. Contrast with the Shafi'i view that intention is required for validity, hence reward follows directly. In the Hanafi school, taharah is a condition for worship but not an act of worship in itself requiring intention.
This distinction affects how one teaches and understands wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكمها استباحة ما لا يحل إلا بها ولم يذكروا أن من حكمها الثواب لأنه ليس بلازم فيها لتوقفه على النية وهي ليست شرطاً فيها، وآلتها الماء والتراب والملحق بهما وأنواعها كثيرة ستأتي مفصلة ومحاسنها شهيرة.
And its ruling (hukm) is to make permissible what is not permissible except through it. And they did not mention that reward (thawab) is from its ruling because reward is not necessary in it, as it depends on intention (niyyah), and intention is not a condition in it. And its tools (alat) are water and earth and what is annexed to them. And its types are many, coming in detail, and its merits are well-known.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This is a ruling statement that explains the hukm (legal effect) of taharah (purification). The primary hukm of taharah is that it permits actions which are otherwise forbidden without it, such as prayer, touching the Quran, etc. The text notes that reward (thawab) is not considered part of the inherent hukm of taharah because reward depends on intention (niyyah), and in the Hanafi school, intention is not a condition (shart) for the validity of taharah (it is only sunnah). Therefore, purification performed without intention is valid but not rewarded. The alat (tools or materials) for purification are water (for wudu/ghusl) and earth (for tayammum), and other substances that are annexed to them by legal ruling. The text promises detailed discussion of types of purification later. This is a straightforward statement of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): The legal effect or consequence of taharah: making permissible what was prohibited.
الآلة (al-alah): The material or tool used for purification: water, earth, or their annexes.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention: the deliberate resolve to perform an act of worship. In this passage, it is not a condition for validity of taharah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is reward (thawab) not considered part of the hukm of taharah?
A) Because taharah is invalid without intention
B) Because reward is not necessary and depends on intention
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran
D) Because taharah is only for removing impurity

Key Takeaway
The hukm of taharah is to permit acts, but reward requires intention; in the Hanafi school, intention is sunnah, not a condition.

Conditions of Wudu: Obligation and Validity
Defines and enumerates conditions for obligation and validity of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and differentiate the nine conditions of obligation (shurut al-wujub) and the four conditions of validity (shurut al-sihhah) for wudu according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Wudu's obligation requires nine preconditions; its validity requires four conditions during the act.

Not everyone is required to perform wudu, and even those who are must ensure certain conditions are met for the wudu to be valid. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi distinction between conditions that make wudu obligatory (shurut al-wujub) and conditions that make it valid (shurut al-sihhah), a classification essential for correct fiqh application.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Two Categories
The text divides conditions into two types: conditions for obligation and conditions for validity. Grasp this dichotomy and why it matters.
The idafah (construct phrase) in 'shurut al-wujub' indicates 'conditions for obligation' (lam), not 'conditions from obligation' (min).
2
List the Nine Conditions of Obligation
Memorize: Islam, intellect, puberty, existence of sufficient pure water, ability to use it, absence of menstruation, absence of postnatal bleeding, actualization of the address (e.g., narrow time for prayer), and being in a state of hadath (ritual impurity).
The poem includes 'hadath' as the ninth; the prose lists eight but implies hadath.
3
List the Four Conditions of Validity
Memorize: direct application of water to all limbs, cessation of menstruation, cessation of postnatal bleeding, and not being engaged in a nullifying act during wudu.
These must be present at the time of performing wudu for it to be valid.
4
Apply the Distinction
Given a scenario, determine whether wudu is obligatory and whether it is valid. Use the conditions as criteria.
For example, a child's wudu is valid (if validity conditions are met) but not obligatory because puberty is a condition of obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شرائطها فذكر العلامة الحلبي في شرح منية المصلي أنه لم يطلع عليها صريحة في كلام الأصحاب وإنما تؤخذ من كلامهم وهي تنقسم إلى شروط وجوب وشروط صحة. فالأولى تسعة: الإسلام والعقل والبلوغ ووجود الماء المطلق الطهور الكافي والقدرة على [الاستعمال] وعدم الحيض وعدم النفاس وتنجيز خطاب المكلف كضيق الوقت. والثانية أربعة: مباشرة الماء المطلق الطهور لجميع الأعضاء، وانقطاع الحيض، وانقطاع النفاس، وعدم التلبس في حالة التطهير بما ينقضه في حق غير المعذور بذلك اهـ. والإضافة فيه بمعنى اللام كما لا يخفى وجعلها بمعنى من بعيد لأن ضابطها كما في التسهيل صحة تقديرها صحة مع الاخبار عن الأول بالثاني كخاتم فضة وهو مفقود هنا إذ لا يصح أن يقال الكتاب طهارة. قوله: (وهي تنقسم إلى شروط وجوب وشروط صحة الخ) وقد نظمت ذلك بقولي: شروط الوجوب تسعة تمام. العقل والبلوغ والإسلام . ونفي حيض وانتفا النفاس وحدث وضيق وقت الناس ومطلق الماء الطهور الكافي. وقدرة استعماله الموافي. وشرط صحة وذاك أربع. فقد النفاس ثم حيض يقطع . وإن يعم الماء كل الأعضا ثم انتفاء ما يفيد النقضا
And as for its conditions (shara'it), al-Allamah al-Halabi mentioned in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli that he did not find them explicitly in the words of the scholars (ashab), but they are derived from their words. And they are divided into conditions of obligation (shurut al-wujub) and conditions of validity (shurut al-sihhah). The first are nine: Islam, intellect, puberty, existence of pure purifying water sufficient, ability to use it, absence of menstruation, absence of postnatal bleeding, and actualization of the address to the responsible person such as narrowness of time. The second are four: direct application of pure purifying water to all limbs, cessation of menstruation, cessation of postnatal bleeding, and not being engaged during purification in something that nullifies it in the case of one not excused. End quote. The idafah here is in the sense of lam, as is not hidden, and making it in the sense of min is far because its criterion, as in al-Tashil, is the validity of estimation: [the validity of predication of the first by the second, like 'a silver ring'], which is absent here, for it is not correct to say 'the book is purification'. His statement: (And they are divided into conditions of obligation and conditions of validity, etc.) And I have versified it in my words: Conditions of obligation are nine complete: intellect, puberty, Islam, negation of menstruation and postnatal bleeding, hadath, narrowness of time of people, absolute purifying sufficient water, and ability for its use in proper time. And condition of validity is four: cessation of postnatal bleeding and then menstruation that cuts, and that water covers all limbs, and then negation of what brings nullification.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a definition and enumeration of the shurut (conditions) of wudu, dividing them into shurut al-wujub (conditions for wudu to be obligatory) and shurut al-sihhah (conditions for wudu to be valid). The author quotes al-Halabi's observation that these conditions are not explicitly listed in the primary texts but are deduced. The nine conditions of obligation are: (1) Islam, (2) intellect, (3) puberty, (4) existence of sufficient pure water, (5) ability to use it, (6) absence of menstruation, (7) absence of postnatal bleeding, (8) actualization of the address (i.e., the person is addressed by the command, such as when prayer time becomes narrow), and (9) arguably hadath is also a condition? The list includes 'hadath' in the poem but not in the prose. The four conditions of validity are: (1) direct application of water to all limbs (i.e., skin must be reached), (2) cessation of menstruation, (3) cessation of postnatal bleeding, (4) not being engaged in something that nullifies wudu during the act (e.g., breaking wind intentionally unless excused). The author then provides a poetic summary for memorization. The passage concludes with a brief grammatical note about the idafah (construct phrase) in 'shurut al-wujub' indicating that it means 'conditions for obligation' (lam), not 'conditions from obligation' (min).

Key Terms
شروط الوجوب (shurut al-wujub): Conditions that must be present for the act (wudu) to be obligatory on a person.
شروط الصحة (shurut al-sihhah): Conditions that must be present for the act (wudu) to be valid if performed.
الإضافة (al-idafah): The grammatical construct phrase; here it clarifies that 'shurut al-wujub' means 'conditions for obligation'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many conditions of validity (shurut al-sihhah) are there for wudu?
A) Nine
B) Four
C) Five
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sharply distinguishes between preconditions that trigger the obligation of wudu and those that render the performed wudu valid; failing to meet a condition of obligation means the act is not required, while failing a condition of validity means the act is null.

Taharah — Purification

The Hanafi Distinction Between Qat'i and Zanni Fard
Commentary expounding the definition of fard and its division into qat'i and zanni

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qat'i and zanni fard in Hanafi usul and explain the practical consequence (takfir) of denying each.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, fard is not monolithic: qat'i fard entails disbelief upon denial, but zanni fard does not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks the Hanafi definition of fard, addressing a key intra-madhhab debate: is the obligation to wipe part of the head in wudu qat'i or zanni? The resolution hinges on the nature of the proof and has profound implications for takfir (declaring someone a disbeliever). Studying this distinction sharpens your understanding of binding force in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions of fard
Note that linguistically 'fard' can mean 'cutting' or 'obligating', while technically it means 'what is binding by a definitive proof' (al-Tahrir). Understand why this technical definition is problematic.
The commentator critiques the Tahrir definition because it excludes the fard of wiping the head, which is based on a speculative proof.
2
Spot the problem: wiping the head as fard
Al-Inayah argues that wiping the head is qat'i because a khabar wahid clarifies a mujmal verse. The commentator counters that the verse is not mujmal, so the proof remains zanni.
This shows that the definition of fard must accommodate both definitive and strong speculative proofs.
3
Grasp the two types of fard: qat'i and zanni
The Hanafi jurists divide mafrud into qat'i (established by decisive proof) and zanni (established by speculative proof). Both are binding in practice, but differ in epistemological certainty.
Zanni fard is 'like qat'i in practice' – omitting it invalidates the act, but denial does not cause disbelief.
4
Understand the practical consequence: takfir
Denying a qat'i fard known necessarily from religion leads to disbelief. Denying a zanni fard does not, due to the presence of shubha (doubt) in the proof.
This is why denying wiping the head does not cause disbelief, even though it is fard.
5
Recognize the distinction between fard and wajib
Fakhr al-Islam clarifies: fard is based on decisive proof (qat'i), wajib on speculative proof (zanni). However, later Hanafis use 'zanni fard' terminologically for certain cases, blurring the line.
The passage notes that in wudu there is no 'wajib' in the technical sense, but the amount of wiping is considered zanni fard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف في الفرض لغة ففي الصحاح : الفرض الخزفي الشيء، والفرض جنس من التمر، والفرض ما أوجبه الله سمي بذلك لأن له معالم وحدوداً اهـ. وفي التلويح: المشهور أنه حقيقة في القطع والإيجاب وذهب الأصوليون إلى أنه حقيقة في التقدير مجاز في غيره لأن اللفظ إذا دار بين الاشتراك والمجاز فالمجاز أولى يقال فرض القاضي النفقة إذا قدرها اهـ. وأما في الاصطلاح ففي التحرير : الفرض ما قطع بلزومه من فرض قطع اهـ. وهو بمعنى قولهم ما لزم فعله بدليل قطعي وعرفه في الكافي بما يفوت الجواز بفوته وهو يشمل كل فرض بخلاف الأول إذ يخرج عنه المقدار في مسح الرأس فإنه فرض مع أنه ثبت بظني لكنه تعريف بالحكم موجب للدور. وفي العناية : إن المفروض في مسح الرأس قطعي لأن خبر الواحد إذا لحق بياناً للمجمل كان الحكم بعده مضافاً إلى المجمل دون البيان والمجمل من الكتاب والكتاب دليل قطعي .اهـ. وهو ينبني على أن الآية مجملة وسيأتي تضعيفه. والظاهر من كلامهم في الأصول والفروع أن المفروض على نوعين: قطعي وظني. هو في قوة القطعي في العمل بحيث يفوت الجواز بفوته فالمقدر في مسح الرأس من قبيل الثاني، وعند الإطلاق ينصرف إلى الأول لكماله والفارق بين الظني القوي المثبت للفرض وبين الظني المثبت للواجب اصطلاحاً خصوص المقام. وليس إكفار جاحد الفرض لازماً له وإنما هو حكم الفرض القطعي المعلوم من الدين بالضرورة، وذكر في العناية : لا نسلم انتفاء اللازم في مقدار المسح لأن الجاحد من لا يكون مؤوّلاً وموجب الأقل أو الاستيعاب مؤوّل يعتمد شبهة قوية وقوة الشبهة تمنع التكفير من الجانبين ألا ترى أن أهل البدع لم يكفروا بما منعوا مما دل عليه الدليل القطعي في نظر أهل السنة لتأويلهم .اهـ. وأما غسل المرافق والكعبين ففرضيته بالإجماع كما سنحققه، وكذا القعدة الأخيرة لا بفعله في الأول وخبر الواحد في الثاني، ولا بما قيل في الغاية كما قد يتوهم. وذكر في النهاية أنه يجوز أن يكون الفرض في مقدار المسح بمعنى الواجب لالتقائهما في معنى اللزوم، وتعقب بأنه مخالف لما اتفق عليه الأصحاب إذ لا واجب في الوضوء، وقد يدفع بأن الذي وقع الاتفاق عليه هو الواجب الذي لا يفوت الجواز بفوته فلا مخالفة بل يحصل بتركه النقصان والكلام هنا في الواجب الذي يفوت الجواز بفوته فلا مخالفة. والفرض بمعنى المفروض والإضافة فيه بيانية إذ الفرض قد يكون من غيره. والظني المثبت للفرض اصطلاحاً خصوص المقام. وليس إكفار جاحد الفرض لازماً له وإنما هو حكم الفرض القطعي المعلوم من الدين بالضرورة. قال فخر الإسلام في أصوله : الحكم إما أن يكون ثابتاً بدليل مقطوع به أو لا، والأول هو الفرض، والثاني إما أن يستحق تاركه العقاب أو لا، والأول هو الواجب الخ . ثم قال: وأما الفرض فحكمه اللزوم علماً بالعقل وتصديقاً بالقلب وهو الإسلام وعملاً بالبدن وهو من أركان الشرائع، ويكفر جاحده، ويفسق تاركه بلا عذر وأما حكم الوجوب فلزومه عملاً بمنزلة الفرض لا علماً . على اليقين لما في دليله من الشهبة حتى لا يكفر جاحده ويفسق تاركه . وهكذا في غير ما كتاب من كتب الأصول كالمغني والمنتخب والتنقيح والتلويح والتحرير والمنار وغيرها.
And they differed concerning the linguistic meaning of fard. In al-Sihah: al-fard is the chip of a thing, and fard is a type of date, and fard is what Allah has made obligatory, named so because it has landmarks and limits. In al-Talwih: the well-known view is that it is literally used for cutting and obligating, and the usuliyun held that it is literally used for estimation and metaphorically for other than that, because if a word is between commonality and metaphor, metaphor is preferred. It is said: the judge imposed (farada) the maintenance when he estimated it. As for the technical meaning: in al-Tahrir: fard is what is definitively proven to be binding from a definitive proof. It is equivalent to their saying: what its performance is incumbent by a definitive proof. And it is defined in al-Kafi as: what validity is lost by its omission, and it includes every fard, unlike the first definition, because it excludes the amount in wiping the head, for it is fard although established by a speculative proof; but it is a definition by the ruling leading to circularity. In al-Inayah: what is obligatory in wiping the head is definitive, because if a solitary report clarifies a mujmal, the ruling is then attributed to the mujmal, not the clarification, and the mujmal is from the Book, and the Book is a definitive proof. It is built on the verse being mujmal, and its refutation will come. The apparent from their speech in usul and furu' is that the obligatory (mafrud) is of two types: definitive (qat'i) and speculative (zanni). It is like the definitive in practice such that validity is lost by its omission. So the estimated amount in wiping the head is of the second type, and when unqualified, it refers to the first due to its perfection. The difference between the strong speculative establishing fard and the speculative establishing wajib is terminologically particular to this context. Disbelief of the denier of fard is not a necessary consequence of it; rather it is the ruling of the definitive fard known necessarily from the religion. In al-Inayah: we do not accept the necessity of the consequence concerning the amount of wiping, because the denier who is not a muta'awwil and the one who imposes the minimum or the complete wiping is a muta'awwil relying on a strong shubha, and the strength of the shubha prevents takfir from both sides. Do you not see that the ahl al-bid'ah were not declared disbelievers due to what they prevented that was indicated by a definitive proof in the view of Ahl al-Sunnah due to their ta'wil. As for washing the forearms and the ankles, its fardiyyah is by consensus, as we will verify. And likewise the final sitting, not by his action in the first nor by khabar wahid in the second, nor by what was said concerning the ghayah as might be imagined. In al-Nihayah: it is permissible that fard in the amount of wiping is in the meaning of wajib due to their meeting in the meaning of binding. It was objected that this contradicts what the ashab agreed upon since there is no wajib in wudu. It may be repelled by saying that what they agreed upon is the wajib that does not cause validity to be lost by its omission, so there is no contradiction. Rather, by its omission, deficiency occurs, and the discussion here is about the wajib that causes validity to be lost by its omission, so there is no contradiction. And fard is in the meaning of mafrud, and the idafah is bayaniyyah, since fard may be from other than it. The speculative (zanni) that establishes fard terminologically is particular to this context. And disbelief of the denier of fard is not necessary for him; rather it is the ruling of the definitive fard known necessarily from the religion. Fakhr al-Islam said in his Usul: The ruling is either established by a proof that is decisive or not. The first is fard. The second is either that its abandoner deserves punishment or not. The first is wajib. Then he said: As for fard, its ruling is binding in knowledge by the intellect, affirmation by the heart which is Islam, and action by the body which is from the pillars of shari'ah. Its denier becomes a disbeliever, and its abandoner without excuse becomes a sinner. As for the ruling of wujub, it is binding in action like fard, not in knowledge with certainty, because there is shubha in its proof, so its denier is not declared a disbeliever, and its abandoner becomes a sinner. And likewise in many books of usul such as al-Mughni, al-Muntakhab, al-Tanqih, al-Talwih, al-Tahrir, al-Manar, and others.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of 'fard' (obligatory) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins with the linguistic meaning, citing al-Sihah and al-Talwih, noting the difference between the literal meaning (cutting/obligating) and the usuli view (estimation). The technical definition is then quoted from al-Tahrir: 'what is definitively proven to be binding from a definitive proof.' The commentary critiques this definition, showing that it excludes the case of wiping the head (masah al-ra's) which is considered fard in wudu but is established by a speculative proof (khabar wahid). Al-Inayah attempts to resolve this by arguing that the khabar wahid clarifies a mujmal verse, making the ruling definitive. However, the commentator notes that this depends on the verse being mujmal, which he will refute later. The passage then explains that the Hanafi jurists actually divide mafrud (the obligatory) into two types: qat'i (definitive) and zanni (speculative). The speculative fard functions like the definitive in practice—validity is lost by its omission—but its denial does not entail disbelief, unlike the definitive fard known necessarily from religion. The example of wiping the head is given; denying it does not make one a disbeliever because of the strong shubha (doubt) in its proof. On the other hand, washing the forearms and ankles is fard by consensus, and its denial would be more serious. The passage also discusses the relationship between fard and wajib, noting that some Hanafis equate the two in terms of binding force but distinguish them by the certainty of their proofs. Fakhr al-Islam's usul is cited to confirm that fard is established by a decisive proof and that its denier becomes a disbeliever, while wajib is established by a speculative proof and its denier does not become a disbeliever. The passage concludes by listing the standard usul works that uphold this distinction.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): That which is established by a definitive proof (qat'i) or by a strong speculative proof (zanni) that functions like qat'i in practice.
القطعي (al-qat'i): A ruling established by a proof that is decisive in both transmission and meaning, such as a clear Quranic verse or mutawatir hadith.
الظني (al-zanni): A ruling established by a proof that is speculative in transmission (khabar wahid) or meaning (mujmal), yet strong enough to require action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of denying the fardiyyah of wiping the head in wudu?
A) The person becomes a disbeliever.
B) The person does not become a disbeliever because it is a zanni fard.
C) The person must repeat the prayer.
D) The person is considered a sinner but not a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul divides fard into qat'i (denial causes disbelief) and zanni (denial does not), and only qat'i fard known necessarily from religion leads to takfir.

Hanafi Definitions of Wudu and Ghusl
Commentary defining wudu and ghusl linguistically and technically, with the dispute on ghusl's meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definitions of wudu and ghusl in Hanafi fiqh, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the requirement of water flow in ghusl.

Core Idea
For ghusl, water must flow and drip; mere application like oil invalidates it.

This lesson unpacks the key purification terms wudu and ghusl as defined in the Hanafi tradition. Understanding the precise technical definitions—especially the disputed condition of water flow in ghusl—is essential for valid worship. We will explore the linguistic roots, the standard Shariah definitions, and the nuance between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani versus other views.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the linguistic derivation of wudu
Identify the root word (wada'ah) and its meaning (cleanliness/beauty). Note the different vowelizations (damm for masdar, fatha for water).
This sets the foundation for the technical usage.
2
Extract the technical definition of wudu
From the Shariah definition: washing three limbs and wiping a quarter of the head. Identify the four arkan (face, hands, feet, quarter of head).
This is the standard Hanafi position.
3
Analyze the linguistic variations of ghusl
Note the three vowelizations: fatha (action of washing), damm (noun of complete washing), kasr (cleansing agent). This clarifies the term's usage.
Understanding these helps avoid confusion in fiqh texts.
4
Identify the dispute on the technical meaning of ghusl
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require isalah (flowing) with taqattur (dripping). If water is applied without flow, ghusl is invalid in the apparent narration.
This is a mukhtalaf fih within the Hanafi school; Abu Yusuf's view may differ.
5
Connect the definitions to the fard classification
The passage repeats the qat'i/zanni fard classification. Relate this to the wajibat in wudu: e.g., wiping a quarter of the head is a zanni fard.
The repetition reinforces the epistemological weight of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوضوء مأخوذ من الوضاءة وهي النظافة والحسن وقد وضؤ يوضؤ وضاءة فهو وضيء. كذا في طلبة الطلبة وفي المغرب: إنه بالضم المصدر وبالفتح الماء الذي يتوضأ به اهـ. وفي الاصطلاح الشرعي : غسل الأعضاء الثلاثة ومسح ربع الرأس. والغسل بفتح الغين إزالة الوسخ عن الشيء ونحوه بإجراء الماء عليه لغة، وبالضم اسم من وهو تمام غسل الجسد واسم للماء الذي يغتسل به، وبالكسر ما يغسل به الرأس من خطمي وغيره. واختلف في معناه الشرعي فقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد : هو الإسالة مع التقاطر ولو قطرة حتى لو لم يسل الماء بأن استعمله استعمال الدهن لم يجز في ظاهر الرواية، وكذا لو توضأ الفرض القطعي يقال على ما يقطع الاحتمال أصلاً كحكم يثبت بمحكم الكتاب ومتواتر السنة، ويسمى بالفرض القطعي ويقال له الواجب وعلى ما يقطع الاحتمال الناشيء عن دليل كما ثبت بالظاهر والنص والمشهور ويسمى بالظني وهو ضربان ما هو لازم في زعم المجتهد كمقدار المسح ويسمى بالفرض الظني، وما هو دون الفرض وفوق السنة كالفاتحة ويسمى بالواجب اهـ وكذا قال في النهاية إن الفرض نوعان: قطعي وظني على زعم المجتهد .اهـ.
And wudu is derived from al-wada'ah, which is cleanliness and beauty. One says wadu'a yawdu'u wada'atan, so he is wadi'. Thus reported in Talbat al-Talabah and al-Mughrib: with damm it is the masdar, with fatha it is the water with which wudu is performed. In the Shariah terminology: washing the three limbs and wiping a quarter of the head. And al-ghusl, with fatha on the ghayn, is the removal of dirt from a thing and similar by flowing water over it linguistically, and with damm it is the noun of the complete washing of the body and a name for the water used for ghusl, and with kasr it is what is used to wash the head like khitmi and other things. And they differed over its Shariah meaning. Abu Hanifah and Muhammad said: it is the flowing with dripping, even if it is a drop, such that if water does not flow by using it like oil, it is not valid in the apparent narration. Likewise if one performs wudu with definitive fard, it is said for what negates possibility absolutely, like a ruling established by a decisive Quranic verse and mutawatir sunnah, and it is called qat'i fard and is also called wajib; and what negates possibility arising from evidence, as established by zahir, nass, and mashhur, and it is called zanni, which is of two types: what is considered binding in the opinion of the mujtahid, like the amount of wiping, called zanni fard; and what is below fard and above sunnah, like al-Fatihah, called wajib. Likewise in al-Nihayah it is said that fard is of two types: qat'i and zanni according to the mujtahid's opinion.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage defines key terms related to purification: wudu and ghusl. For wudu, it gives the linguistic derivation from 'wada'ah' (cleanliness) and cites lexical sources. The Shariah definition is given as: washing the three limbs (face, hands, feet) and wiping a quarter of the head. For ghusl, the passage first explains the different vowelizations of 'ghusl': fatha for the action of washing, damm for the noun of complete washing, and kasr for the cleansing agent. The main point of dispute is the Shariah meaning of ghusl: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) hold that ghusl requires water to flow over the body with dripping (isalah ma'a al-taqattur), even if just a drop. If water is applied like oil without flowing, the ghusl is not valid in the apparent narration. The passage then digresses into a repetition of the classification of fard into qat'i and zanni, which was covered in the previous lesson; this appears to be a textual redundancy. The passage concludes by noting that in al-Nihayah, fard is divided into two types according to the mujtahid's perspective.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): The flowing of water over the body with dripping, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; if water does not flow, ghusl is not valid.
الوضوء (al-wudu): Washing the face, hands (including elbows), and feet (including ankles), and wiping a quarter of the head.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi technical meaning of ghusl according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) Rubbing the body with wet hands.
B) Flowing of water over the body with dripping, even if a drop.
C) Submersion in a pool of water.
D) Pouring water without any specific condition.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ghusl requires water to flow and drip; mere application without flow invalidates the purification.

Defining Ghusl and Face Limits in Wudu
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ghusl (washing) for wudu, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the rules for washing the face including the treatment of hair.

Core Idea
Water must flow when washing wudu limbs; the face includes the earlobes, and thick hair exempts the skin underneath.

Washing the face is a fard act in wudu, but what exactly counts as 'washing' and what is the precise boundary of the face? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies both issues, including a key disagreement between the Hanafi imams. Understanding these definitions ensures your wudu is valid according to the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of ghusl
From the passage, extract the definition given by Abu Hanifah and Muhammad: causing water to flow with dripping. Note the contrast with Abu Yusuf's position of mere wetting.
The passage uses the example of using water like oil and snow to illustrate when flow is absent.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Distinguish the positions: majority (Abu Hanifah and Muhammad) require flow; Abu Yusuf suffices with wetting. Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position from the passage (zahir al-riwayah supports flow).
The passage also mentions Khalaf ibn Ayyub's recommendation for winter, which still assumes flow is the norm.
3
Define the face for wudu
Identify the limits: from the hairline (qisas al-sha‘r) to the bottom of the chin, and laterally to the two earlobes (shahmatay al-udhun). Understand that this definition is from non-standard narrations but is accepted.
The commentary clarifies each term using classical lexicons.
4
Apply the rule for hair on the face
Distinguish between thick hair (skin not visible) and thin hair (skin visible). For thick hair, washing the hair itself suffices; for thin hair, water must reach the skin underneath.
The passage notes that this is the position of the generality of scholars, and it applies to eyebrows and mustache as well.

(فائدة) ذكر بعضهم الفرق بين التفسير بأي والتفسير بيعني أن التفسير بأي للبيان والتوضيح والتفسير بيعني لدفع السؤال وإزالة الوهم .اهـ. وهذا أغلبي واصطلاح لبعض العلماء وإلا فبعضهم لا يفرق بينهما كما في حواشي ابن قاسم على جمع الجوامع . قوله : (والمراد بالخفيفة) تأويل لقول البدائع أو خفيفاً لإيهامه عدم وجوب إيصال الماء إلى ما تحت التي ترى بشرتها، كيف وقد ذكر في النهر أنه لا خلاف في وجوبه، وفي قول البدائع لأن ما تحته خرج أن يكون وجهاً الخ. إشارة إلى هذا
And 'ghusl' with fatha on the ghayn is the removal of dirt from something and the like by causing water to flow over it, linguistically. With damm it is the masdar meaning the complete washing of the body, and a noun for the water with which one bathes. With kasr it is what is used to wash the head, like khitmi and other things. They differed regarding its legal meaning: Abu Hanifah and Muhammad said: it is the causing of water to flow with dripping, even if a drop, such that if the water does not flow because he used it like oil, it is not permissible in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah). Similarly, if he performed wudu with snow and nothing dripped from it, it is not permissible. From Khalaf ibn Ayyub, he said: In winter, the one performing wudu should moisten his limbs with water like oil, then cause water to flow over them, because water repels from the limbs in winter. Thus in Al-Bada’i‘. From Abu Yusuf: It is mere wetting of the place with water, whether it flows or not. Then on both opinions, rubbing (dalk) is not part of the concept of washing; rather it is recommended (mandub). It is mentioned in Al-Khulasah that it is sunnah alone to pass the hand over the washed limbs, and the pronoun in 'his face' (wajhihi) refers to the person performing wudu derived from wudu. His statement: (It is from the hairline to the bottom of the chin and to the two cartilages of the ear) meaning the face. And 'qisas al-sha‘r' is its cutting place and the end of its growth from the front of the head or around it. It is with triple qaf and the damm is the highest. In Al-Sihah: The chin of a person is the meeting point of his two jawbones. End quote. And 'lahy' (jawbone) is the growth place of the beard of a person and others. The relative adjective is 'lahawi'. There are two jawbones and three in the plural on the pattern 'af‘ul' except that they broke the ha to preserve the ya, and the common plural is 'luhiy' on 'fu‘ul'. In Al-Maghrib: The 'laha' is the bone that has teeth. End quote. This definition of the face is narrated in other than the transmitted narration (riwayat al-usul) and its limit is not mentioned in the apparent narration. It is stated in Al-Bada’i‘: This is because it is a definition of the thing by what the word indicates linguistically, for the face (wajh) is the name for what a person faces with or what is faced in custom, and the facing occurs with this defined part. So washing it is obligatory before the growth of hair. When hair grows, washing under it falls away according to the generality of scholars, whether the hair is thick or thin, because what is under it ceases to be considered the face since it is not faced. Likewise, it is not obligatory to deliver water under the hair of the eyebrows and mustache. End quote. And by 'thin' (khafifah) is meant that whose skin is not visible. As for that whose skin is visible, it is obligatory to deliver water. His statement: 'its definition' (haddahu) that is, rubbing (dalk). His statement: the author (and to the two cartilages of the ear). It was said in Al-Nahr: it is an adjunct of sentences, for it is not correct to make it an adjunct to 'to the bottom of his chin' in the Nahr. His statement: 'i.e., the face' is an interpretation of the referent of the pronoun, said al-Ramli. (Benefit): Some mentioned the difference between interpretation with 'ay' and interpretation with 'ya‘ni': that interpretation with 'ay' is for clarification and explanation, and interpretation with 'ya‘ni' is to repel a question and remove a misunderstanding. End quote. This is dominant and a convention of some scholars, otherwise some do not differentiate between them, as in the footnotes of Ibn Qasim on Jam‘ al-Jawami‘. His statement: 'and by thin is meant' is an interpretation of the saying of Al-Bada’i‘ 'or thin' because it suggests that there is no obligation to deliver water under that which shows its skin. How so, when it is mentioned in Al-Nahr that there is no disagreement regarding its obligation. And in the saying of Al-Bada’i‘ 'because what is under it ceases to be the face...' it is an indication to this.

Commentary
This passage addresses two key rulings for wudu: the definition of washing (ghusl) and the boundaries of the face (wajh). First, it explains the linguistic meanings of 'ghusl' with different vowels, then the legal meaning. The central dispute is between Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, who require water to flow with dripping (isalah ma‘a al-taqatir), and Abu Yusuf, who suffices with mere wetting (mujarrad ball). The passage supports the flow requirement with examples (using water like oil, using snow) and cites a recommendation from Khalaf ibn Ayyub for winter. It clarifies that rubbing (dalk) is not part of the definition but is a separate sunnah. The second part defines the face for the purpose of wudu. The matn states the limits: from the hairline (qisas al-sha‘r) to the bottom of the chin and the two earlobes (shahmatay al-udhun). The commentary explains each term, citing classical lexicons. It then discusses a crucial ruling: when thick hair (like a beard) grows on the face, the obligation to wash the skin underneath falls away because the hair itself becomes the surface that is faced. However, thin hair through which the skin is visible requires the underlying skin to be washed. The passage also notes that the definition of the face is from non-standard narrations (ghayr riwayat al-usul), but it is accepted. Finally, it includes grammatical notes on the text and a distinction between 'ay' and 'ya‘ni' in interpretation.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): The legal definition of washing: causing water to flow over the limb with dripping; mere wetting is insufficient according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad.
الوجه (al-wajh): The face is defined from the hairline (qisas al-sha‘r) to the chin, including the earlobes (shahmatay al-udhun). It is the area that is faced in normal interaction.
شحمتا الأذن (shahmatay al-udhun): The two cartilages of the ear (the soft lower part). The face extends to these points.
الدلك (al-dalk): Rubbing of the limbs during washing; it is not part of the definition of ghusl but is a separate sunnah (or mustahabb).
الخفيفة (al-khafifah): Thin hair on the face through which the skin is visible. The ruling: water must reach the skin under thin hair, but not under thick hair that hides the skin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi requires water to flow when washing wudu limbs, and the face is defined from hairline to chin including earlobes, with thick hair exempting the skin underneath.

Boundaries of the Face in Wudu
Defining the boundaries of the face for obligatory washing in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the face for wudu (hairline to chin, including earlobes) and apply the ruling that washing under hair is not obligatory after hair growth.

Core Idea
The face in wudu extends from the hairline to the chin, including the earlobes; after hair growth, washing under it is not required.

Washing the face is a fard of wudu, but what exactly counts as the face? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definition, clarifying the precise boundaries based on linguistic and customary usage. Understanding this definition ensures your wudu is valid according to the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three boundaries
Locate the upper bound (hairline), lower bound (bottom of chin), and lateral bounds (earlobes) in the text. Note that the Arabic terms are defined linguistically.
The text also defines 'chin' (dhaqan) as the meeting of the jawbones.
2
Recognize the source of this definition
Note that this definition is from non-principal narrations (nawadir), not from zahir al-riwaya. It is derived from linguistic and customary understanding of 'face'.
Al-Bada'i explains it as a linguistic definition.
3
Apply the hair growth exception
The text states that before hair growth (e.g., beard), the entire area is washed. After hair grows, washing under the hair is dropped by general consensus.
This will be detailed in a later lesson.
4
Connect to the ruling (hukm)
Washing the face is fard ayn. The definition determines what must be washed for validity. Ensure all three zones are covered.
If any part within these boundaries is missed, wudu is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وهو من قصاص الشعر إلى أسفل الذقن وإلى شحمتي الأذن) أي الوجه وقصاص الشعر مقطعه ومنتهى منبته من مقدم الرأس أو حواليه وهو مثلث القاف والضم أعلاها. وفي الصحاح : ذقن الإنسان مجتمع لحييه .اهـ واللحي منبت اللحية من الإنسان وغيره والنسبة إليه لحوي وهما لحيان وثلاثة ألح على «أفعل إلا أنهم كسروا الحاء لتسلم الياء والكثير لحي على فعول». وفي المغرب : اللحى العظم الذي عليه الأسنان اهـ. وهذا الحد للوجه مروي في غير رواية الأصول ولم يذكر حده في ظاهر الرواية. قال في البدائع : وهذا لأنه تحديد الشيء بما ينبيء عنه اللفظ لغة لأن الوجه اسم لما يواجه به الإنسان أو ما يواجه إليه في العادة والمواجهة تقع بهذا المحدود فوجب غسله قبل نبات الشعر، فإذا نبت الشعر يسقط غسل ما تحته عند عامة العلماء
His saying: (and it is from the forelock hair to the bottom of the chin and to the lobes of the ears) i.e. the face. And 'forelock hair' means its cut and the end of its growth from the front of the head or its surroundings, and it is pronounced with the qaf having three vowels, with damm being the highest. And in al-Sihah: the chin of a person is the meeting place of his jawbones. End. And the 'lahy' is the growth place of the beard of a human and others, and its relative adjective is 'lahawi', and there are two 'lahyan' and three 'al-hun' on the pattern 'af'al' except that they broke the ha to preserve the ya, and the plural is 'lahin' on 'fu'ul'. And in al-Maghrib: the 'laha' is the bone upon which the teeth are. End. This definition of the face is narrated in other than the principal narrations, and its limit was not mentioned in the apparent narration. He said in al-Bada'i: This is because it is a definition of something by what the word indicates linguistically, for the face is a name for what one faces with or what one faces towards customarily, and facing occurs with this defined portion, so washing it was obligatory before the growth of hair. When hair grows, washing underneath it is dropped according to the generality of scholars.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines the face (al-wajh) for wudu, a definition-ruling. The commentator explains the boundaries: from the hairline (قصاص الشعر) to the bottom of the chin (أسفل الذقن), and laterally to the ear lobes (شحمتي الأذن). This definition is not from the explicit texts of the Hanafi school's principal narrations (zahir al-riwaya) but is mentioned in other narrations. Al-Bada'i justifies it linguistically: the face is what one faces others with, and this area is customary. Before beard or hair grows, the entire area must be washed; after hair growth, washing under the hair is not obligatory according to the majority of scholars (which will be detailed in a later lesson). The passage also clarifies the linguistic terms for chin, jaw, etc.

Key Terms
قصاص الشعر (qisās al-sha‘r): The beginning of the hair growth on the forehead; the hairline
شحمة الأذن (shahmat al-udhun): The soft, hanging part of the outer ear; the earlobe
ذقن (dhaqan): The chin; the meeting point of the jawbones

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The face in wudu is precisely defined as from the hairline to the chin, including the earlobes; after hair growth, washing under the hair is not obligatory.

Washing Under Hair in Wudu: Thick vs Thin
Ruling on obligation to wash skin under hair based on thickness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on washing under hair in wudu and apply the criterion of thinness to determine obligation.

Core Idea
In Hanafi wudu, only thin hair requires washing the skin underneath; thick hair suffices with passing water over it.

When performing wudu, you must wash your face. But what about hair that grows on the face, like a beard, mustache, or eyebrows? Does water have to reach the skin underneath? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq clarifies the Hanafi position: the obligation depends on the thickness and visibility of the skin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the act
The act is washing the skin under facial hair during wudu. The ruling is that it is obligatory (wajib) only under certain conditions.
2
Understand the criterion: thick vs thin
Thin hair allows the skin to be visible; thick hair conceals it. If the skin is not visible, washing underneath is not required.
The text says 'as for that whose skin is visible, it is obligatory.'
3
Identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
According to al-Walwaliji and other later authorities, the fatwa is that it is not obligatory to wash under thick hair, including the mustache and eyebrows.
4
Apply to specific facial features
Apply the rule: mustache only if roots appear; eyebrows always thick so no need; lips: the concealed part follows the mouth, the visible part is part of the face.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on lips
Abu Ja'far said the concealed part of the lip when closed is part of the mouth and need not be washed, while the visible part is part of the face. This was corrected in al-Khulasa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بالخفيفة التي لا ترى بشرتها، أما التي ترى بشرتها فإنه يجب إيصال الماء إلى ما تحتها . كذا في فتح القدير. وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل قول من قال أنه يجب إيصال الماء إلى ما تحت شعر الشارب على ما إذا كان بحيث يبدو منابت الشعر، وقد جعله في التجنيس من الآداب، وصرح الولوالجي في باب الكراهية على أن المفتى به أنه لا يجب إيصال الماء إلى ما تحته كالحاجبين. وأما الشفة فقيل تبع للفم. وقال أبو جعفر : ما انكتم عند انضمامه فهو تبع له وما ظهر فللوجه وصححه في الخلاصة.
And what is meant by thin (hair) is that whose skin is not visible. As for that whose skin is visible, it is obligatory to deliver water to underneath it. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And upon this should be carried the statement of whoever said that it is obligatory to deliver water to under the mustache hair, in the case that the roots of hair appear. And he made it in al-Tajnis from the etiquette. And al-Walwaliji explicitly stated in the chapter of dislike that the fatwa is that it is not obligatory to deliver water to underneath it, like the eyebrows. As for the lip, it is said it follows the mouth. And Abu Ja'far said: What is concealed when the lips are closed is part of the mouth, and what is apparent is part of the face, and this was corrected in al-Khulasa.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage, a ruling with proof, clarifies the obligation of washing under hair during wudu based on hair thickness. The criterion: if hair is so thick that the underlying skin is not visible, it is not obligatory to wash the skin (the water only needs to pass over the hair). If the skin is visible (i.e., thin or sparse hair), then it is obligatory to ensure water reaches the skin. This includes the mustache: if sparse enough to see the roots, water must reach them; otherwise not. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to al-Walwaliji is that water need not reach under the mustache, similar to eyebrows. The lip is considered part of the mouth when closed, but the visible part is part of the face and must be washed. This ruling is based on the general principle that the face is what is visible and customarily faced.

Key Terms
خفيفة (khafīfa): Thin or sparse hair through which the skin is visible
كثيفة (kathīfa): Thick hair that conceals the skin

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this text, when is it obligatory to wash the skin under the mustache?
A) Always
B) When the mustache is thick and hides the skin
C) When the roots of the hair are visible through thinness
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The obligation to wash under hair in wudu depends on whether the skin is visible through the hair; thick hair (skin not visible) does not require washing underneath.

Sideburn and Earlobe as Part of the Face (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Ruling on whether the white area between sideburn and ear is part of the face for wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether the white area between the sideburn and ear is part of the face for wudu, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The white strip between sideburn and ear is wajib to wash – Abu Yusuf disagreed, but the school affirms it.

In the Hanafi school, the face for wudu includes the area from the forehead hairline to the bottom of the jawbone and from ear to ear. But what about the white strip between the sideburn and the ear? This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute, clarifying that the relied-upon position includes it. It also addresses edge cases like baldness, which further refine the definition of the face's boundary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed area
The white skin between the sideburn and the ear. Is it part of the face?
2
State the majority Hanafi position
It is part of the face and must be washed. This is the zahir al-madhhab supported by al-Hulwani, al-Tahawi, and al-Sarakhsi.
3
Recognize the opponent
Abu Yusuf holds it is not part of the face. This is mentioned in al-Bada'i.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The definition of the face is not based solely on the hairline; the hairline is for the normal case. Edge cases like the aghamm (low hairline) and asla‘ (bald) show the true limit is the forehead surface to the jawbone, not the hairline.
Al-Mujtaba notes a view that if the hair is little it is part of the face, but the correct view is it is part of the head.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
In wudu, ensure washing from the ear to the beard including the white area. For a person with receding hairline, the limit is the forehead surface, not the hairline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد المصنف أن البياض الذي بين العذار والأذن من الوجه فيجب غسله وهو ظاهر المذهب كما ذكره الحلواني وهو الصحيح وعليه أكثر مشايخنا كما ذكره الطحاوي وهو الصحيح من المذهب كما ذكره السرخسي وعن أبي يوسف عدمه كذا في البدائع ، وظاهره أن مذهبه بخلافه. وفي تبيين الحقائق أن قوله من قصاص الشعر» خرج مخرج الغالب وإلا فحد الوجه في الطول من مبدأ سطح الجبهة إلى منتهى اللحيين، كان عليه شعر أو لم يكن اهـ . لأنه يرد عليه الأغم والأصلع لأن الأغم الذي على جبهته شعر لا يكفي غسله من قصاص شعره، والأصلع الذي انحسر شعره إلى وسط رأسه لا يجب عليه أن يغسله من قصاص شعره على الأصح كما في الخلاصه. وصرح في المجتبى بالخلاف فيه فقيل إن قل فمن الوجه، وإن كثر فمن الرأس، والصحيح أنه من الرأس حتى جاز المسح عليه.
And the author (al-Imam) indicated that the whiteness that is between the sideburn and the ear is from the face, so it is obligatory to wash it. That is the apparent school (madhhab) as al-Hulwani mentioned, and it is the correct (sahih) position, and it is upon it the majority of our scholars as al-Tahawi mentioned, and it is the correct position of the school as al-Sarakhsi mentioned. And from Abu Yusuf is its non-inclusion (i.e., it is not from the face). Thus in al-Bada'i, and it appears that his madhhab is contrary. And in Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: His saying 'from the forelock hair' is on the basis of what is normal, otherwise the limit of the face in length is from the beginning of the forehead surface to the end of the two jawbones, whether there is hair or not. Because the bald and the hairless (aghamm and asla') are exceptions: the aghamm has hair on his forehead, so washing his face from his forelock hair is not sufficient; the asla' whose hair receded to the middle of his head, he is not required to wash from his forelock hair according to the most correct position as in al-Khulasa. And it is stated in al-Mujtaba that there is disagreement: it is said if the hair is little then it is of the face, if much then of the head, and the correct is that it is of the head, so much so that wiping over it is permitted.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the white area between the sideburn (العذار) and the ear. The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq indicates that this area is part of the face and must be washed. The commentator confirms that this is the relied-upon (sahih) position of the Hanafi school, supported by al-Hulwani, al-Tahawi, and al-Sarakhsi, and held by the majority of our scholars. However, Abu Yusuf held that this area is not part of the face. The commentary also addresses the edge case of baldness: the definition from the hairline is for the normal case; for a bald person whose hairline has receded, the actual limit is from the forehead surface to the bottom of the jawbone. For the aghamm (one with hair on the forehead), washing from his actual hairline would miss part of the face; for the asla' (bald), the limit is the forehead surface itself, not the receded hairline. There is also a view in al-Mujtaba that if the receded hair is little, it is part of the face; if much, part of the head. The correct position is that it is all head, so wiping over it (in wudu) is allowed.

Key Terms
العذار (al-‘idhār): The sideburn; the hair on the side of the face between the ear and the beard
الأغم (al-aghamm): A person whose forehead hair grows low and covers part of the forehead
الأصلع (al-asla‘): A bald person whose hairline has receded

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is part of the face and must be washed
B) It is part of the head and may be wiped
C) It is not part of the face according to the majority
D) Abu Yusuf included it but the school does not

Key Takeaway
The white area between sideburn and ear is part of the face and must be washed in wudu; this is the correct Hanafi position despite Abu Yusuf's contrary view.

Limits of the Face in Wudu: Hair, Eyes, and Intra-Hanafi Differences
Defines the boundaries of the face regarding hair, eyes, and intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of the face to determine which areas must be washed in wudu and identify the relied-upon position regarding disputed boundaries.

Core Idea
The face in wudu is from forehead to chin, but thick hair exempts skin underneath unless visible.

This lesson clarifies the precise boundaries of the face (wajh) for wudu according to the Hanafi school. You will learn which areas are obligatory to wash, how to handle hair on the mustache and eyebrows, the ruling on closing the eyes, and the intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the white area between the ear and beard.

Guided Path
1
Water under mustache and eyebrows
Determine whether water must reach under the hair of the mustache and eyebrows. The relied-upon view is that it is not obligatory unless the skin is visible (sparse hair).
Al-Walwaliji and others confirm the mufta bihi.
2
Classification of the lip
Identify which part of the lip is considered part of the face. The hidden part when the mouth is closed belongs to the mouth; the visible part belongs to the face.
Abu Ja'far's view is authenticated in al-Khulasa.
3
Ruling on the eyes and ramas
Understand that the eye itself is not washed, but if dried matter (ramas) is present, water must reach under it if it remains after closing the eye.
Tightly shutting the eyes is discouraged.
4
The two naz'ahs
Recognize that the receding hair areas on the sides of the forehead (naz'atan) are part of the head, not the face, and thus are not washed.
This ruling is from al-Mujtaba.
5
The white area dispute
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether the white area between the beard and ear is part of the face. The relied-upon view is that it is wajib to wash it.
Abu Yusuf's opposing view is reported but the majority uphold wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة إلى ما تحتها . كذا في فتح القدير. وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل قول من قال أنه يجب إيصال الماء إلى ما تحت شعر الشارب على ما إذا كان بحيث يبدو منابت الشعر، وقد جعله في التجنيس من الآداب، وصرح الولوالجي في باب الكراهية على أن المفتى به أنه لا يجب إيصال الماء إلى ما تحته كالحاجبين. وأما الشفة فقيل تبع للفم. وقال أبو جعفر : ما انكتم عند انضمامه فهو تبع له وما ظهر فللوجه وصححه في الخلاصة. وذكر في المجتبى : لا تغسل العين بالماء ولا بأس بغسل الوجه مغمضاً عينيه . وقال الفقيه أحمد بن ابراهيم : إن غمض عينيه شديداً لا يجوز ولو رمدت عينه فرمصت يجب إيصال الماء تحت الرمص إن بقي خارجاً بتغميض العين وإلا فلا. المغرب: الرمص ما جمد من الوسخ في الموق والموق مؤخر العين والماق مقدمها اهـ. وفي المجتبى : ولا يدخل في حد الوجه النزعتان وهو ما انحسر من الشعر من جانبي الجبهة إلى الرأس لأنه من الرأس .اهـ والنزعة بالفتح وأفاد المصنف أن البياض الذي بين العذار والأذن من الوجه فيجب غسله وهو ظاهر المذهب كما ذكره الحلواني وهو الصحيح وعليه أكثر مشايخنا كما ذكره الطحاوي وهو الصحيح من المذهب كما ذكره السرخسي وعن أبي يوسف عدمه كذا في البدائع ، وظاهره أن مذهبه بخلافه. وفي تبيين الحقائق أن قوله من قصاص الشعر» خرج مخرج الغالب وإلا فحد الوجه في الطول من مبدأ سطح الجبهة إلى منتهى اللحيين، كان عليه شعر أو لم يكن اهـ . لأنه يرد عليه الأغم والأصلع لأن الأغم الذي على جبهته شعر لا يكفي غسله من قصاص شعره، والأصلع الذي انحسر شعره إلى وسط رأسه لا يجب عليه أن يغسله من قصاص شعره على الأصح كما في الخلاصه. وصرح في المجتبى بالخلاف فيه فقيل إن قل فمن الوجه، وإن كثر فمن الرأس، والصحيح أنه من الرأس حتى جاز المسح عليه، وفي المغرب: عذار اللحية جانباها وشحمة الأذن مالان منها .
Book of Purification: ... to what is beneath them. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And on this should be carried the statement of one who said that it is obligatory to convey water to what is under the hair of the mustache – on the condition that the hair follicles appear. And he made it in al-Tajnis from the etiquettes. And al-Walwaliji explicitly stated in the chapter on dislikes that the fatwa is that it is not obligatory to convey water to what is under it, like the eyebrows. As for the lip, it is said it is part of the mouth. Abu Ja'far said: what is hidden when it is closed is part of it, and what appears is part of the face, and he authenticated it in al-Khulasa. And it is mentioned in al-Mujtaba: do not wash the eye with water, and there is no harm in washing the face with eyes closed. And al-Faqih Ahmad ibn Ibrahim said: if he closes his eyes tightly, it is not permitted. And if his eye is inflamed and rimmed, it is obligatory to convey water under the rim if it remains outside by closing the eye, otherwise not. al-Mughrib: al-ramas is what dries of dirt in the muq (corner of the eye), and al-muq is the back, and al-maq is the front. In al-Mujtaba: the two naz'ahs (receding hair areas) do not enter into the definition of the face – that is, what has receded of hair from the sides of the forehead to the head, because it is from the head. And al-naz'ah is with fath (vowel). And the author (al-Musannif) conveys that the white area between the beard and the ear is from the face, so it must be washed. This is the apparent madhhab as al-Halwani mentioned, and it is the correct view, and most of our mashayikh are upon it as al-Tahawi mentioned, and it is the correct view in the madhhab as al-Sarakhsi mentioned. And from Abu Yusuf, there is its non-requirement, thus in al-Bada'i', and its apparent meaning is that his madhhab is contrary to it. And in Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, his statement 'from the very top of the hair' came out as a predominant expression, otherwise the limit of the face in length is from the beginning of the surface of the forehead to the end of the jawbones, whether there is hair or not. Because the aghamm (one with hair on forehead) and the asla' (bald) are problematic: for the aghamm, who has hair on his forehead, washing from the very top of his hair is insufficient; for the asla', whose hair has receded to the middle of his head, it is not obligatory to wash from the very top of his hair, according to the most correct view as in al-Khulasa. And in al-Mujtaba, it is explicitly stated that there is a difference of opinion in it: it is said if it is little, it is from the face; if much, it is from the head. And the correct view is that it is from the head, so that wiping over it is permitted. In al-Mughrib: 'idhar' of the beard are its two sides, and the lobe of the ear is what droops from it.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the limits of the face (wajh) in wudu. The author clarifies that the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that water does not need to reach under the hair of the mustache or eyebrows, unless the skin underneath is visible due to sparse hair. Regarding the lips, there is a distinction: the hidden part when the mouth is closed is considered part of the mouth; the visible part is part of the face. The eyes themselves are not washed, but closing the eyes during washing is allowed, though tightly shutting them is discouraged. The term 'ramas' (dried matter) must have water reach under it if it remains after closing the eye. The two 'naz'ahs' (receding hair areas on the forehead) are part of the head, not the face. The white area between the beard and ear is part of the face and must be washed, contra Abu Yusuf who reportedly did not require it. The passage also clarifies that the face's length is from the top of the forehead to the bottom of the chin, regardless of hair presence, thus addressing cases of baldness or forehead hair.

Key Terms
إلى ما تحتها (ila ma tahtaha): Referring to the area beneath the mustache or eyebrow hair.
النزعتان (al-naz'atan): Two areas of hair receding on the sides of the forehead towards the head.
الرمص (al-ramas): Dried matter that collects in the corner of the eye.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, it is wajib.
B) No, it is not wajib.
C) It is sunnah.
D) There is no ruling on it.

Key Takeaway
The face in wudu is strictly defined; thick hair exempts underlying skin, but the white area between beard and ear is wajib to wash according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Inclusion of the Elbows in Wudu: The Meaning of 'Ila'
Defends Hanafi interpretation of 'ila' as inclusive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the elbows must be washed in wudu, and evaluate the strength of opposing arguments regarding the interpretation of 'ila'.

Core Idea
Customary understanding, not bare linguistics, determines that 'ila' includes the endpoint here.

The Quran commands Muslims to wash their hands 'ila al-marafiq' (to the elbows). Does this mean the elbows are included in the washing or excluded? The Hanafi school argues for inclusion, based on customary understanding (urf) and the practical impossibility of excluding the joint. In this passage, the author Ibn Nujaym presents a robust defense of the Hanafi position, refuting several objections. Let's walk through the key arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Determine what exactly is contested: is the elbow part of the hand (yad) that must be washed? The Hanafi school says yes; some Shafi'is say no.
The practical consequence is whether a wudu that omits washing the elbows is valid.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof from the verse
Examine how the author interprets 'ila' as implying accompaniment (ma'a) based on the ba' in the phrase 'bi-marafiqihim'. Understand why he rejects the plain meaning of 'ila' as exclusive.
The ba' is said to indicate 'accompaniment' (musahabah), so the verse reads 'wash your hands with your elbows', i.e., include them.
3
Examine the refutation of opposing arguments
The author counters several points: that 'ila' normally excludes, that the verse is ambiguous, that precaution dictates inclusion, and the 'ghayah (limit) as exclusion' argument. Follow each counter and understand the Hanafi reply.
Pay attention to the use of 'mafhum al-laqab' (concept of proper noun) – Hanafis do not accept it as a proof.
4
Understand the final position on precaution
The author notes that precaution (ihtiyat) here means certainty of fulfilling the obligation, but this does not require a strained interpretation. Instead, customary understanding suffices.
The Hanafi view does not rely on ihtiyat; it relies on clear customary and linguistic evidence.
5
Apply the ruling to practice
State the mufta bihi: washing the hands from fingertips to and including the elbows is fard. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) with the opposing view.
A Hanafi who omits the elbows must repeat wudu; a Shafi'i who omits them considers wudu valid but incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويديه بمرفقيه) أي مع مرفقيه فالباء للمصاحبة بمعنى «مع» نحو اهبط بسلام» أي معه. والفرق بين استعمالها بمعنى (مع) وبين مع» أن «مع» لابتداء المصاحبة والباء لاستدامتها . كذا ذكره ابن الملك في بحث القياس والمرفق بكسر الميم وفتح الفاء وفيه العكس اسم لملتقى العظمين، عظم العضد وعظم الذراع. وأشار المصنف إلى أن «إلى» في الآية بمعنى «مع» وهو مردود لأنهم قالوا : إن اليد من رؤوس الأصابع للمنكب، فإذا كانت «إلى» بمعنى التأويل قوله : (وظاهره أن مذهبه بخلافه قال الرملي : وذلك لأن لفظة عن دالة على أنه رواية عنه لا أنه قوله وإلا لقال بدل عن وعند قول المصنف : (ويديه بمرفقيه قيل : كان الأولى أن يقول ومرفقيه بيديه لما تقرر في النحو أن مدخول مع هو المتبوع تقول جاء زيد . السلطان لا عكسه لكن نقل في الأطول أن دخول مع شاع على المتبوع، فما هنا إما أن يخرج على غير الشائع أو ينزل منزلة المتبوع لكمال العام إذ هو تنصيص على بعض متعلق الحكم بتعليق عين ذلك الحكم وذلك لا يخرج غيره ولو أخرج كان بمفهوم اللقب وهو ليس بحجة، وما في المحيط من أنه لما كان المرفق ملتقى العظمين ولا يمكن التمييز بينهما فلما وجب غسل الذراع ولا يمكن تحديده وجب غسل المرفق احتياطاً مردود، لأنه لم يتعلق الأمر بغسل الذراع ليجب غسل ما لازمه وإنما تعلق الأمر بغسل اليد إلى المرفق وما بعد «إلى» لما لم يدخل لم يدخل جزاهما الملتقيان وما في البدائع من أنه لما احتمل الدخول واحتمل الخروج صار مجملاً وفعله عليه السلام بيان للمجمل، مردود بأن اللفظ لا يوجب الإجمال والأصل براءة الذمة، وإنما يوجب الدلالة المشتبهة فبقي مجرد فعله دليل السنة. وما في غاية البيان من أنها قد تدخل وقد لا تدخل فتدخل احتياطاً مردود، لأن الحكم إذا توقف على الدليل لا يجب مع عدمه والاحتياط العمل بأقوى الدليلين وهو فرع تجاذ بهما وهو منتف. وما في الهداية وغيرها من أنه غاية المقدر وتقديره اغسلوا أيديكم مسقطين إلى المرافق مردود، لأن الظاهر تعلقه باغسلوا وتعلقه بمقدر خلاف الظاهر بلا ملجيء مع أن المقصود منه الإسقاط لا وهو يوجبه عما فوق المرفق بل عما قبله باللفظ، إذ يحتمل أسقطوا من المنكب إلى المرفق أو من رؤوس الأصابع إلى المرفق فلم يتعين الأول كما لا يخفى. وفرقهم بين غاية الإسقاط وبين غاية المدّ بأن صدر الكلام إن كان متناولاً لما بعد «إلى» فهي للإسقاط كمسئلتنا، وإلا فهي للمد نحو أتموا الصيام إلى الليل ) [البقرة : ۱۸۷] ليس بمطرد لانتقاضه أقول : إن كان المراد أن ذلك من عبارة المتن فهذه الإشارة في حيز المنع إذ كون الباء بمعنى «مع» في كلام المصنف لا يفهم منه أن «إلى» في الآية بمعناها حتى يرد الردّ المذكور لاحتمال كونها باقية على معناها، وأن ما فوق المرافق خارج بالإجماع على أنه لو قيل اغسل جسدك إلى الترقوة مثلاً لا يتوهم منه غسل الجميع، بل الذي يتبادر إلى الفهم بحسب العرف أن المغسول ما تحتها لتعذر غسل ما فوقها دونها ودون ما تحتها إذ يحتاج إلى غاية التكلف، فهو بدون الإجماع يفهم منه غسل الأيدي من رؤوس الأصابع إلى المرافق لا . من المنكب، وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى تأويل «إلى» بمعنى «مع». نعم يبقى الكلام في الغاية وذاك شيء آخر فتأمله فإني لم أر أحداً ذكره قوله : ( ولو أخرج كان بمفهوم اللقب وهو ليس بحجة) أي عندنا كغيره من المفاهيم على ما بين في محله خلافاً لبعض الشافعية. وأقول: كيف يمكن إخراج غيره مع تنصيص الحكم على الكل حتى يقال إنه بمفهوم اللقب، ويدفع بأنه ليس بحجة فإن قولك اضرب القوم مع زيد لا يفهم منه أن غير زيد ليس مأموراً بضربه حتى عند من يقول بحجية مفهوم اللقب، نعم لو قيل اضرب زيداً واقتصر المتكلم على ذلك جرى فيه الخلاف لأنه تعليق الحكم بجامد كفى الغنم زكاة كما في التحرير فافهم قوله : (وما في غاية البيان إلى آخر هذا البحث قال في النهر بعد نقله لذلك أقول : معنى الاحتياط هنا هو الخروج عن العهدة بيقين،
His statement: (and your hands with your elbows) meaning along with your elbows. So the ba' is for accompaniment meaning 'with', like 'descend with peace' meaning along with it. The difference between its usage in the sense of 'with' and 'ma'a' is that 'ma'a' indicates the beginning of accompaniment, while the ba' indicates its continuity. Thus Ibn al-Malik mentioned in the discussion on qiyas. And al-mirfaq (elbow), with kasra on the mim and fatha on the fa', and the opposite is also found, is the name for the meeting point of two bones: the bone of the upper arm and the bone of the forearm. The author indicated that 'ila' in the verse is in the sense of 'ma'a', and this is rejected because they said: the hand (yad) is from the fingertips to the shoulder. So if 'ila' means ... (the text continues with refutations). Then: I say: If what is meant is that this is from the wording of the matn, then this indication is in the realm of prohibition, since the fact that the ba' is in the sense of 'ma'a' in the author's speech does not imply that 'ila' in the verse is in its meaning, otherwise the mentioned refutation would occur, because it is possible that it remains on its meaning, and that what is above the elbows is excluded by consensus. For example, if one said 'wash your body to the collarbone', one would not understand washing all of it. Rather, what occurs to the understanding according to custom is that what is washed is that which is below it, due to the difficulty of washing what is above it without it and what is below it, as that requires excessive effort. So even without consensus, one understands from it the washing of the hands from the fingertips to the elbows, not from the shoulder. Thus there is no need to interpret 'ila' in the sense of 'ma'a'. Yes, the discussion remains concerning the limit (ghayah), and that is another matter; so ponder it, for I have not seen anyone mention it. His statement: (And if he excluded, it would be by the concept of a proper noun, which is not a proof) meaning in our school, like other concepts, as explained in its place, contrary to some Shafi'is. And I say: How can one exclude others when the ruling is textually applied to the whole, so that it is said to be by the concept of a proper noun? And it is refuted by the fact that it is not a proof. For example, if you say 'strike the people with Zayd', it is not understood that other than Zayd is not commanded to be struck, even for those who assert the validity of the concept of a proper noun. However, if one says 'strike Zayd' and the speaker restricts it, then there is disagreement, because it is attaching a ruling to a fixed noun (jamid), like 'sheep alone are zakatable', as in al-Tahrir. So understand. His statement: (And what is in Ghayat al-Bayan up to the end of this discussion) – in al-Nahr after quoting it, he said: I say: The meaning of precaution (ihtiyat) here is to exit the responsibility with certainty.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute, focusing on the interpretation of 'ila' in the Quranic verse for wudu (5:6). The Hanafi position is that the hands are washed from the fingertips to and including the elbows. The author (Ibn Nujaym) defends this by explaining that the phrase 'with your hands to the elbows' implies accompaniment, meaning the elbows are included. He refutes several arguments against this: first, that 'ila' indicates exclusion; second, that the verse is ambiguous and the Prophet's practice clarifies inclusion; third, that precaution dictates inclusion. He argues that the customary understanding of 'ila' is exclusion in general, but in this context, because the hand (yad) extends to the elbow, the elbow is included. He also addresses the linguistic point about 'ba' for accompaniment, and the concept of 'mafhum al-laqab' (concept of a proper noun), stating that it is not a valid proof in the Hanafi school. The passage ends with a note that precaution in worship means certainty of fulfillment, but the Hanafi view does not require the strained interpretation of 'ila' as 'ma'a'.

Key Terms
إلى (ila): Preposition meaning 'to' or 'up to'; here disputed whether it includes the endpoint.
مرفق (mirfaq): Elbow, the joint between upper arm and forearm.
مفهوم اللقب (mafhum al-laqab): Concept of a proper noun: the implication that the ruling is limited to that noun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, are the elbows included in the wajib washing of the hands in wudu?
A) Yes, they are included.
B) No, they are excluded.
C) It is recommended but not required.
D) Only the right elbow is included.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that the elbows must be washed in wudu because 'ila' in the verse, interpreted by custom and the accompanying ba', includes the endpoint.

The Obligation of Washing the Elbows and Ankles in Wudu
Debate on the obligation of washing elbows/ankles, culminating in consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on washing the elbows in wudu and explain why consensus (ijma) is the primary proof, refuting arguments based on the word 'ila' or precaution.

Core Idea
The elbows must be washed in wudu, proven by consensus, not by the ambiguous word 'ila'.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq tackles a classic debate: does the Quranic command 'wash your hands to the elbows' (5:6) include the elbows or stop before them? The commentator systematically refutes several interpretations that exclude the elbows, including the concept of mafhum al-laqab and arguments from precaution. He ultimately grounds the obligation on the consensus (ijma) of scholars, citing Imam al-Shafi'i's statement that there is no known disagreement. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to evaluate linguistic and rational arguments in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the dispute centers on the preposition 'ila' in the wudu verse: does it include the endpoint (elbows) or not? The Hanafi school, along with the majority, says yes.
The verse: '...wash your faces and your hands to the elbows...' (5:6).
2
Analyze the refuted arguments
The author addresses several weak arguments: (1) mafhum al-laqab – that naming a specific part excludes others; (2) that 'ila' necessarily excludes; (3) that precaution (ihtiyat) requires washing; (4) that the Prophet's practice alone suffices. Understand why each is rejected.
For example, mafhum al-laqab is not a valid proof in the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the consensus proof
The author quotes Imam al-Shafi'i's claim of no disagreement on the obligation of including the elbows. This, when known to later scholars, constitutes a binding consensus (ijma). Note how the Hanafi school treats such statements as evidence.
The passage: 'قال الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه في الأم : لا نعلم مخالفاً في ايجاب دخول المرفقين في الوضوء.'
4
Apply the ruling to the ankles
The passage concludes that the ankles share the same ruling as the elbows, by analogy or by the same reasoning. Identify that both are mentioned with 'ila' in the verse.
The author: 'وحكم الكعبين كالمرفقين.'
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that washing the elbows and ankles is wajib. This is based on Zahir al-Riwayah and the consensus. Zufar's contrary view is rejected.
The tarjih authority: the author sides with the majority and the consensus.

بجامد كفى الغنم زكاة كما في التحرير فافهم قوله : (وما في غاية البيان إلى آخر هذا البحث قال في النهر بعد نقله لذلك أقول : معنى الاحتياط هنا هو الخروج عن العهدة بيقين، بالغاية في اليمين، فإن ظاهر الرواية عدم الدخول كما إذا حلف لا يكلمه إلى عشرة أيام لا يدخل العاشر مع تناول الصدر له كما في جامع الفصولين، وكذلك رأس السمكة في قوله والله لا آكل السمكة إلى رأسها فإنها لا تدخل مع التناول المذكور. وما ذكره المحققون - ومنهم الزمخشري والتفتازاني - من أن إلى تفيد معنى الغاية مطلقاً فأما دخولها في الحكم وخروجها عنه فأمر يدور مع الدليل، فما فيه دليل، فما فيه دليل الخروج قوله تعالى فنظرة إلى ميسرة )
