فلا يفيد الافتراض لأن الفعل لا يفيده وتقدم منع الاحتياط . والحق أن شيئاً مما ذكروه لا يدل على الافتراض فالأولى الاستدلال بالإجماع على فرضيتهما. قال الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه في الأم : لا نعلم مخالفاً في ايجاب دخول المرفقين في الوضوء. وهذا منه حكاية للإجماع . قال في فتح الباري بعد نقله عنه : فعلى هذا فزفر محجوج بالإجماع قبله، وكذا من قال ذلك من أهل الظاهر بعده ولم يثبت ذلك عن مالك صريحاً وإنما حكى عنه أشهب كلاماً محتملاً. وحكم الكعبين كالمرفقين.
Book of Purification. ... The ruling of the ankles is like the elbows.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a detailed debate on the obligation of washing the elbows and ankles in wudu. The commentator refutes various opinions that attempt to exclude the elbows from the wash (e.g., Zufar's view that 'إلى' excludes the endpoint). He argues that the word 'إلى' does not necessarily exclude, and the consensus of scholars (including Imam al-Shafi'i's statement of no known disagreement) establishes the obligation. He also discusses the concept of 'مفهوم اللقب' (the implication of a name) showing it is not a valid proof in the Hanafi school. The passage concludes that the ankles follow the same ruling as the elbows.

Key Terms
مفهوم اللقب (mafhūm al-laqab): The concept of a specific name; the idea that singling out someone or something by name implies exclusion of others. Here it is argued that mentioning a specific part does not negate others.
إلى (ilā): A preposition meaning 'to, until, up to'. The debate is whether it includes the endpoint or not in the context of wudu.
الاحتياط (al-ihtiyāṭ): Precaution; performing an action to be certain of fulfilling an obligation. The author warns that precaution is not a basis for obligation without proof.
الإجماع (al-ijmā‘): Consensus of the mujtahidūn on a ruling. Here it is used to prove the obligation of washing the elbows.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the obligation to wash the elbows and ankles in wudu is established by consensus, not by a strained interpretation of the word 'ila'.

Practical Rulings on Washing Hands, Feet, Nails and Rings
Listing rulings on obstacles in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for common obstacles during wudu and apply them to real-life scenarios involving dirt under nails, henna, tight rings, amputated limbs, long nails, and extra fingers.

Core Idea
Habitual barriers are excused, but intentional barriers that prevent water from reaching the skin invalidate wudu.

This lesson covers specific situations where water might not reach the skin during wudu: dirt under nails, henna, dough, dry mud, tight rings, amputated limbs, long nails, and extra hands. The Hanafi school distinguishes between habitual and intentional barriers. Understanding these rulings ensures the validity of your wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obstacle
Determine what is preventing water from reaching the skin: e.g., dirt under nails, a ring, dry mud, an amputated limb, long nails, an extra hand.
2
Classify as habitual or intentional
Is this obstacle part of one's normal daily work (habitual) or is it something unusual that can be removed? For example, a farmer's soil under nails is habitual; dry mud left intentionally is not.
3
Check water access
Does the obstacle completely prevent water from reaching the skin? The size of the dry area matters: a needle-head size of dry mud invalidates.
4
Analyze extra limbs
If there is an extra hand or finger, only the part that lies within the obligatory area (elbow to fingertips for hands, ankle to toes for feet) must be washed.
5
State the ruling
Based on the above, determine whether the wudu is valid or invalid. Habitual barriers are excused; intentional barriers that leave even a small dry spot invalidate the wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان في أظفاره درن أو طين أو عجين أو المرأة تضع الحناء جاز في القروي والمدني وهو صحيح وعليه الفتوى. ولو لصق بأصل ظفره طين يابس وبقي قدر رأس إبرة من موضع الغسل لم يجز، وإذا كان في أصبعه خاتم إن كان ضيقاً فالمختار أنه يجب نزعه أو تحريكه بحيث يصل الماء إلى ما تحته. ولو قطعت يده أو رجله فلم يبق من المرفق والكعب شيء سقط الغسل ولو بقي وجب، ولو طالت أظفاره حتى خرجت عن رؤوس الأصابع وجب غسلها بلا خلاف ولو خلق له يدان على المنكب فالتامة الأصلية يجب غسلها والأخرى ،زائدة، فما حاذى منها محل الفرض وجب غسله، وما لا هي وما نسبه إلى الهداية سهو وإنما الذي فيها ردّ لقول زفر الغاية لا تدخل في المغيا أن هذه الغاية لإسقاط ما وراءها يعني فهي داخلة والجار متعلق باغسلوا على كل حال والنقض بمسألة اليمين أجاب عنه في فتح القدير بأن الكلام هنا في اللغة والإيمان مبنية على العرف. نعم يرد النقض بمثل قرأت القرآن إلى سورة كذا والهداية إلى كتاب كذا، فإن الغاية فيهما لا تدخل تحت المغيا مع تناول الصدر لها. وقوله والأولى الخ مما لا حاجة إليه إذ الفروض العملية لا تحتاج في إثباتها إلى القاطع فيحتاج إلى الإجماع على أن قول المجتهد لا أعلم مخالفاً لا يكون حكاية للإجماع الذي يكون غيره محجوباً به، فقد قال الإمام اللامشي في أصوله : لا خلاف أن جميع المجتهدين لو أجمعوا على حكم واحد ووجد الرضا من الكل نصاً كان ذلك إجماعاً، فأما إذا نص البعض وسكت الباقون لا عن خوف بعد اشتهار القول فعامة أهل السنة أن ذلك يكون إجماعاً. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى : لا أقول إنه إجماع ولكن أقول لا أعلم فيه خلافاً. وقال أبو هاشم من المعتزلة لا يكون إجماعاً اهـ.
And if there is dirt or mud or dough under his nails, or if a woman puts henna, it is permissible for villager and city-dweller, and it is sound and upon it is fatwa. And if dry mud sticks to the base of his nail and remains the size of a needle head from the place of washing, it is not permissible. If he has a ring on his finger, if it is tight, the chosen view is that he must remove it or shift it so that water reaches what is under it. If his hand or foot is severed so that nothing remains of the elbow or ankle, the washing is dropped; if something remains, it is obligatory. If his nails grow long and extend beyond the fingertips, their washing is obligatory without dispute. If he is created with two hands on the shoulder, the complete original one must be washed, and the other is extra. What of it aligns with the obligatory area must be washed; what does not... And what he attributed to al-Hidayah is a slip; rather what is in it is a refutation of Zufar's saying that the endpoint does not enter into the ranged, that this endpoint is to drop what is beyond it, meaning it is included, and the preposition is attached to 'wash' in any case. The objection with the oath issue was answered in Fath al-Qadir that speech here is language and oaths are built on custom. Yes, the objection arises with examples like 'I read the Quran until such surah' and 'al-Hidayah until such book', for the endpoint therein does not enter under the ranged while the initial part includes it. And his saying 'the first' etc. is unnecessary, for practical obligations do not require definitive proof, rather they need consensus; but a mujtahid's saying 'I know no difference' is not a narration of consensus that would bind others. Imam al-Lamishi said in his Usul: 'There is no dispute that if all mujtahidun agree on one ruling and the approval of all is found in text, that is consensus. If some state and the rest are silent not out of fear after the statement becomes widespread, then according to the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah that is consensus.' Al-Shafi'i said: 'I do not call it consensus, but I say I know no difference.' Abu Hashim of the Mu'tazila said it is not consensus.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents practical rulings related to washing the hands and feet in wudu. The author details: (1) Dirt, dough, or henna under the nails is excused if it is customary (as for villagers or city-dwellers), but dry mud at the base of the nail that prevents water reaching a small spot invalidates the wash. (2) A tight ring must be removed or shifted. (3) If the limb is amputated above the required area (elbow/ankle), no wash is needed on that limb; if some of the required area remains, it must be washed. (4) Long nails extending beyond the fingertips are to be washed. (5) An extra hand born on the shoulder: the original hand is washed fully; the extra one is washed only over the part that aligns with the obligatory area (elbow to fingertips). The passage then returns to the earlier theoretical debate about the meaning of 'إلى' and the nature of consensus, clarifying that a scholar's statement 'I know no difference' does not necessarily transmit binding consensus unless other conditions are met, referencing Imam al-Lamishi and Imam al-Shafi'i.

Key Terms
درن (daran): Dirt or filth that accumulates under the nails. The ruling depends on whether it is habitual or not.
خاتم (khātam): Ring. A tight ring must be removed or moved to allow water to reach the skin beneath it.
زائدة (zā'ida): An extra limb, such as an extra hand or finger. Its washing is required only where it aligns with the obligatory area of the original limb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is not necessary to move it because water can seep under it.
B) He must remove it or move it so that water reaches under it.
C) He can ignore it and the wudu is valid.
D) He must cut the ring off.

Key Takeaway
Habitual barriers are excused, but intentional obstacles that prevent water from reaching the skin invalidate wudu.

The Interpretation of the Endpoint (Ghayah) in the Wudu Verse and the Nature of Consensus
Theoretical discourse on linguistic interpretation and consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the preposition 'إلى' in the wudu verse includes the elbows and ankles, and distinguish between different views on what constitutes binding consensus.

Core Idea
The preposition 'إلى' in 'wash your hands to the elbows' includes the elbows, and mere silence of scholars does not constitute binding consensus.

This lesson addresses a finer point of usul al-fiqh: the interpretation of the Arabic preposition 'إلى' in the wudu verse 'wash your hands to the elbows'. The Hanafi school, contra Zufar, holds that 'إلى' includes the endpoint. The passage also clarifies that a scholar's statement 'I know no difference' is not binding consensus; consensus requires explicit agreement or silent approval after the ruling becomes widespread.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute
Zufar claimed that 'إلى' excludes the endpoint, meaning the elbows are not washed. The Hanafis argue it includes the endpoint. Identify the grammatical basis.
2
Analyze the Hanafi argument
The Hanafis state that 'إلى' is a preposition of endpoint, and the phrase 'wash... to the elbows' implies washing includes the elbows. They also use custom: in Arabic, 'I read until the end' includes the end.
3
Explore the consensus discussion
The passage then discusses that a mujtahid's statement 'I know no difference' does not transmit consensus. Real consensus requires either explicit agreement from all or silence after the statement becomes widespread (not out of fear).
4
Identify the positions
The Hanafis (Imam al-Lamishi) say that silence after widespread knowledge counts as consensus. Al-Shafi'i says it is not consensus but 'I know no difference'. Abu Hashim of the Mu'tazila says it is not consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان في أظفاره درن أو طين أو عجين أو المرأة تضع الحناء جاز في القروي والمدني وهو صحيح وعليه الفتوى. ولو لصق بأصل ظفره طين يابس وبقي قدر رأس إبرة من موضع الغسل لم يجز، وإذا كان في أصبعه خاتم إن كان ضيقاً فالمختار أنه يجب نزعه أو تحريكه بحيث يصل الماء إلى ما تحته. ولو قطعت يده أو رجله فلم يبق من المرفق والكعب شيء سقط الغسل ولو بقي وجب، ولو طالت أظفاره حتى خرجت عن رؤوس الأصابع وجب غسلها بلا خلاف ولو خلق له يدان على المنكب فالتامة الأصلية يجب غسلها والأخرى ،زائدة، فما حاذى منها محل الفرض وجب غسله، وما لا هي وما نسبه إلى الهداية سهو وإنما الذي فيها ردّ لقول زفر الغاية لا تدخل في المغيا أن هذه الغاية لإسقاط ما وراءها يعني فهي داخلة والجار متعلق باغسلوا على كل حال والنقض بمسألة اليمين أجاب عنه في فتح القدير بأن الكلام هنا في اللغة والإيمان مبنية على العرف. نعم يرد النقض بمثل قرأت القرآن إلى سورة كذا والهداية إلى كتاب كذا، فإن الغاية فيهما لا تدخل تحت المغيا مع تناول الصدر لها. وقوله والأولى الخ مما لا حاجة إليه إذ الفروض العملية لا تحتاج في إثباتها إلى القاطع فيحتاج إلى الإجماع على أن قول المجتهد لا أعلم مخالفاً لا يكون حكاية للإجماع الذي يكون غيره محجوباً به، فقد قال الإمام اللامشي في أصوله : لا خلاف أن جميع المجتهدين لو أجمعوا على حكم واحد ووجد الرضا من الكل نصاً كان ذلك إجماعاً، فأما إذا نص البعض وسكت الباقون لا عن خوف بعد اشتهار القول فعامة أهل السنة أن ذلك يكون إجماعاً. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى : لا أقول إنه إجماع ولكن أقول لا أعلم فيه خلافاً. وقال أبو هاشم من المعتزلة لا يكون إجماعاً اهـ.
And if there is dirt or mud or dough under his nails, or if a woman puts henna, it is permissible for villager and city-dweller, and it is sound and upon it is fatwa. And if dry mud sticks to the base of his nail and remains the size of a needle head from the place of washing, it is not permissible. If he has a ring on his finger, if it is tight, the chosen view is that he must remove it or shift it so that water reaches what is under it. If his hand or foot is severed so that nothing remains of the elbow or ankle, the washing is dropped; if something remains, it is obligatory. If his nails grow long and extend beyond the fingertips, their washing is obligatory without dispute. If he is created with two hands on the shoulder, the complete original one must be washed, and the other is extra. What of it aligns with the obligatory area must be washed; what does not... And what he attributed to al-Hidayah is a slip; rather what is in it is a refutation of Zufar's saying that the endpoint does not enter into the ranged, that this endpoint is to drop what is beyond it, meaning it is included, and the preposition is attached to 'wash' in any case. The objection with the oath issue was answered in Fath al-Qadir that speech here is language and oaths are built on custom. Yes, the objection arises with examples like 'I read the Quran until such surah' and 'al-Hidayah until such book', for the endpoint therein does not enter under the ranged while the initial part includes it. And his saying 'the first' etc. is unnecessary, for practical obligations do not require definitive proof, rather they need consensus; but a mujtahid's saying 'I know no difference' is not a narration of consensus that would bind others. Imam al-Lamishi said in his Usul: 'There is no dispute that if all mujtahidun agree on one ruling and the approval of all is found in text, that is consensus. If some state and the rest are silent not out of fear after the statement becomes widespread, then according to the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah that is consensus.' Al-Shafi'i said: 'I do not call it consensus, but I say I know no difference.' Abu Hashim of the Mu'tazila said it is not consensus.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents practical rulings related to washing the hands and feet in wudu. The author details: (1) Dirt, dough, or henna under the nails is excused if it is customary (as for villagers or city-dwellers), but dry mud at the base of the nail that prevents water reaching a small spot invalidates the wash. (2) A tight ring must be removed or shifted. (3) If the limb is amputated above the required area (elbow/ankle), no wash is needed on that limb; if some of the required area remains, it must be washed. (4) Long nails extending beyond the fingertips are to be washed. (5) An extra hand born on the shoulder: the original hand is washed fully; the extra one is washed only over the part that aligns with the obligatory area (elbow to fingertips). The passage then returns to the earlier theoretical debate about the meaning of 'إلى' and the nature of consensus, clarifying that a scholar's statement 'I know no difference' does not necessarily transmit binding consensus unless other conditions are met, referencing Imam al-Lamishi and Imam al-Shafi'i.

Key Terms
غاية (ghāyah): The endpoint or limit. In the wudu verse, it refers to the elbows and ankles as the limit of washing.
مغيا (mughtā): The thing ranged or limited by the ghayah. The part of the arm up to the elbow.
إجماع (ijmā'): Consensus of the mujtahidun on a ruling. The text discusses its conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling regarding washing the elbows in wudu?
A) They are not washed because 'إلى' excludes the endpoint.
B) They must be washed because 'إلى' includes the endpoint.
C) Washing them is recommended but not obligatory.
D) It is optional depending on the school one follows.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the elbows and ankles are included in the washing obligation, and consensus requires explicit agreement or widespread silent approval.

Inclusion of Elbows and Ankles in Wudu
Commentary proving obligatory inclusion of elbows and ankles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers washing the elbows and ankles obligatory in wudu, and identify the consensus as the primary proof.

Core Idea
Consensus, not linguistic assumption, proves the obligation to wash elbows and ankles.

The verse of wudu (5:6) commands washing the hands 'to the elbows' and feet 'to the ankles'. Does the preposition 'ilā' include or exclude the endpoint? This lesson explores the Hanafi resolution: since the linguistic meaning is ambiguous, the school relies on the consensus (ijmā‘) of scholars, as reported by Imam al-Shāfi‘ī, to establish the obligation. You will see how consensus overrides speculative linguistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed point
The act is wudu; the dispute is whether washing the elbows and ankles is obligatory. The verse literally says 'your hands to the elbows' and 'your feet to the ankles'.
The preposition 'ilā' is the focus of the linguistic debate.
2
Understand the linguistic issue
The preposition 'ilā' can either include the endpoint (the elbows) or exclude it. The author gives examples from oaths and Quran to show the ambiguity: in some contexts it includes, in others it excludes.
Examples: 'deferment until a time of ease' (excludes), 'night journey to the mosque' (includes).
3
Locate the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is consensus (ijmā‘), not mere linguistic interpretation. Imam al-Shāfi‘ī's statement in al-Umm is cited: 'We do not know any opposition to the obligation of including the elbows.' This indicates absence of dissent and establishes consensus.
Consensus is a binding proof in Hanafi usul.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The absence of known opposition is taken as a sign of consensus (ijmā‘ sukūtī). This consensus resolves the ambiguity and establishes the ruling definitively, overriding speculative linguistic arguments.
This is stronger than individual scholars' opinions.
5
Note the ikhtilāf and the tarjīḥ
Zufar (early Hanafi) and some Ẓāhirīs disagreed, arguing that 'ilā' excludes the endpoint. The Hanafi response is that the consensus refutes them, and the relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is inclusion of the elbows and ankles.
The author of al-Baḥr al-Rā’iq rejects precaution-based reasoning in favor of consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالغاية في اليمين، فإن ظاهر الرواية عدم الدخول كما إذا حلف لا يكلمه إلى عشرة أيام لا يدخل العاشر مع تناول الصدر له كما في جامع الفصولين، وكذلك رأس السمكة في قوله والله لا آكل السمكة إلى رأسها فإنها لا تدخل مع التناول المذكور. وما ذكره المحققون - ومنهم الزمخشري والتفتازاني - من أن إلى تفيد معنى الغاية مطلقاً فأما دخولها في الحكم وخروجها عنه فأمر يدور مع الدليل، فما فيه دليل، فما فيه دليل الخروج قوله تعالى فنظرة إلى ميسرة ) [البقرة : ٢٨٠] ومما فيه دليل الدخول آية الإسراء للعلم بأنه لا يسرى به إلى المسجد الأقصى من غير أن يدخله، وما نحن فيه لا دليل فيه على أحد الأمرين فقالوا بدخولهما احتياطاً إذ لم يرو عنه قط الترك غسلهما فلا يفيد الافتراض لأن الفعل لا يفيده وتقدم منع الاحتياط . والحق أن شيئاً مما ذكروه لا يدل على الافتراض فالأولى الاستدلال بالإجماع على فرضيتهما. قال الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه في الأم : لا نعلم مخالفاً في ايجاب دخول المرفقين في الوضوء. وهذا منه حكاية للإجماع . قال في فتح الباري بعد نقله عنه : فعلى هذا فزفر محجوج بالإجماع قبله، وكذا من قال ذلك من أهل الظاهر بعده ولم يثبت ذلك عن مالك صريحاً وإنما حكى عنه أشهب كلاماً محتملاً. وحكم الكعبين كالمرفقين.
With the end point in the oath, the apparent transmission is non-inclusion, just as if he swore not to speak to him for ten days, the tenth day is not included even though the beginning includes it, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. Similarly, the head of the fish in his saying 'By Allah, I will not eat the fish up to its head' is not included with the mentioned consumption. And what the verifiers - among them al-Zamakhshari and al-Taftazani - mentioned that 'إلى' conveys the meaning of end point absolutely. As for its inclusion in the ruling or its exclusion, it is a matter that revolves with the evidence. So what has evidence of exclusion is His saying 'then a deferment until a time of ease' [2:280]. And what has evidence of inclusion is the verse of al-Isra' because it is known that he was not taken to the furthest mosque without entering it. And our case has no evidence for either of the two matters, so they said they are included out of precaution since it has never been reported that he left them unwashed. But that does not indicate obligation because an action does not indicate it, and the objection to precaution has preceded. And the truth is that none of what they mentioned indicates obligation, so it is better to use consensus for their obligation. Imam al-Shafi'i said in al-Umm: 'We do not know any opposition to the obligation of including the elbows in wudu.' This is from him a report of consensus. He said in Fath al-Bari after quoting him: 'Thus, Zufar is refuted by the consensus before him, and similarly those of the Zahiri school after him. And it is not explicitly established from Malik, rather Ashhab narrated from him a possible statement.' And the ruling of the ankles is like the elbows.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the obligation of washing the elbows and ankles in wudu. The author begins by discussing the linguistic meaning of the preposition 'إلى' (to/until) and whether it includes the endpoint. He cites the views of al-Zamakhshari and al-Taftazani that 'إلى' indicates an endpoint absolutely, but whether the endpoint is included in the ruling depends on evidence. He gives examples from the Quran where the endpoint is excluded (2:280) and included (17:1). Since there is no explicit evidence in the verse of wudu to decide, some scholars argued for inclusion out of precaution. However, the author rejects this reasoning and instead relies on the consensus (ijma') that the elbows are obligatory to wash, quoting Imam al-Shafi'i's statement in al-Umm that he knows no dissenter. He further notes that Zufar, who argued that the endpoint is not included in the ruling, is refuted by this consensus. The passage concludes that the ruling of the ankles follows that of the elbows.

Key Terms
إلى (ilā): Preposition indicating the endpoint; its inclusion or exclusion of the limit depends on evidence.
المرفقين (al-mirfaqayn): The two elbows; the joint where the upper arm and forearm meet.
الكعبين (al-ka'bayn): The two ankle bones that protrude on either side of the foot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is washing the elbows obligatory in wudu?
A) Because the verse explicitly mentions them
B) Because of consensus (ijma') that they must be washed
C) Because the Prophet always washed them
D) Because it is logically necessary

Key Takeaway
The obligation to wash the elbows and ankles in wudu is established by consensus, not by the ambiguous linguistic meaning of 'ilā'.

Rulings on Dirt Under Nails and Tight Rings in Wudu
Ruling statements on obstacles from dirt and rings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dirt under nails and the obligation to remove a tight ring, and identify the conditions that affect their validity in wudu.

Core Idea
Natural dirt under nails is permissible; a tight ring must be removed or moved for valid wudu.

In wudu, the hands must be washed completely. But what about dirt under nails or a tight ring? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position: common dirt like mud, dough, or henna is permissible, but if dry mud sticks at the root and leaves a needlehead spot unwashed, wudu is invalid. Similarly, a tight ring must be removed or moved to allow water to reach the skin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Locate the sentences about dirt under nails and tight rings in the passage. Note the key Arabic terms: درن, خاتم ضيق.
2
Analyze the dirt ruling
The text says dirt is permissible ('جاز') in rural and urban areas, and this is the fatwa. But if dry mud sticks at the root and a needlehead remains unwashed, it is not permissible. Understand the condition: the dirt must not prevent water from reaching the skin entirely.
3
Analyze the tight ring ruling
The text states that for a tight ring, the chosen (مختار) position is that it must be removed or moved so water reaches under it. 'يجب نزعه أو تحريكه' – obligation (وجوب).
This is an obligation, not a recommendation.
4
Connect to the principle
Both rulings stem from the requirement that water must reach the skin. Any obstacle that prevents water from reaching a fard (obligatory) washing area must be removed.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider: A farmer has dried mud under his nails after work. According to the ruling, if it is normal dirt, it is permissible. But if it's dry and stuck, leaving an unwashed spot, wudu is invalid. For a ring, if it is tight, he must remove or move it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان في أظفاره درن أو طين أو عجين أو المرأة تضع الحناء جاز في القروي والمدني وهو صحيح وعليه الفتوى. ولو لصق بأصل ظفره طين يابس وبقي قدر رأس إبرة من موضع الغسل لم يجز، وإذا كان في أصبعه خاتم إن كان ضيقاً فالمختار أنه يجب نزعه أو تحريكه بحيث يصل الماء إلى ما تحته. ولو قطعت يده أو رجله فلم يبق من المرفق والكعب شيء سقط الغسل ولو بقي وجب، ولو طالت أظفاره حتى خرجت عن رؤوس الأصابع وجب غسلها بلا خلاف ولو خلق له يدان على المنكب فالتامة الأصلية يجب غسلها والأخرى زائدة فما حاذى منها محل الفرض وجب غسله، وما لا فلا يجب بل يندب غسله، وكذا يجب غسل ما كان مركباً على اليد من الأصبع الزائدة والكف الزائدة والسلعة، وكذا يجب إيصال الماء إلى ما بين الأصابع إذا لم تكن ملتحمة .
And if there is dirt or mud or dough in his nails, or a woman puts henna, it is permissible in the rural and urban areas, and it is authentic and upon it is the fatwa. And if dry mud sticks to the root of his nail and the amount of a needlehead remains from the place of washing, it is not permissible. And if he has a ring on his finger and it is tight, the chosen (position) is that he must remove it or move it so that water reaches under it. And if his hand or foot is cut off and nothing remains of the elbow and ankle, the washing is dropped, and if any remains it is obligatory. And if his nails become long until they go beyond the tips of the fingers, washing them is obligatory without dispute. And if he is created with two hands on the shoulder, the complete original one must be washed, and the other, extra; so what is opposite the obligatory site from it must be washed, and what is not, its washing is not obligatory but recommended. Similarly, what is attached to the hand from an extra finger, extra palm, or a wart must be washed. And similarly, water must be made to reach between the fingers if they are not fused.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage contains a series of rulings regarding obstacles or exceptional cases in the washing of hands and feet in wudu. The author first discusses dirt under nails: if the dirt is natural (like mud or dough) or henna for women, it is permissible and not an obstacle, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. However, if dry mud is stuck and leaves a spot the size of a needlehead unwashed, the wudu is invalid. For a tight ring, one must remove it or move it to allow water to reach the skin. If a limb is amputated, the obligation to wash the missing part drops, but if any part of the obligated area remains, it must be washed. If nails are long beyond the fingertips, they must be washed. For an extra hand on the shoulder, the original hand is washed; the extra is washed only on the part that aligns with the obligatory wash area, and the rest is recommended. Extra fingers, palms, or warts attached to the hand must be washed. Finally, water must be made to reach between the fingers unless they are fused.

Key Terms
درن (daran): Dirt or grime under the nails that is not removable without difficulty.
خاتم ضيق (khātim ḍayyiq): A tight ring that prevents water from reaching the skin underneath.
سلعة (sala'): A wart or growth attached to the skin.
ملتحمة (multahimah): Fused together (fingers).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person has a tight ring on their finger during wudu?
A) It is permissible to leave it on
B) It must be removed or moved so water reaches under it
C) Only moving it is needed, not removal
D) The ring must be removed after wudu

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, natural dirt under nails does not invalidate wudu, but a tight ring must be removed or moved to allow water to reach the skin.

Rulings on Amputated Limbs and Long Nails in Wudu
Ruling statements on amputated limbs and long nails

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi obligations for washing the hands and feet when a limb is amputated or when nails are long beyond the fingertips.

Core Idea
Washing is required on whatever remains of the obligated limb; long nails must be washed completely.

When a limb is amputated, the obligation to wash the missing part drops, but any remaining part of the obligated area must still be washed. Additionally, if nails grow beyond the fingertips, they become part of the hand that must be washed without dispute. This lesson explores these two cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Find the sentences about amputated limbs and long nails in the passage. Note the Arabic: 'قطعت يده أو رجله' and 'طالت أظفاره'.
2
Analyze the amputation ruling
The text says if the hand or foot is cut and nothing remains of the elbow or ankle, the washing is dropped (سقط الغسل). If any part of the obligated area remains, washing that part is obligatory (وجب). Understand that the fard area is hand up to elbow and foot up to ankle.
3
Analyze the long nails ruling
If nails extend beyond the fingertips, washing them is obligatory without dispute (بلا خلاف). The extension beyond the tip is still considered part of the limb that must be washed.
This includes the nail itself; the water must reach the nail and the skin under it if possible.
4
Connect the principle
Both rulings emphasize that the obligation is defined by the original fard area, but when parts are missing or extended, the ruling adjusts accordingly: missing parts drop, extended parts become obligated.
5
Apply to a scenario
A person has their hand amputated at the wrist. The elbow remains, so the remaining forearm must be washed. Another person has long nails; they must wash the nail portion beyond the fingertip.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if there is dirt or mud or dough in his nails, or a woman puts henna, it is permissible in the rural and urban areas, and it is authentic and upon it is the fatwa. And if dry mud sticks to the root of his nail and the amount of a needlehead remains from the place of washing, it is not permissible. And if he has a ring on his finger and it is tight, the chosen (position) is that he must remove it or move it so that water reaches under it. And if his hand or foot is cut off and nothing remains of the elbow and ankle, the washing is dropped, and if any remains it is obligatory. Similarly, what is attached to the hand from an extra finger, extra palm, or a wart must be washed. And similarly, water must be made to reach between the fingers if they are not fused.

Commentary
This passage contains a series of rulings regarding obstacles or exceptional cases in the washing of hands and feet in wudu. The author first discusses dirt under nails: if the dirt is natural (like mud or dough) or henna for women, it is permissible and not an obstacle, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. However, if dry mud is stuck and leaves a spot the size of a needlehead unwashed, the wudu is invalid. For a tight ring, one must remove it or move it to allow water to reach the skin. If a limb is amputated, the obligation to wash the missing part drops, but if any part of the obligated area remains, it must be washed. If nails are long beyond the fingertips, they must be washed. For an extra hand on the shoulder, the original hand is washed; the extra is washed only on the part that aligns with the obligatory wash area, and the rest is recommended. Extra fingers, palms, or warts attached to the hand must be washed. Finally, water must be made to reach between the fingers unless they are fused.

Key Terms
المرفق (al-mirfaq): Elbow; the upper limit of the hand-washing fard area.
الكعب (al-ka'b): Ankle; the upper limit of the foot-washing fard area.
أظفار (azfar): Nails; they must be washed as part of the fingers.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person's nails become long and extend beyond the fingertips?
A) Washing the extended part is recommended but not obligatory
B) Washing the extended part is obligatory without dispute
C) Only the tips of the nails need to be washed
D) The nails do not need washing because they are not part of the original finger

Key Takeaway
Amputation drops the obligation only for the missing part of the fard area; long nails must be fully washed as they are still part of the finger.

Rulings on Extra Limbs, Growths, and Fused Fingers in Wudu
Ruling statements on extra limbs, growths, and fused fingers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which parts of extra limbs, warts, and fused fingers must be washed in wudu according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Extra limbs are washed only on the part that aligns with the fard area; warts attached to the hand must be washed; water between fingers is not required if they are fused.

What if a person has an extra hand, an extra finger, or a wart? Must they be washed? The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance: the original limb is fully washed, while an extra limb is washed only on the portion opposite the obligatory site. Any attached growth like a wart must be washed. For fused fingers, water does not need to reach between them because it is impossible without harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rulings on extra limbs
Find the sentence about having two hands on the shoulder (يدان على المنكب). Note the distinction between the original (التامة الأصلية) and the extra (الزائدة).
2
Analyze the extra hand ruling
The original hand must be washed fully. For the extra hand, only the part that is opposite the obligatory site (محل الفرض) must be washed; the rest is recommended (يندب) but not obligatory.
The obligatory site for hand washing is from fingertips to elbow. So the extra hand is washed only in the area that corresponds to that region.
3
Analyze rulings on attached parts
The text says that an extra finger, extra palm, or wart (سلعة) attached to the hand must be washed (يجب غسلها). They are treated as part of the hand.
4
Analyze the fused fingers ruling
The final sentence says water must be made to reach between the fingers if they are not fused (ملتحمة). If they are fused, it is not required because it is not possible without difficulty.
This is based on the principle of ease.
5
Apply to a scenario
A person has a small extra finger attached to the pinky side. It must be washed as part of the hand. Another person has fingers fused together; water need not go between them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان في أظفاره درن أو طين أو عجين أو المرأة تضع الحناء جاز في القروي والمدني وهو صحيح وعليه الفتوى. ولو لصق بأصل ظفره طين يابس وبقي قدر رأس إبرة من موضع الغسل لم يجز، وإذا كان في أصبعه خاتم إن كان ضيقاً فالمختار أنه يجب نزعه أو تحريكه بحيث يصل الماء إلى ما تحته. ولو قطعت يده أو رجله فلم يبق من المرفق والكعب شيء سقط الغسل ولو بقي وجب، ولو طالت أظفاره حتى خرجت عن رؤوس الأصابع وجب غسلها بلا خلاف ولو خلق له يدان على المنكب فالتامة الأصلية يجب غسلها والأخرى زائدة فما حاذى منها محل الفرض وجب غسله، وما لا فلا يجب بل يندب غسله، وكذا يجب غسل ما كان مركباً على اليد من الأصبع الزائدة والكف الزائدة والسلعة، وكذا يجب إيصال الماء إلى ما بين الأصابع إذا لم تكن ملتحمة .
And if there is dirt or mud or dough in his nails, or a woman puts henna, it is permissible in the rural and urban areas, and it is authentic and upon it is the fatwa. And if dry mud sticks to the root of his nail and the amount of a needlehead remains from the place of washing, it is not permissible. And if he has a ring on his finger and it is tight, the chosen (position) is that he must remove it or move it so that water reaches under it. And if his hand or foot is cut off and nothing remains of the elbow and ankle, the washing is dropped, and if any remains it is obligatory. And if his nails become long until they go beyond the tips of the fingers, washing them is obligatory without dispute. And if he is created with two hands on the shoulder, the complete original one must be washed, and the other, extra; so what is opposite the obligatory site from it must be washed, and what is not, its washing is not obligatory but recommended. Similarly, what is attached to the hand from an extra finger, extra palm, or a wart must be washed. And similarly, water must be made to reach between the fingers if they are not fused.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage contains a series of rulings regarding obstacles or exceptional cases in the washing of hands and feet in wudu. The author first discusses dirt under nails: if the dirt is natural (like mud or dough) or henna for women, it is permissible and not an obstacle, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. However, if dry mud is stuck and leaves a spot the size of a needlehead unwashed, the wudu is invalid. For a tight ring, one must remove it or move it to allow water to reach the skin. If a limb is amputated, the obligation to wash the missing part drops, but if any part of the obligated area remains, it must be washed. If nails are long beyond the fingertips, they must be washed. For an extra hand on the shoulder, the original hand is washed; the extra is washed only on the part that aligns with the obligatory wash area, and the rest is recommended. Extra fingers, palms, or warts attached to the hand must be washed. Finally, water must be made to reach between the fingers unless they are fused.

Key Terms
منكب (mankib): Shoulder; the location where the extra hand is attached.
أصبع زائدة (isba' za'ida): An extra finger attached to the hand.
ملتحمة (multahimah): Fused together (fingers).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on washing a wart (sala') attached to the hand?
A) It must be washed
B) It is recommended but not obligatory
C) It does not need washing because it is not part of the original body
D) Only if it is large

Key Takeaway
Extra limbs and attachments are generally obligatory to wash; but a full extra hand is washed only on the part opposing the fard area, and fused fingers exempt interdigital washing.

Definition of the Ankles: Protruding Bones on the Sides
Commentary defining the ankles and providing linguistic and hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the ankle (ka'b) and cite the linguistic and hadith evidence for it.

Core Idea
The ankle is the protruding bone on the side of the foot; the Hanafi definition is supported by language and a hadith about straightening rows.

The correct definition of the ankle is essential for wudu, as the feet must be washed up to and including the ankles. In this passage, the commentator clarifies that the ankles are the two prominent bones on the sides of the foot, not on the back. He provides linguistic evidence from Arabic lexicons and a prophetic hadith, and refutes an alternative view transmitted by Hisham from Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed definition
The passage contrasts two definitions of the ankle: one as the protruding side bone (Hanafi) and one as the bone on the back of the foot (reported by Hisham). Recognize that the issue is about the location of the ankle for wudu.
The alternative view is based on a transmission from Imam Muhammad via Hisham.
2
State the Hanafi definition
The Hanafi definition is that the ankles are the two bones protruding from the sides of the foot, i.e., the raised ones. This is the relied-upon position.
3
Examine the linguistic evidence
The text cites al-Sihah and al-Asma'i: 'ka'b' derives from height, and the word refers to the protruding bone. The denier al-Asma'i rejected the popular notion that it is on the back of the foot.
The term 'ka'b' is etymologically linked to elevation, like the Ka'bah.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The hadith about straightening rows in prayer: companions touched their ankles to each other's ankles. This indicates that the ankle is the lateral bone that can make contact side-to-side.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud and is considered supporting evidence.
5
Understand the refutation of Hisham
The text explains that Hisham's narration is a mistake in transmission. Muhammad's statement about cutting khuffs below the ankles for a muhrim was misapplied to wudu. Also, the linguistic dual form 'ka'bayn' would require a single form 'ka'b' if it were one bone per foot, but it is two per foot.
This refutation demonstrates how Hanafi scholars critically evaluate narrations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ورجليه بكعبيه) أي مع كعبيه كما تقدم والكعبان هما العظمان الناشزان من جانبي القدم أي المرتفعان كذا في المغرب وصححه في الهداية وغيرها. وروى هشام عن محمد أنه في ظهر القدم عند معقد الشراك قالوا : هو سهو من هشام لأن محمداً إنما قال ذلك في المحرم إذا لم يجد النعلين حيث يقطع خفيه أسفل من الكعبين وأشار محمد بيده إلى موضع القطع فنقله هشام إلى الطهارة. ويرد على هشام من جهة المعنى أيضاً بأن ما يوجد من خلق الإنسان فإن تثنيته بعبارة الجمع كقوله تعالى فقد صغت قلوبكما) [التحريم: ٤] أي قلبا كما وما كان اثنين من خلقه فتثنيته بلفظها ولو كان كما زعمه هشام لقيل الكعاب كالمرافق . كذا في المبسوط وغيره. وقد يقال أنه غير متعين الجواز أن يعتبر الكعبان بالنسبة إلى ما للمرء من جنس الرجل وهو اثنان لا بالنظر إلى كل رجل وحدها، فالأولى الردّ عليه من اللغة والسنة؛ أما اللغة فقد صرح في الصحاح بأنه العظم الناشز كما ذكرناه قال: وأنكر الأصمعي قول الناس أنه في ظهر القدم .اهـ. قالوا: الكعب في كلام العرب مأخوذ من العلق ومنه سميت الكعبة لارتفاعها وأما السنة فما رواه أبو داود مرفوعاً «والله لتقيمن صفوفكم أو ليخالفن الله بين قلوبكم قال فرأيت الرجل يلزق منكبه بمنكب صاحبه وركبته بركبة صاحبه وكعبه بكعبه. وما وقع في الشروح من أنه كان ينبغي غسل يد واحدة ورجل واحدة لأن مقابلة الجمع بالجمع تقتضي انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد والجواب بأن وجوب واحدة بالعبارة والأخرى بالدلالة طائل تحت بعد انعقاد الإجماع القطعي على افتراضهما بحيث صار معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة، ومن البحث في «إلى» وفي القراءتين في الأرجل فإن الإجماع انعقد على غسلهما، ولا اعتبار بخلاف الروافض فلذا تركنا ما قرروه هنا، والزائد على الرجلين كالزائد على اليدين كما صرح به في المجتبى، ولو قال ورجليه بكعبيه أو مسح على خفيه لكان أولى .
His statement: 'and his feet with his ankles' means with his ankles as previously. And the two ankles are the two bones protruding from the two sides of the foot, i.e., the raised ones. This is in al-Maghrib and was corrected in al-Hidayah and others. Hisham narrated from Muhammad that it is on the back of the foot at the crossing of the shoelace. They said: It is a mistake of Hisham because Muhammad only said that regarding the muhrim when he does not find sandals, where he cuts his khuff below the ankles, and Muhammad pointed with his hand to the place of cutting, but Hisham transferred it to purification. And a refutation against Hisham from the perspective of meaning is also that whatever is found of the creation of man, its dual is expressed with the plural term, like His statement: 'Your hearts have inclined' [66:4] meaning two hearts. And whatever is two from His creation, its dual is expressed with its own word. If it were as Hisham claimed, it would have been said 'al-ka'ab' (like al-marafiq). This is in al-Mabsut and others. And it may be said that it is not determined that the two ankles be considered in relation to what a person has of the genus of foot, which is two, not with regard to each foot alone. So the better approach is to refute him from language and sunnah. As for language, al-Sihah explicitly states that it is the protruding bone as we mentioned, and al-Asma'i denied the common saying that it is on the back of the foot. They said: The word 'ka'b' in Arabic is derived from 'al-uluww' (height), and from it the Ka'bah is named for its height. As for sunnah, what Abu Dawud narrated as a marfu' hadith: 'By Allah, you will straighten your rows or Allah will cause discord among your hearts.' He said: 'I saw a man attaching his shoulder to the shoulder of his companion, his knee to his companion's knee, and his ankle to his companion's ankle.' And what occurred in the commentaries that it would have been sufficient to wash one hand and one foot because the matching of plural with plural implies distribution of individuals; the response is that the obligation of one is by expression and the other by implication, which is a benefit after the definitive consensus on their obligation, to the point that it is known from the religion necessarily. And from the investigation of 'إلى' and the two readings of 'al-arjul', there is consensus on washing them, and no consideration is given to the opposition of the Rafidah. Therefore, we left what they established here. And what is extra on the feet is like what is extra on the hands, as stated in al-Mujtaba. And if he had said 'and his feet with his ankles' or 'wiping on his khuff', it would have been better.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a definition and evidence section regarding the ankles in the context of wudu. The author explains that the phrase 'and his feet with his ankles' means including the ankles, and defines the ankles as the two prominent bones on the sides of the foot. He refutes the narration of Hisham from Muhammad that the ankle is on the back of the foot, showing it is a mistake in transmission. He then provides linguistic evidence from the Arabic language (the word 'ka'b' relates to height) and from the sunnah (the hadith about straightening rows, where companions touched ankles). The passage also addresses a possible objection about the distribution of plural commands and concludes that consensus has firmly established the obligation. Finally, it notes that the ruling for extra feet parts is the same as for extra hands, as stated in al-Mujtaba.

Key Terms
الكعبان (al-ka'bayn): The two ankle bones on the sides of the foot, protruding and raised.
الشراك (al-shirak): The shoelace or strap crossing.

Key Takeaway
The ankle for wudu is the protruding bone on the side of the foot, proven by language and prophetic practice.

Definition of Masah and the Ruling on Leftover Water
Definition of masah and a disputed ruling on using residual water for wiping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of masah and apply the relied-upon ruling regarding the use of leftover water for wiping the head.

Core Idea
Leftover water from washing cannot be used for wiping – the water has already been used for purification once.

This lesson examines the technical definition of masah (wiping) in Hanafi fiqh and a key dispute about the source of wetness for wiping. While the linguistic meaning is simple, the jurists refined it to clarify that wiping requires contact with a wet hand, and the wetness can come from washing but not from a previous wipe. A significant disagreement arises over using residual water that remains on a limb after washing – can one use that leftover wetness to wipe the head? The passage presents both sides and gives the relied-upon Hanafi ruling.

Guided Path
1
Define masah linguistically and technically
Understand the two definitions: linguistically, it is passing the hand over something; technically, it is the contact of a wet hand with the limb, even with remaining wetness after washing, but not after wiping.
The technical definition excludes wetness from a previous wipe, as that water is considered used.
2
Identify the key condition about the source of wetness
Note that masah can be performed with wetness remaining from washing (fadl ghusl) but not with wetness remaining from a previous wipe (fadl masah). This distinction is crucial for the subsequent dispute.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Hakim al-Shahid held that leftover water from washing cannot be used for wiping the head. The majority of mashayikh rejected this, citing Muhammad's narration that wiping the khuff with leftover wetness on the palm is permissible. The author sides with al-Hakim because al-Karkhi transmitted from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that it is not permissible.
The dispute is about the permissibility of using fadl ghusl for wiping, not about the obligation of wiping the head itself.
4
Apply the tarjih mechanism
The author considers al-Karkhi's explicit text from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf as the authoritative statement. Thus, the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that one must use new water for wiping the head; leftover water from washing is not sufficient.
This is an example of tarjih based on the reliability of the narration and the principle that a clear text (nass) outweighs a general analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومسح ربع رأسه) هو في اللغة إمرار اليد على الشيء، واصطلاحاً إصابة اليد المبتلة العضو ولو ببلل باقٍ بعد غسل لا بعد مسح. والآلة لم تقصد إلا للإبطال إلى المحل فإذا قوله : (ولو ببلل باق بعد غسل قال الرملي : أقول قال ابن كمال باشا في الإصلاح والإيضاح : وأما الذي بقي في العضو بعد الغسل فقال الحاكم الشهيد : لا يجوز المسح به أيضاً. وخطأه عامة المشايخ لما ذكره محمد في مسح الخف إذا توضأ ثم مسح على الخف ببلة بقيت على كفه بعد الغسل جاز والصحيح ما قاله الحاكم فقد نص الكرخي في جامعه الكبير على الرواية عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله مفسراً معللاً إذا مسح رأسه بفضل غسل ذراعيه لم يجز إلا بماء جديد لأنه قد تطهر به مرة
His statement: 'and wiping a quarter of his head' – it is linguistically passing the hand over something, and technically, it is the contact of a wet hand with a limb, even with remaining wetness after washing, not after wiping. And the instrument is not intended except for invalidation to the place. So his statement: 'even with remaining wetness after washing.' Al-Ramli said: I say: Ibn Kamal Pasha said in al-Islah and al-Idah: As for that which remains on the limb after washing, al-Hakim al-Shahid said: It is not permissible to wipe with it. But the majority of scholars considered him wrong because of what Muhammad mentioned about wiping the khuff: if he performed wudu and then wiped his khuff with leftover wetness on his palm after washing, it is permissible. The correct is what al-Hakim said, for al-Karkhi explicitly stated in his al-Jami' al-Kabir the narration from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, explaining and giving cause: if he wipes his head with leftover from washing his arms, it is not permissible except with new water because he has purified with it once.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'masah' (wiping) in the context of wudu. Linguistically, it means passing the hand over something. Technically, it is the contact of a wet hand with the limb, and the wetness can be residual from washing (not from a previous wipe). The text then engages in a dispute: Al-Hakim al-Shahid held that leftover water on the limb after washing cannot be used for wiping the head, but the majority of scholars (the general body of mashayikh) rejected this, citing Muhammad's narration that one can wipe the khuff with leftover wetness on the palm. However, the author suggests that the correct position is that of al-Hakim, because al-Karkhi transmitted from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that wiping the head with leftover from washing the arms is not permissible; one must use new water because the water has already been used for purification once. This indicates a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school on this detail. The passage ends abruptly but gives the core ruling.

Key Terms
المسح (al-masah): Legally: wiping a limb with a wet hand, whether the wetness is from washing or from a separate source, but not from a previous wiping.
فضل غسل (fadl ghusl): Excess water remaining on a limb after washing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Wiping the head requires fresh water; leftover water from washing is considered used and cannot be used for another act of purification.

Definition of Ka'bayn and Ruling on Washing Feet
Explains the ruling on washing feet including the ankles and refutes a divergent opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ka'bayn, explain why Hisham's narration is rejected, and derive the ruling from the hadith.

Core Idea
The ankles (ka'bayn) are the two protruding bones on the sides, not the back of the foot—established by language and prophetic practice.

This passage addresses a key point in wudu: what exactly must be washed when washing the feet. It resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the location of the ankles (ka'bayn) through linguistic and hadith evidence, affirming the established position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the definition of ka'bayn as the two protruding bones on the sides of the ankle. This is the relied-upon view in the school.
The passage cites al-Mughrib, al-Hidayah, and other sources to support this.
2
Examine the opposing narration
Hisham narrated from Imam Muhammad that the ka'b is on the back of the foot at the crossing point of the shoelace. Understand why this was considered a mistake.
The text explains that Hisham confused a ruling about cutting khuffs for a muhrim with the location for wudu.
3
Analyze the linguistic proof
Arabic language sources (al-Sihah, al-Asma'i) confirm that ka'b refers to the protruding bone. Also, the dual form 'ka'ban' indicates it refers to two feet, not two bones on one foot.
If it were on the back, the plural 'ka'ab' would be used like 'marafiq' (elbows).
4
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith from Abu Dawud shows the companions aligning their ankles (ka'bahum) when straightening rows, which only makes sense if the ankle is the protruding side bone.
The hadith is used to confirm the definition and practice.
5
Conclude the tarjih
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi. The opposing narration is rejected as a misunderstanding (sahw).
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and others affirm the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يجب بل يندب، غسله، وكذا يجب غسل ما كان مركباً على اليد من الأصبع الزائدة والكف الزائدة والسلعة، وكذا يجب إيصال الماء إلى ما بين الأصابع إذا لم تكن ملتحمة .

قوله : ( ورجليه بكعبيه) أي مع كعبيه كما تقدم والكعبان هما العظمان الناشزان من جانبي القدم أي المرتفعان كذا في المغرب وصححه في الهداية وغيرها. وروى هشام عن محمد أنه في ظهر القدم عند معقد الشراك قالوا : هو سهو من هشام لأن محمداً إنما قال ذلك في المحرم إذا لم يجد النعلين حيث يقطع خفيه أسفل من الكعبين وأشار محمد بيده إلى موضع القطع فنقله هشام إلى الطهارة. ويرد على هشام من جهة المعنى أيضاً بأن ما يوجد من خلق الإنسان فإن تثنيته بعبارة الجمع كقوله تعالى فقد صغت قلوبكما) [التحريم: ٤] أي قلبا كما وما كان اثنين من خلقه فتثنيته بلفظها ولو كان كما زعمه هشام لقيل الكعاب كالمرافق . كذا في المبسوط وغيره. وقد يقال أنه غير متعين الجواز أن يعتبر الكعبان بالنسبة إلى ما للمرء من جنس الرجل وهو اثنان لا بالنظر إلى كل رجل وحدها، فالأولى الردّ عليه من اللغة والسنة؛ أما اللغة فقد صرح في الصحاح بأنه العظم الناشز كما ذكرناه قال: وأنكر الأصمعي قول الناس أنه في ظهر القدم .اهـ. قالوا: الكعب في كلام العرب مأخوذ من العلق ومنه سميت الكعبة لارتفاعها وأما السنة فما رواه أبو داود مرفوعاً «والله لتقيمن صفوفكم أو ليخالفن الله بين قلوبكم قال فرأيت الرجل يلزق منكبه بمنكب صاحبه وركبته بركبة صاحبه وكعبه بكعبه. وما وقع في الشروح من أنه كان ينبغي غسل يد واحدة ورجل واحدة لأن مقابلة الجمع بالجمع تقتضي انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد والجواب بأن وجوب واحدة بالعبارة والأخرى بالدلالة طائل تحته بعد انعقاد الإجماع القطعي على افتراضهما بحيث صار معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة، ومن البحث في «إلى» وفي القراءتين في الأرجل فإن الإجماع انعقد على غسلهما، ولا اعتبار بخلاف الروافض فلذا تركنا ما قرروه هنا، والزائد على الرجلين كالزائد على اليدين كما صرح به في المجتبى، ولو قال ورجليه بكعبيه أو مسح على خفيه لكان أولى .
So it is not obligatory but recommended to wash it, and likewise washing what is attached to the hand of an extra finger, an extra palm, and a wart is obligatory, and likewise it is obligatory to deliver water between the fingers if they are not fused. His statement: ( and his two feet with his two ankles) i.e., including his two ankles as previously mentioned, and the two ankles are the two protruding bones from the sides of the foot, i.e., the elevated ones. Thus in al-Mughrib, and it was declared correct in al-Hidayah and others. Hisham narrated from Muhammad that it is on the back of the foot at the crossing point of the shoelace. They said: This is a mistake from Hisham because Muhammad only said that regarding the muhrim if he does not find sandals, where he cuts his khuffs below the two ankles, and Muhammad pointed with his hand to the place of cutting, so Hisham transmitted it to purity. And he is refuted from his meaning also by the fact that whatever exists of the creation of man, its dual is expressed by a plural expression like His statement: 'the hearts of you both have inclined' meaning the two hearts, and whatever is two from his creation, its dual is expressed by its own word; if it were as Hisham claimed, one would say 'al-ka'ab' like 'al-marafiq'. Thus in al-Mabsut and others. And it may be said that it is not determined as permissible to consider the two ankles in relation to what a person has of the two feet, which are two, not with respect to each foot alone. So the first response is from language and sunnah: As for language, it is stated in al-Sihah that it is the protruding bone as we mentioned. He said: Al-Asma'i denied people's saying that it is on the back of the foot. End. They said: The word ka'b in Arabic is derived from 'uluww (height), and from it the Ka'bah is named for its height. As for the Sunnah, there is what Abu Dawud narrated as a marfu' hadith: 'By Allah, you will straighten your rows or Allah will cause discord among your hearts.' He said: Then I saw a man sticking his shoulder to his companion's shoulder, his knee to his companion's knee, and his ankle to his companion's ankle. And what occurs in the commentaries that it would require washing one hand and one foot because the plural addressing the plural implies the division of individuals among individuals, and the answer that one is obligatory by wording and the other by indication is vain after the definitive consensus on their being fard such that it became known from the religion by necessity, and from the discussion of 'ila' and the two readings of 'al-rijal' (the feet), because consensus concluded on washing them, and no attention is paid to the Rafidis' opposition; thus we left what they established here. And the extra on the two feet is like the extra on the two hands as stated in al-Mujtaba. And if he had said: 'and his two feet with his two ankles' or 'or wiping over his khuffs', it would have been better.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on washing the feet during wudu, specifically the inclusion of the ankles (ka'bayn). The author begins by clarifying that while washing the extra parts of the hand is recommended but not obligatory, washing the feet must include the ankles. The term 'ka'bayn' is defined as the two prominent bones on either side of the ankle joint, and this definition is supported by linguistic sources and the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as recorded in the hadith about straightening prayer rows. The text then refutes a narration from Hisham, who claimed that Imam Muhammad considered the ka'b to be on the back of the foot. The author explains that Hisham misunderstood Imam Muhammad's statement, which was actually about where a muhrim should cut his khuffs, not about the location of the ankle in wudu. Additionally, linguistic analysis shows that the dual 'ka'ban' is used for the two feet, not for each foot individually, and that the correct term for the back of the foot would be 'ka'ab' if it were as Hisham claimed. The hadith of Abu Dawud is cited to confirm that the ankles are indeed the protruding bones, as the companions used to align their ankles when straightening rows. The passage concludes by affirming the consensus on washing both feet including the ankles, rejecting the Rafidi view, and noting that rulings on extra parts apply similarly to hands and feet.

Key Terms
الكعب (al-ka'b): The two protruding bones on the sides of the ankle.
السلعة (al-sil'ah): A wart or growth on the hand that requires washing if attached.
الأصبع الزائدة (al-isba' al-za'idah): An extra finger; it is wajib to wash it during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The ankles (ka'bayn) are the two protruding bones on the sides of the foot, and washing them is part of the obligatory wudu.

Taharah — Purification

The Obligatory Amount of Wiping the Head in Hanafi Wudu
Defines masah, sets the obligatory amount as a quarter, presents proofs and rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the fard in wiping the head is a quarter, explain the evidence from the Quranic baa and the hadith of Mughirah, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i requirement of the entire head.

Core Idea
Wiping a quarter of the head suffices for wudu — the baa means adhesion, not entire coverage.

In this lesson, we delve into the Hanafi ruling on the minimum amount required when wiping the head (masah al-ra's) during wudu. The source text from al-Bahr al-Raiq defines masah, establishes that a quarter of the head is obligatory (fard), and presents the proofs from the Quran and the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him). It also addresses the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school, which requires wiping the entire head. By understanding the linguistic and legal arguments, you will appreciate why the Hanafi school adopts the quarter view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is masah al-ra's (wiping the head) during wudu. The ruling is that it is fard, and the obligatory minimum is a quarter of the head (rub' al-ra's). Note that the Hanafi school uniquely specifies a quarter, while others require more.
The source states: 'والصحيح ما قاله الحاكم' but later the author sides with the majority that a quarter is fard.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The primary evidence is the Quranic verse (5:6): 'wa imsahu bi ru'usikum' (وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ). The preposition 'baa' is the crux of the interpretation. Hanafis interpret it as ba' al-ilhaq (adhesion), meaning the wiping attaches to any part, not necessarily the whole.
The source discusses that the baa cannot be ba' al-tab'id (partitive) as the primary meaning; it is only incidental.
3
Analyze the secondary dalil from Sunnah
The hadith of al-Mughirah ibn Shu'bah reports that the Prophet wiped his forelock (nasiyah) during wudu. This shows that wiping a part (even less than a quarter) is permissible, but the Hanafi school sets the quarter as the minimum based on other texts and consensus.
The source uses the hadith to rebut the claim that the entire head is required, but notes that the forelock alone is less than a quarter, so it does not set the minimum itself.
4
Understand the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that wiping the entire head is fard. His evidence includes the same verse but interprets the baa as partitive, and he also uses the hadith of Mughirah differently. The Hanafi response is that the baa's primary meaning is adhesion, and the partitive meaning is weak.
The source refutes the Shafi'i claim that the verse is mujmal, arguing that the baa clearly indicates adhesion.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and practical consequence
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi (the relied-upon position) and is supported by the majority of early Hanafi scholars (al-Karkhi, al-Tahawi, etc.). The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that a Hanafi's wudu is valid if he wipes a quarter of his head, whereas a Shafi'i would not consider it valid unless the entire head is wiped.
The source also discusses a sub-issue: using leftover moisture from washing another limb for wiping; the majority allow it if the moisture is from washing, not from a previous wiping.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: (and wiping a quarter of his head) in language is passing the hand over something, and terminologically it is the wet hand touching the limb, even with leftover moisture after washing not after wiping. And the instrument is not intended except for delivering to the place, and then his statement: (even with leftover moisture after washing) al-Ramli said: I say, Ibn Kamal Pasha said in al-Islah wa al-Idah: As for what remains on the limb after washing, al-Hakim al-Shahid said: wiping with it is not permissible either. And the majority of scholars declared him wrong because of what Muhammad mentioned about wiping the khuff: if he performs wudu then wipes over the khuff with moisture that remained on his palm after washing, it is permissible. And the correct is what al-Hakim said, for al-Karkhi explicitly stated in his al-Jami' al-Kabir based on a narration from Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, explaining and giving reason: if he wipes his head with the leftover of washing his arms, it is not permissible unless with new water because he has purified with it once. A person hit by rain in the amount of the obligatory part, it suffices him; and if he wipes with moisture on his hand taken from another limb, it is absolutely not permissible. Regarding the amount of the obligatory part, there are narrations, the most authentic in both transmission and understanding is what is in al-Mukhtasar. As for the first, it is due to the agreement of the texts upon it and the transmission of the early scholars like Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi and Abu Ja'far al-Tahawi, and the narration of al-nasiyah is other than it because the forelock is less than a quarter of the head. As for the understanding, there is difference in its interpretation. In al-Hidayah, the Book is mujmal, and the hadith of al-Mughirah that he, peace be upon him, wiped his forelock became a clarification for it, and it is rebutted by several aspects: The first aspect: there is no ijmal in it because if there is no custom that validates intending some part, it would indicate its essence, which is the whole; or if it indicates some part absolutely, it is obtained within the whole and other than it, so there is no ijmal. Thus in al-Tahrir. And what is in al-Bada'i' of establishing ijmal by the baa carrying the meanings of connection, adhesion, and partitivity, with no evidence specifying one, is refuted by the fact that according to the verifying scholars, its meaning is adhesion because it is the agreed-upon meaning, unlike others, for the verifying scholars did not establish them. Partitivity is not an original meaning but is obtained incidentally within adhesion. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And he said in al-Tahrir: Know that a group of later scholars claimed partitivity in examples like 'sharibna bi-ma' al-bahr' (we drank from the sea water), and Ibn Jinni says in al-Sina'ah: We do not know that from our companions. The conclusion is that it is weak due to strong disagreement, and because the agreed-upon meaning of adhesion is possible for it, so partitivity is established accidentally due to the lack of covering of the adhered-to object, not as a denoted meaning. End. The second: The disputed baa exists in the hadith of al-Mughirah, so it is mujmal according to their claim; then how does it clarify the mujmal? Thus the dispute returns to the hadith as well. The third: Making the hadith of al-Mughirah a clarifier of the verse is dependent on establishing that this wudu was the first wudu of the Prophet, peace be upon him, after the revelation of the verse, because if not, it would necessitate a delay in clarification from its time. Sar. And Allah knows best. And Ibn al-Kamal took it from al-Mujtaba, commentary of al-Quduri. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah, the symbol of al-Muhit: If he has moisture in his palm and wipes his head with it, it suffices. Al-Hakim al-Shahid said: This is if he did not use it on a limb of his limbs, i.e., by washing some of his limbs by putting his hand into a vessel until it becomes wet. But if he used it on a limb of his limbs and moisture remained in his palm, it is not permissible. And most are of the opinion that what al-Hakim al-Shahid said is wrong; the correct is that Muhammad intended by that when he washed a limb of his limbs and moisture remained in his palm, meaning he did not intend to put his hand into a vessel until it became wet as al-Hakim claimed. His statement: And the instrument is not intended for delivery; the first expression is 'arrival' to make the branching correct after it. His statement: (it is absolutely not permissible) i.e., whether that limb was washed or wiped. His statement: (and it is rebutted by aspects up to the fourth) I say: In these three aspects there is consideration. As for the first, because the lack of custom does not indicate wiping the whole due to what he will transmit from al-Tahrir that the agreed-upon meaning of adhesion for the baa is possible, so partitivity is established accidentally due to the lack of covering of the adhered-to object; and because his statement 'or if' meaning the custom yielded some part absolutely etc. It is said upon it that that absolute part which is obligatory is not known in its amount, and then ijmal is not negated, and its occurrence within the whole does not negate it either, rather it negates the need for its clarification. And if it is intended by the absolute part that the obligatory duty is fulfilled by any part, and if
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq covers the rulings on wiping the head (masah) during wudu. It begins by defining masah as passing the wet hand over the head, and notes that any leftover moisture after washing (not after wiping) can be used. The main discussion is about the obligatory amount: the Hanafi school holds that wiping one quarter of the head (rub' al-ra's) fulfills the fard, based on texts of earlier scholars and a preferred narration in al-Mukhtasar. The author presents proofs from the Quran (the baa in verse 5:6 is interpreted as adhesive, not partitive) and the hadith of al-Mughirah where the Prophet wiped his forelock. He refutes the idea that the entire head must be wiped, citing the majority of scholars (including al-Tahawi and al-Karkhi) who affirm the quarter. The passage also contains a lengthy rebuttal of objections to the quarter view, addressing the interpretation of the baa (ba' al-ilhaq vs. ba' al-tab'id) and the use of the hadith of Mughirah as a clarifier. Additionally, there is a sub-discussion on using leftover water from washing other limbs for wiping; the author sides with the majority that it is permissible if the moisture remains after washing a limb, contrary to al-Hakim al-Shahid's stricter view. The passage ends with critical analysis of the three objections raised against the quarter view, showing that they are not conclusive.

Key Terms
المسح (al-masah): Terminologically: passing the wet hand over the limb.
الربع (al-rub'): One quarter of the head; the obligatory amount for wiping.
الناصية (al-nasiyah): The forelock; the part of the head that the Prophet wiped in the hadith of Mughirah, which is less than a quarter.
الباء (al-baa'): The preposition 'bi' in the Quranic verse; its interpretation (adhesion vs. partitivity) determines the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires wiping at least one quarter of the head in wudu, based on the interpretation of the baa as adhesion and the practice of the Prophet.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
The Wajib Amount of Head Wipe in Wudu: One Quarter
Commentary on the required amount of head wipe and refutation of ambiguity claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the minimum wajib head wipe is one quarter of the head, and explain the proof from the Quranic verse using the Hanafi interpretation of the preposition 'ba'.

Core Idea
The 'ba' in 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' means attachment, not partiality—so a quarter suffices for wudu.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq resolves a key dispute: how much of the head must be wiped? The author affirms that the most authentic position, both in transmission and reasoning, is that a quarter is the wajib amount. He then refutes the claim that the Quranic verse is ambiguous (mujmal) and requires a hadith for clarification, using three powerful arguments grounded in Arabic linguistics and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Wiping the head is wajib in wudu; the minimum amount according to the preferred narration is a quarter of the head.
The narration of the forelock (nasiyah) is rejected because it is less than a quarter.
2
Locate the primary proof
The Quranic verse 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' (5:6) is the primary dalil for the obligation of head wipe.
The imperative 'imsahu' indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the preposition 'ba'
Hanafi linguists and usulis hold that the preposition 'ba' here means ilsaq (attachment), not tab'id (partiality).
Partiality is an accidental consequence when the attached object does not cover the whole; it is not an original meaning.
4
Examine the opposing claim
Some scholars argue that the verse is mujmal because 'ba' could indicate partiality, requiring the hadith of al-Mughirah (wiping the forelock) for clarification.
This claim is attributed to later Hanafis and Shafi'is.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi refutation
The author gives three refutations: (a) the verse is not mujmal because 'ba' has a clear agreed meaning; (b) the same 'ba' appears in the hadith, so it too would be mujmal; (c) proving that the Prophet's wudu in the hadith occurred after the revelation of the verse is required but not established.
These refutations are drawn from al-Tahrir, Fath al-Qadir, and other sources.
