If rain water reaches him to the extent of the fard, it suffices for him. And if he wipes with moisture in his hand taken from another limb, it is not permissible at all. Regarding the extent of the fard, there are narrations; the most authentic according to narration and reasoning is what is in al-Mukhtar. As for the first, it is due to the agreement of the texts upon it and the transmission of the early scholars such as Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi and Abu Ja'far al-Tahawi. The narration of the forelock is something else because the forelock is less than a quarter of the head. As for the reasoning, they differed in its interpretation. In al-Hidayah, [it states] that the Book [Quran] is mujmal (ambiguous) and that the hadith of al-Mughirah regarding the Prophet’s wiping of his forelock becomes a clarification (bayan) for it. This is refuted in several ways: The first way is that there is no ambiguity in it because if there is no customary usage in its like that validates intending a part, it would denote its name, which is wiping, i.e., the whole. Or if there is a custom, it would denote a part absolutely, and this [part] is obtained within the comprehensive wiping, so there is no ambiguity. Thus in al-Tahrir. What is in al-Bada'i' regarding the affirmation of ambiguity by saying that the ba could be for connection, attachment, or partiality, and there is no evidence specifying one of them, is refuted because its meaning according to the verifying scholars is attachment, as that is the agreed-upon meaning, unlike others. The verifying scholars have not established [the other meanings]; partiality is not an original meaning but rather occurs within attachment. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. He said in al-Tahrir: Know that a group of later scholars claimed partiality in [phrases] like 'we drank with the water of the sea,' and Ibn Jinni says in al-Sina'ah: We do not know this from our companions. The conclusion is that it is weak due to the strong disagreement, and because the agreed-upon meaning of attachment is possible for it, so partiality is established accidentally due to the non-comprehensiveness of the attached thing, not as a denoted meaning. End. The second: The disputed ba is present in the hadith of al-Mughirah, so it is mujmal according to their claim; how then can it clarify the mujmal? The dispute returns to the hadith as well. The third: Making the hadith of al-Mughirah a clarifier of the verse is contingent upon proving that this wudu was the first wudu of the Prophet after the revelation of the verse, because if it were not so, it would entail delaying clarification from the time of need. And Allah knows best. Ibn al-Kamal took this from al-Mujtaba Sharh al-Quduri. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah, with the symbol of al-Muhit: If there is moisture in his palm and he wipes his head with it, it suffices him. Al-Hakim al-Shahid said: This is if he did not use it on a limb among his limbs, i.e., he washed some of his limbs by inserting his hand into a vessel until it became wet. But if he used it on a limb among his limbs and moisture remained in his palm, it is not permissible. Most [scholars] hold that what al-Hakim al-Shahid said is wrong; the correct view is that Muhammad intended by that the case when he washed a limb among his limbs and the moisture remained in his palm, meaning he did not intend that he inserted his hand into a vessel until it became wet as al-Hakim claimed. His statement: 'The tool was not intended for transmission' – it would be better to express it as 'reaching' so that the sub-branches that follow are correct. His statement: 'It is not permissible absolutely' – i.e., whether that limb was washed or wiped. His statement: 'It is refuted in ways up to the fourth' – I say: There is consideration in these three ways. As for the first, because the lack of custom does not necessitate wiping the whole, as he will later quote from al-Tahrir that the agreed-upon attachment of ba is possible, so partiality is established accidentally due to non-comprehensiveness of the attached thing. And because his saying 'or if there is a custom, it would denote a part absolutely' etc. It is said against it that that absolute part which is obligatory is not known in its extent, and then ambiguity is not removed. Its occurrence within the comprehensive does not negate it either; rather it negates the need for its clarification. If it is intended by 'denoting the absolute part' that the fard is fulfilled by any part whatsoever, and
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a critical commentary on the required amount of head wipe in wudu and the use of moisture from other limbs. The author begins with a ruling: if a person is hit by rain to the extent of the fard (the obligatory amount of water for wudu), it suffices. Then he states that wiping with leftover moisture from a washed limb is invalid unconditionally. The core discussion is the quantity of the head wipe. The author notes multiple narrations, affirming that the most authentic in both transmission and reasoning is the one in al-Mukhtar: one quarter of the head. The narration of the forelock (nasiyah) is rejected because the forelock is less than a quarter. The reasoning part delves into a dispute about the Quranic verse 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum'. Some scholars, including later ones, claimed the verse is ambiguous (mujmal) because the preposition 'ba' could mean partiality (tab'id) or attachment (ilsaq), requiring the hadith of al-Mughirah (where the Prophet wiped his forelock) to clarify. The author refutes this claim with three arguments: First, the verse is not ambiguous; 'ba' in the view of verifying scholars always means attachment, and partiality is only an accidental outcome when the attached object is not completely covered. Second, the same 'ba' appears in the hadith, so if it were ambiguous, the hadith would be ambiguous too and could not clarify. Third, proving that the Prophet's wudu in that hadith occurred after the revelation of the verse is required for it to be a clarification, and this is not established. The passage then quotes Ibn al-Kamal from al-Mujtaba and al-Tatarkhaniyyah about a second ruling: wiping the head with moisture already in the palm is valid if that moisture was not used on a limb (i.e., taken directly from water). Al-Hakim al-Shahid mistakenly thought it referred to moisture remaining after washing a limb, but most scholars correct this: Imam Muhammad's statement actually refers to the case where one washes a limb and moisture remains in the palm, and they interpret it as valid because the moisture is still pure and sufficient. The author concludes with lexical remarks on the text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Key Terms
الباء (al-ba'): The preposition 'ba' in the Quranic verse 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum'. Its interpretation is central: Hanafis hold it means ilsaq (attachment), not tab'id (partiality).
الإلصاق (al-ilsaq): Attachment; the meaning Hanafis assign to the preposition 'ba' in the verse. It implies that the wiping action is attached to the head, not that the entire head must be covered.
التبعيض (al-tab'id): Partiality; the claim that 'ba' indicates only a part of the object. Hanafis reject this as an original meaning.
الناصية (al-nasiyah): The forelock; the part of the hair above the forehead. The hadith mentions the Prophet wiping his forelock. Hanafis reject this as evidence for the required amount because the forelock is less than a quarter.
المجمل (al-mujmal): Ambiguous; a text that requires external clarification. The author refutes the claim that the Quranic verse is mujmal regarding the quantity of the wipe.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum amount of head wipe required in wudu according to the preferred Hanafi position in this passage?
A) The entire head
B) A quarter of the head
C) The forelock only
D) Any part

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires wiping at least one quarter of the head in wudu, based on the Quranic verse interpreted with 'ba' as attachment, and rejects the claim that the verse is ambiguous.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
6 min
Using Leftover Moisture from Washed Limbs for Wiping: Invalidity and Correct Interpretation
Commentary on the ruling of using leftover moisture from a washed limb for wiping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that wiping the head with moisture remaining on the hand after washing another limb is invalid, and identify the correct interpretation of Imam Muhammad's statement.

Core Idea
Moisture left from a washed limb is considered used (musta'mal) and cannot be used for wiping; only moisture taken directly from water is valid.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq also addresses a secondary issue: if a person wipes his head with moisture still on his hand after washing a limb, is it valid? The text rules that it is invalid unconditionally. It then clarifies a misunderstanding by al-Hakim al-Shahid regarding Imam Muhammad's statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states: 'If he wipes with moisture in his hand taken from another limb, it is not permissible at all.' This is the absolute rule.
This applies whether the other limb was washed or wiped.
2
Understand the dispute
Al-Hakim al-Shahid misinterpreted Imam Muhammad's statement, thinking it referred only to moisture from immersing the hand in water (not used on a limb).
He said it is valid if the moisture is from immersion, invalid if from a washed limb.
3
Apply the correct interpretation
Most scholars corrected al-Hakim: Imam Muhammad's statement actually refers to the case where one washes a limb and then uses the leftover moisture—still invalid. The correct view is that it is invalid in both cases.
The moisture from a washed limb is considered used (musta'mal).
4
Recognize the exception
If the moisture is obtained by immersing the hand in water without washing a limb (e.g., from rain or dipping), then wiping with it is valid.
This is derived from the discussion on 'kuff' (palm).

The author concludes with lexical remarks on the text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Key Terms
الماء المستعمل (al-ma' al-musta'mal): Used water; water that has been used for a act of purification (like washing a limb) and is no longer valid for a new act.
البلل (al-balal): Moisture, wetness left on the hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling when a person wipes his head with moisture that remains on his hand after washing his forearm?
A) It is valid
B) It is invalid
C) It is valid only if the forearm was washed with the hand submerged
D) It is invalid only if the forearm was not submerged

Key Takeaway
Wiping the head with moisture leftover from washing another limb is invalid; only fresh water from the source is valid for head wipe.

Washing the Beard in Wudu: The Correct Hanafi Position and the Absence of Wajib
Discusses conflicting narrations on washing the beard and clarifies the correct position, then explains the absence of wajib in wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on washing the beard in wudu, distinguish between the non-hanging and hanging parts, and explain why wudu has no wajib category.

Core Idea
Washing the entire non-hanging beard is fard in wudu; the hanging part is only sunnah.

Throughout Hanafi fiqh, there is a well-known dispute about what is required of the beard during wudu. Some narrations suggest wiping a quarter, others wiping all, but the relied-upon position is that washing the entire beard is fard. This lesson clarifies the correct ruling and distinguishes between the thick and thin beard, and between the hanging and non-hanging parts. Additionally, it explains why wudu has a category of fard and sunnah but no wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The issue is what is obligatory for the beard in wudu: wiping or washing, and how much of it?
The disagreement is within the Hanafi school, not between madhahib.
2
List the conflicting narrations
There are multiple riwayat: wipe a quarter, wipe all, wipe what contacts the skin, wipe a third, and nothing obligatory.
The author expresses surprise that some later works mention the abandoned views instead of the corrected one.
3
Determine the mufta bihi and its evidence
The correct position (al-sahih) is that washing the entire beard is fard, as stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Zahiriyya. Other narrations are abandoned (marju' anhu).
The evidence: the beard is part of the face, and washing the face is fard.
4
Apply the ruling to different beard types
Distinguish between thick beard (wash the entire non-hanging part), thin beard (water must reach skin), and hanging part (not obligatory but sunnah).
If one shaves after washing, the chin need not be rewashed.
5
Explain why wudu has no wajib category
Fard is established by qat'i evidence, sunnah by zanni evidence. Since no evidence in wudu is zanni thubut qat'i dalalah, there is no wajib. Wudu types: fard (for obligatory prayer), wajib (for tawaf), mandub (for sleep).
The term 'wajib' in the classification is used loosely; technically the obligatory acts are all fard.

The author in al-Kafi: even if another aspect is possible, which is to attach it to 'quarter' to indicate wiping the whole. Here are narrations concerning the obligatory in the beard, with agreement that it is not obligatory to reach water to what is under the beard from the facial skin: it is narrated to wipe a quarter of it, and the author chose it and expressed it in al-Kafi with his words 'and for us'. It is narrated to wipe all of it, it is narrated to wipe what contacts the skin and Qadi Khan authenticated it in his commentary on al-Jami' al-Saghir and followed him in al-Majma', it is narrated to wipe a third, it is narrated that nothing is obligatory. The correct is obligation of washing it in the sense of fard as stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and the fatwa is upon it as in al-Zahiriyya. In al-Bada'i that other than this narration is abandoned. Amazing from the authors of the matns that they mention the abandoned and leave the corrected mufta bihi while it falls within the definition of the face as mentioned in Fath al-Qadir. All this is for the thick beard. As for the thin through which the skin is visible, it is obligatory to reach water to what is under it. All this is for the non-hanging. As for the hanging, it is not obligatory to wash it nor wipe it, but it is mentioned in Munyat al-Musalli that it is sunnah. If one pours water on the beard hair then shaves it, he is not obligated to wash the chin like the head. The apparent meaning of their words is that the beard is the hair that grows on the cheeks from 'idhar and 'arid and the chin. In Sharh al-Irshad: the beard is the hair that grows at the meeting of the jaws, the 'arid is what is between them, and the 'idhar is what is parallel to the ear, connected from above to the temple and from below to the 'arid. When the author finished the fara'id of wudu, he began to explain its sunan indicating that wudu has no wajib because the establishment of the ruling is according to its evidence, and the evidence establishing it is that which is zanni al-thubut qat'i al-dalalah and what is of its status like ahad reports whose explicit meaning is qat'i al-dalalah, and none such exists in wudu. What is in al-Khulasa does not contradict it: wudu is three types: fard which is wudu for fard prayer, funeral prayer, and sajdat al-tilawah; wajib which is wudu for tawaf; mandub which is wudu for sleep and backbiting and lying. The predicate that in the two issues based on this correction, he mentioned the dispute between them incorrectly. He said: (Even if another aspect is possible) I say: another aspect is possible, that it be connected to 'his face' so the meaning is 'and wash his beard' agreeing with the relied-upon narration even if the apparent is opposite, so the wonder is removed. It is possible that he corrects it here even if he chose other in al-Kafi, as occurred for Qadi Khan for he authenticated wiping all of it in his Fatawa, and authenticated wiping what contacts the skin in his commentary on al-Jami' al-Saghir. So reflect, Allah knows best. He said: (All this is for the non-hanging) what is meant by mustarsil is what goes out of the circle of the face and it is the non-contacting because the contacting is what does not go out of the circle of the face, as in Sharh al-Durar wa al-Gharar. He said: (The 'arid is between them and the 'idhar) al-Ramli said: i.e., the hair growing on the cheeks to the protruding bone near the ear is called 'arid and the hair growing on the protruding bone near the ear is called 'idhar. He said: (Like ahad reports whose explicit meaning is qat'i al-dalalah) is an example of his statement 'what is zanni al-thubut qat'i al-dalalah' because what is of its status is its opposite, which is qat'i.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the obligation of washing the beard during wudu, followed by a clarification that wudu has no category of wajib. The author begins by noting different narrations (riwayat) within the Hanafi school about what is required for the beard: some say wipe a quarter, some wipe all, some wipe only what contacts the skin, some wipe a third, and some say nothing is obligatory. Despite these differences, all agree that water does not need to reach the skin under the beard. The correct position (al-sahih) according to the author is that washing the entire beard is fard (obligatory), as stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, and this is the basis for fatwa. The author expresses surprise that some later commentators mention the abandoned views instead of the corrected, relied-upon ruling. Then he distinguishes between a thick beard (kaththa) and a thin beard (khafifa): for a thin beard, water must reach the skin underneath. He also distinguishes between the non-hanging part of the beard (ghayr mustarsil) and the hanging part (mustarsil): the hanging part does not require washing or wiping, but it is sunnah to wash it. If one washes the beard but then shaves it off, he need not rewash the chin, similar to the head. The passage defines the beard: it includes hair on the cheeks (from 'idhar and 'arid) and the chin. After this, the author shifts to explain that wudu has no wajib elements; all obligatory acts are fard (since they are established by qat'i evidence), while sunnah acts are those established by zanni evidence. He gives examples from al-Khulasa of three types of wudu: fard (for obligatory prayer, funeral prayer, and sajdat al-tilawah), wajib (for tawaf), and mandub (for sleep and to expiate backbiting and lying). The super-commentary that follows clarifies a grammatical point in the main text and provides definitions of 'idhar and 'arid, and finally explains that ahad reports with qat'i dalalah are like zanni thubut qat'i dalalah.

Key Terms
اللحية (al-lihya): The beard: hair that grows on the cheeks (from 'idhar and 'arid) and the chin.
العذار (al-'idhar): The hair growing on the protruding bone near the ear.
العارض (al-'arid): The hair growing on the cheeks up to the protruding bone near the ear.
المسترسل (al-mustarsil): The hanging part of the beard that extends beyond the face's circumference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi ruling for washing a thick beard in wudu?
A) It is sufficient to wipe a quarter of the beard.
B) It is obligatory (fard) to wash the entire beard.
C) It is not required to wash or wipe the beard.
D) It is sunnah to wipe the beard but not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Washing the entire non-hanging beard is fard in wudu; the hanging part is sunnah; wudu has no wajib category because its rulings are based on qat'i or zanni evidence.

The Obligation of Washing the Beard in Wudu: Hanafi Ikhtilaf and Rulings
Intra-Hanafi dispute on obligatory amount of beard washing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi Hanafi ruling on washing the beard in wudu, distinguish it from abandoned narrations, and apply the correct ruling to thick vs thin beards.

Core Idea
The beard is part of the face; washing it fully is fard — but only the part within the face boundary, not the hanging length.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute about a common practice: how much of the beard must be washed during wudu? While all Hanafi positions agree on the principle that the beard is part of the face, they differ on the obligatory amount. We will trace the various narrations, identify the relied-upon view, and understand why some earlier matn authors cited weaker positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and common ground
Recognize that the dispute is about the obligatory amount of beard washing, but all agree the beard is part of the face and water must reach the skin under the beard (for thin beards).
2
List the variant narrations
The passage presents five narrations: quarter, all, what touches the skin, third, nothing. Note which author chose which.
These narrations represent differences among early Hanafi authorities.
3
Identify the relied-upon position
The text states that the correct view (al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, al-Zahiriyya) is that washing the beard is fard and the mufta bihi is full washing of the facial beard.
The beard is part of the face, so it falls under the Quranic command to wash the face.
4
Explain the tarjih mechanism
In al-Bada'i, all narrations other than full washing are declared abandoned (marju' 'anhu). The author expresses surprise that some matn authors mention the abandoned view.
This shows the importance of following later tarjih (preference) by later Hanafi masters.
5
Apply the ruling to different beard types
For a thick beard, washing the facial hair suffices; for a thin beard (skin visible), water must reach the skin; the hanging part (mustarsil) is not obligatory but sunnah.
These distinctions are based on textual definitions of 'idhar and 'arid.

There are narrations regarding what is obligatory concerning the beard, while agreeing that water must reach the skin under the beard: some narrate washing a quarter, chosen by the author and expressed in al-Kafi as 'for us'; some narrate washing all of it; some narrate washing what touches the skin, which Qadhi Khan deemed sound in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, followed by the author of al-Majma'; some narrate washing a third; some narrate nothing obligatory. Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about what is obligatory in washing the beard during wudu. The author presents multiple narrations: washing a quarter, washing all, washing what touches the skin, washing a third, and nothing obligatory. The correct and relied-upon view is that washing the beard is fard because it is part of the face, as confirmed in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Zahiriyya. The author notes with astonishment that some matn authors mention abandoned views instead of the sound ones. The ruling differs for a thick beard (full washing required) vs thin beard (skin visible, water must reach skin) and for the hanging part (not obligatory but sunnah). The definitions of 'idhar and 'arid are given. The overall message: the beard is part of the face; washing it is fard, but there is ikhtilaf on the extent, with the correct being full washing of the facial beard.

العذار (al-'idhar): Hair adjacent to the ear, connected above to the temple and below to the 'arid
العارض (al-'arid): Hair between the two jawbones on the cheeks
المسترسل (al-mustarsil): The part of the beard that hangs below the face boundary

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view is that washing the entire beard (hair on the cheeks and chin) is fard in wudu because it is part of the face; the hanging part is sunnah.

Wudu Has No Wajib Component; Defining Sunnah
Statement that wudu has no wajib, with definition of sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that wudu has no wajib component, distinguish between fard, wajib, and sunnah in the context of wudu, and define sunnah technically as per Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Wudu's actions are either fard or sunnah; there is no separate wajib category within wudu.

This passage clarifies a key Hanafi position: while the act of wudu can be wajib (e.g., for tawaf), no individual step within wudu carries a wajib ruling. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly classifying actions in purification. The passage also provides a precise technical definition of sunnah that excludes fard and wajib.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the author's claim
Identify the core assertion: 'wudu has no wajib'. Understand that this is a ruling statement about the components of wudu.
The absence of a wajib category within wudu is a distinctive Hanafi position.
2
Understand the evidentiary basis
The sunan are derived from ahad hadith, which in Hanafi usul are zanni al-thubut but can be qat'i al-dalala. This evidentiary classification supports why they are not fard or wajib.
Only evidence that is qat'i in both thubut and dalala establishes fard.
3
Resolve the contradiction from al-Khulasa
The passage mentions that wudu for tawaf is called 'wajib' in al-Khulasa. This refers to the entire act being wajib, not to a component within wudu. Differentiate between overall obligation and internal category.
This resolution prevents confusion between macro and micro levels of ruling.
4
Define sunnah lexically and technically
Lexically, sunnah means customary way, even bad. Technically, it is refined to mean a path followed in religion without obligation and with continuity. Trace the refinement from al-'Inaya to al-Kashf to the final preferred definition.
The condition of 'without obligation' excludes fard and wajib; 'continuity' excludes occasional practices.
5
Apply the definition to distinguish sunnah from other categories
Use the definition to separate sunnah muakkadah from fard, wajib, and mustahabb. Emphasize that in wudu, only fard and sunnah exist.
This concrete application solidifies the theoretical definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ المصنف من فرائض الوضوء شرع في بيان سننه إشارة إلى أن الوضوء لا واجب فيه لما أن ثبوت الحكم بقدر دليله والدليل المثبت له هو ما كان ظني الثبوت قطعي الدلانة وما كان بمنزلته كأخبار الآجاد التي مفهومها قطعي الدلالة ولم يوجد في الوضوء، ولا ينافيه ما في الخلاصة من الوضوء ثلاثة أنواع : فرض وهو الوضوء لصلاة الفريضة وصلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة، وواجب وهو الوضوء للطواف بالبيت ومندوب وهو الوضوء للنوم وعن الغيبة والكذب وإنشاد الشعر ومن القهقهة والوضوء على الوضوء لغسل الميت .اهـ لأن هذا حكم على نفس الوضوء بأنه واجب لا أن فيه واجباً وظاهر تقييده بصلاة الفريضة أن الوضوء للنافلة ليس بفرض وإن كان شرطاً، والظاهر أنه فرض عند إرادتها الجازمة كما سبق تقريره في بيان السبب، ومراده من الوضوء للنوم الوضوء عند إرادة النوم فإنه مستحب، وأما الوضوء من النوم الناقض ففرض .
من
قوله: (وسنته) أي الوضوء. هي لغة الطريقة المعتادة ولو سيئة، واصطلاحاً الطريقة المسلوكة في الدين كذا في العناية وفيه نظر لشموله الفرض والواجب فزاد في الكشف غير افتراض ولا وجوب وفيه نظر لشموله المستحب والمندوب، فالأولى أن يقال : هي الطريقة المسلوكة في الدين من غير لزوم على سبيل المواظبة ليخرج غير المحدود. وما في غاية الثبوت ظني الدليل وأخبار الآحاد ليس كذلك تأمل.
When the author finished the fara'id of wudu, he began explaining its sunan, indicating that wudu has no wajib because the ruling's establishment is according to its evidence, and the evidence that establishes it is that which is zanni al-thubut but qat'i al-dalala, and what is similar to it such as ahad hadith whose meaning is qat'i al-dalala, and this is not found in wudu. This is not contradicted by what is in al-Khulasa that wudu is three types: fard—wudu for obligatory prayer, funeral prayer, and prostration of recitation; wajib—wudu for circumambulation of the Kaaba; and mandub—wudu for sleep, from backbiting, lying, reciting poetry, from laughing, and wudu on wudu for washing the deceased—because this is a ruling on the wudu itself being wajib, not that there is a wajib in it. The apparent restriction to obligatory prayer indicates that wudu for supererogatory prayer is not fard, although it is a condition, and apparently it becomes fard when one firmly intends it as established earlier. What is meant by wudu for sleep is wudu when intending to sleep, for it is mustahabb; as for wudu from sleep that invalidates, it is fard. From [the author's words]: 'And its sunnah' i.e., of wudu. Lexically, sunnah is the customary way, even if bad; technically, it is the path followed in religion. Thus in al-'Inaya, but there is a consideration: it includes fard and wajib, so in al-Kashf they added 'without being fard or wajib', but that also includes mustahabb and mandub. So it is better to say: it is the path followed in religion without obligation, in the manner of continuity, to exclude what is not defined. And what is in Ghayat al-Thubut is zanni al-dalil, and ahad hadith are not like that—reflect.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement and definition. The author establishes that in wudu, there is no wajib category; actions are either fard or sunnah. He explains that the evidence for sunan comes from ahad hadith which, in Hanafi usul, are zanni al-thubut but their meaning may be qat'i. The apparent contradiction from al-Khulasa is resolved: when it calls wudu for tawaf 'wajib', it means the entire act of wudu is wajib in that context, not that there is a wajib component within wudu. The definition of sunnah is refined lexically and technically, concluding that sunnah is a path followed in religion without obligation and with continuity, excluding fard and wajib. The final sentence notes that reports in Ghayat al-Thubut are zanni, whereas ahad hadith are not to be considered qat'i.

Key Terms
السنة (al-sunnah): The path followed in religion without obligation and with continuity, excluding fard and wajib.
الواجب (al-wajib): In the context of wudu, there is no component level of wajib; only the act as a whole can be wajib (as in wudu for tawaf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the technical definition of 'sunnah' according to this passage?
A) A path followed in religion that is obligatory.
B) Any habitual practice, good or bad.
C) A path followed in religion without obligation and with continuity.
D) A recommended action that is not fard but becomes wajib if vowed.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, wudu has no wajib component; the sunan are defined as non-obligatory practices based on zanni evidence and performed with continuity.

Wudu from Reciting Poetry: Types and Rulings
Ruling on wudu from reciting poetry with classification and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of poetry according to Hanafi fiqh and state the ruling on performing wudu after each type.

Core Idea
Wudu is recommended (mustahabb) after reciting blameworthy poetry (hija) or poetry intended for amusement, but not after good poetry.

This passage, from the commentary of 'Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi, addresses an often-overlooked aspect of taharah: the spiritual impurity that may result from reciting certain types of poetry. The author classifies poetry into three categories—permissible, rewarded, and prohibited—and explains when wudu becomes recommended. The key factors are the content of the poetry and the intention behind it. Understanding this ruling helps refine one's practice of taharah and awareness of spiritual states.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of poetry
According to Nabulsi, poetry is divided into: (1) good poetry praising Allah's creation, (2) blameworthy poetry (hija) that causes aversion, and (3) poetry intended for amusement or heedlessness.
The text also mentions a permitted type with no specific intention.
2
Determine the hukm for each type
For hija (blameworthy poetry), wudu is recommended (mustahabb). For poetry intended for amusement, it is also recommended. For good poetry with a good intention, wudu is not required.
The text explicitly says 'فَيُسْتَحَبُّ الْوُضُوءُ مِنْهُ' for hija.
3
Locate the dalil for the recommendation
The proof includes the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' and the Quranic verse 'almost its oil would shine' (24:35), which shows that hyperbole in good poetry is not considered lying.
The hadith is cited to show that intention matters.
4
Analyze the role of intention
Wudu is recommended only when the poetry is recited with a blameworthy intention (amusement, backbiting, or falsehood). Good intention eliminates the need for wudu.
The passage says: 'فَإِنْ أَرَادَ بِذَلِكَ اللهوَ وَالْغَفْلَةَ... فَهُوَ مَنْهِيٌّ عَنْهُ أَيْضًا'
5
Note the practical application
If one recites or composes poetry that is satirical or purely for fun, it is recommended to perform wudu before prayer to remove spiritual impurity. Good poetry does not require wudu.
This is a specific ruling not found in all madhhabs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإنشاد الشعر) قال سيدي العارف بالله تعالى عبد الغني النابلسي في شرحه على هدية ابن العماد : اعلم أن الشعر ثلاثة أنواع : مباح ومثاب عليه ومنهي عنه لأنه لا يخلو إما أن يكون مشتملاً على أوصاف المخلوقات الحسنة كالإنسان والحيوان والنباتات والمعادن ونحو ذلك، أو على الأوصاف القبيحة في الإنسان ونحوه وهو المسمى بالهجو وهو ما ينفر قلب الرجل عن أخيه المسلم وهو المنهي عنه فإن كان ذلك صدقاً فقد دخل في الغيبة وإن كان كذباً فقد دخل في الكذب فيستحب الوضوء منه، وأما إذا كان مشتملاً على الأوصاف الحسنة كذكر إنسان معين أو غير معين أو ذكر زهر أو روض معين أو غير معين فذلك دائر مع القصد والإرادة، فإن أراد بذلك اللهو والغفلة والغرور بزخارف الدنيا ولذائذها فهو منهي عنه أيضاً. قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام كل لهو ابن آدم حرام الحديث. وقد مدح ما لا يستوجب المدح وهو عرض الدنيا القبيح المنتن فقد أصابته بسبب ذلك نجاسة معنوية فيستجب له إعادة الوضوء بإنشاد ذلك على هذا الوجه المذكور وأما إن أراد بما ذكرنا بيان صنعة الله تعالى وعظيم حكمته وعجيب ما أظهرته قدرته على صفحات الأكوان من بدائع المخلوقات وغرائب المصنوعات فله إرادته ونيته . قال صلى الله عليه وسلم «إنما الأعمال بالنيات وإنما لكل امرئ ما نوى وهذا النوع من الشعر مثاب عليه ... وأما المباح فهو أن لا يقصد شيئاً مما ذكرنا فظهر بذلك أن الشعر بمنزلة الكلام فحسنه حسن وقبيحه قبيح. ولا تعد الاستعارات فيه ولا التشابيه ولا المبالغات من قبيل الكذب بعد أن يكون على حسب التفصيل الذي ذكرناه، وأحسن المبالغات ما فيه شيء من أفعال المقاربة قال الله تعالى يكاد زيتها يضيء ولو لم تمسسه نار [النور: ٣٥] وقد ورد في مدح الشعر ما لا مزيد عليه من الأخبار وكذلك في ذمه اهـ وتمامه فيه. قوله ومراده من الوضوء للنوم الوضوء عند إرادة النوم هذا الذي يتبادر، ويحتمل أن يراد الوضوء الاستيقاظه منه فيكون على تقدير مضاف، وكل منهما صحيح إذ الوضوء للنوم على طهارة والوضوء إذا استيقظ منه ليكون مبادراً للطهارة وأداء العبادة كما صرح به الشرنبلالي. وأما منع الشارح ذلك فغير مسلم إذ الوضوء الفرض إنما هو للصلاة أي إذا أرادها ولم
His saying: 'and from reciting poetry'. My master, the knower of Allah, 'Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi said in his commentary on Hidayat Ibn al-'Imad: Know that poetry is of three types: permitted, rewarded, and prohibited. It inevitably either contains descriptions of beautiful created things like humans, animals, plants, minerals etc., or contains ugly descriptions of humans etc., which is called hija (satire), which causes aversion between Muslims—this is prohibited. If it is true, it becomes backbiting; if false, it becomes lying. Wudu is recommended from it. As for poetry containing good descriptions, like mentioning a specific or unspecified person, flower, or garden, this depends on intention. If one intends amusement and heedlessness, it is also prohibited. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Every amusement of the son of Adam is haram...' And if one praises what does not deserve praise, i.e., the ugly and foul worldly goods, then he is affected by a spiritual impurity and so wudu is recommended for him due to that recitation. But if he intends to show the craftsmanship of Allah and His wisdom and power in the wonders of creation, then he has his intention and reward. The Prophet said: 'Actions are by intentions...' This type of poetry is rewarded. As for the permissible, it is when one intends nothing of the above. Thus poetry is like speech: its good is good, its bad is bad. Figures of speech, similes, and hyperboles are not considered lies if within the above detail. The best hyperboles are those containing verbs of approximation, as Allah says: 'almost its oil would shine even if untouched by fire' (24:35). There are many reports praising poetry and also condemning it. The commentary completes there. His saying: 'And what is meant by wudu for sleep is wudu when intending to sleep'—this is what is apparent. It is also possible that what is intended is wudu after waking up, implying an omitted word. Both are valid: wudu before sleep is for purity, and wudu after waking is to hasten to purity and worship, as stated by al-Sharnblali. As for the commentator's objection, it is not accepted, for the obligatory wudu is only for prayer, i.e., when one intends it, and...
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning wudu from reciting poetry. The author quotes 'Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi's classification of poetry into three types: permissible (mubah), rewarded (for praising Allah's creation), and prohibited (hija/backbiting). Wudu is recommended (mustahabb) after reciting prohibited poetry or poetry intended for amusement, as it incurs a spiritual impurity. For good poetry, wudu is not needed unless one intends worldly amusement. The proof includes the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' and Quranic verse on hyperbole. The passage also clarifies the meaning of 'wudu for sleep'—whether before sleep or after waking—both are valid, with the first being mustahabb for purity and the second to be ready for worship. The final sentence addresses an objection: obligatory wudu is only for prayer when one intends it.

Key Terms
إنشاد الشعر (inshad al-shi'r): Reciting or composing poetry
الهجو (al-hija): Satire or defamatory poetry that causes aversion between Muslims
النجاسة المعنوية (al-najasa al-ma'nawiyya): Spiritual impurity that warrants recommended wudu

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of poetry is recommended to perform wudu after reciting it according to Nabulsi?
A) Poetry praising Allah's creation with good intention.
B) Satirical poetry (hija) that causes aversion.
C) Permissible poetry with no specific intention.
D) All types of poetry require wudu.

Key Takeaway
Wudu is mustahabb after reciting prohibited poetry (hija) or poetry intended for amusement; good poetry with right intention does not require wudu.

The Sunnah of Wudu: Definition, Types, and Rulings
Commentary defining sunnah and applying it to specific acts (poetry, sleep).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sunnah, identify the categories of poetry and their effect on wudu, and distinguish between emphasized and non-emphasized sunnah.

Core Idea
Sunnah is the Prophet's continuous practice that companions never objected to when omitted — not every persistent act is wajib.

The concept of sunnah in wudu is central to understanding recommended acts that carry reward but no punishment for omission. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definition of sunnah, explores its application to specific acts like reciting poetry, and resolves apparent contradictions in the sources.

Guided Path
1
Define sunnah linguistically and technically
Identify the lexical meaning (customary way) and the terminological definition from the commentary (path followed in religion without obligation).
The definition excludes fard and wajib but includes mustahabb and mandub.
2
Identify the key criterion: muwazabah and absence of objection
The Prophet's continuous practice (muwazabah) combined with the companions' silence (sukoot) towards those who omit it distinguishes sunnah from wajib.
If objection had occurred, it would indicate obligation.
3
Understand the two types of sunnah
Emphasized sunnah (mu'akkadah) involves continuous practice without any omission; non-emphasized sunnah (ghayr mu'akkadah) involves occasional omission without excuse.
This resolution reconciles the apparent conflict between al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir.
4
Apply the framework to poetry recitation
Poetry is classified into three types: prohibited (satire/heedless), rewarded (reflecting divine wisdom), and permissible (neutral). Wudu is recommended after prohibited or heedless poetry due to spiritual impurity.
The ruling is based on intention (niyyah) as per the hadith.
5
Recognise the grammatical significance
The plural form 'sunan' in the matn indicates that omitting one sunnah does not invalidate the act, unlike fard where omission of one nullifies the whole.
This underscores the non-essential nature of sunan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنشاد الشعر ومن القهقهة والوضوء على الوضوء لغسل الميت .اهـ لأن هذا حكم على نفس الوضوء بأنه واجب لا أن فيه واجباً وظاهر تقييده بصلاة الفريضة أن الوضوء للنافلة ليس بفرض وإن كان شرطاً، والظاهر أنه فرض عند إرادتها الجازمة كما سبق تقريره في بيان السبب، ومراده من الوضوء للنوم الوضوء عند إرادة النوم فإنه مستحب، وأما الوضوء من النوم الناقض ففرض . قوله: (وسنته) أي الوضوء. هي لغة الطريقة المعتادة ولو سيئة، واصطلاحاً الطريقة المسلوكة في الدين كذا في العناية وفيه نظر لشموله الفرض والواجب فزاد في الكشف غير افتراض ولا وجوب وفيه نظر لشموله المستحب والمندوب، فالأولى أن يقال : هي الطريقة المسلوكة في الدين من غير لزوم على سبيل المواظبة ليخرج غير المحدود. وما في غاية الثبوت ظني الدليل وأخبار الآحاد ليس كذلك تأمل. قوله : (وإنشاد الشعر) قال سيدي العارف بالله تعالى عبد الغني النابلسي في شرحه على هدية ابن العماد : اعلم أن الشعر ثلاثة أنواع : مباح ومثاب عليه ومنهي عنه لأنه لا يخلو إما أن يكون مشتملاً على أوصاف المخلوقات الحسنة كالإنسان والحيوان والنباتات والمعادن ونحو ذلك، أو على الأوصاف القبيحة في الإنسان ونحوه وهو المسمى بالهجو وهو ما ينفر قلب الرجل عن أخيه المسلم وهو المنهي عنه فإن كان ذلك صدقاً فقد دخل في الغيبة وإن كان كذباً فقد دخل في الكذب فيستحب الوضوء منه، وأما إذا كان مشتملاً على الأوصاف الحسنة كذكر إنسان معين أو غير معين أو ذكر زهر أو روض معين أو غير معين فذلك دائر مع القصد والإرادة، فإن أراد بذلك اللهو والغفلة والغرور بزخارف الدنيا ولذائذها فهو منهي عنه أيضاً. قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام كل لهو ابن آدم حرام الحديث. وقد مدح ما لا يستوجب المدح وهو عرض الدنيا القبيح المنتن فقد أصابته بسبب ذلك نجاسة معنوية فيستجب له إعادة الوضوء بإنشاد ذلك على هذا الوجه المذكور وأما إن أراد بما ذكرنا بيان صنعة الله تعالى وعظيم حكمته وعجيب ما أظهرته قدرته على صفحات الأكوان من بدائع المخلوقات وغرائب المصنوعات فله إرادته ونيته . قال صلى الله عليه وسلم «إنما الأعمال بالنيات وإنما لكل امرئ ما نوى وهذا النوع من الشعر مثاب عليه ... وأما المباح فهو أن لا يقصد شيئاً مما ذكرنا فظهر بذلك أن الشعر بمنزلة الكلام فحسنه حسن وقبيحه قبيح. ولا تعد الاستعارات فيه ولا التشابيه ولا المبالغات من قبيل الكذب بعد أن يكون على حسب التفصيل الذي ذكرناه، وأحسن المبالغات ما فيه شيء من أفعال المقاربة قال الله تعالى يكاد زيتها يضيء ولو لم تمسسه نار [النور: ٣٥] وقد ورد في مدح الشعر ما لا مزيد عليه من الأخبار وكذلك في ذمه اهـ وتمامه فيه. قوله ومراده من الوضوء للنوم الوضوء عند إرادة النوم هذا الذي يتبادر، ويحتمل أن يراد الوضوء الاستيقاظه منه فيكون على تقدير مضاف، وكل منهما صحيح إذ الوضوء للنوم على طهارة والوضوء إذا استيقظ منه ليكون مبادراً للطهارة وأداء العبادة كما صرح به يندب الشرنبلالي. وأما منع الشارح ذلك فغير مسلم إذ الوضوء الفرض إنما هو للصلاة أي إذا أرادها ولم يكن متوضئاً. بيان من أنها ما في فعله ثواب وفي تركه عتاب لا عقاب فهو تعريف بالحكم، وما في شرح النقاية من أنها ما ثبت بقوله أو فعله وليس بواجب ولا مستحب ففيه نظر لشموله المباح، وما في فتح القدير وغيره من أنها ما واظب النبي الله عليه مع الترك أحياناً فمنتقض بالفرض فإن القيام في الصلاة مثلاً حصلت المواظبة عليه مع الترك أحياناً لعذر المرض، فلذا زاد في التحرير أن يكون الترك أحياناً بلا عذر ليلزم كونه بلا وجوب، وظاهره أن المواظبة بلا ترك أصلاً لا تفيد السنية بل الوجوب، وظاهر الهداية يخالفه فإنه في الاستدلال على سنية المضمضة والاستنشاق . قال لأنه عليه السلام فعلهما على المواظبة، وكذا استدلالهم على سنية الاعتكاف في العشر الأخير من رمضان بأنه عليه السلام واظب على الاعتكاف في العشر الأخير من رمضان حتى توفاه الله تعالى كما في الصحيحين، يفيد أنها تفيد السنية مطلقاً، ولذا قال في فتح القدير: فهذه المواظبة المقرونة بعدم الترك مرة لما اقترنت بعدم الإنكار على من لم يفعله من الصحابة كانت دليل السنية وإلا كانت تكون دليل الوجوب انتهى. والذي ظهر للعبد الضعيف أن السنة ما واظب النبي الله لكن إن كانت لا مع الترك. فهي دليل السنة المؤكدة وإن كانت مع الترك أحياناً فهي دليل غير المؤكدة، وإن اقترنت بالإنكار على من لم يفعله فهي دليل الوجوب فافهم هذا فإن به يحصل التوفيق. وفي بعض النسخ «وسننه بالجمع ونكته جمعها وأفراد الفرض الإشارة إلى أن الفروض وإن كثرت في حكم شيء واحد بدليل فساد البعض بترك البعض بخلاف السنن إذ لا يبطل بعضها بترك بعضها. والإضافة هنا بمعنى اللام كما لا يخفى وجعلها المصنف في المستصفى من إضافة الشيء إلى محله لأن الطهارة محل لهذه السنن، وفي النهاية أنها بمعنى «من» وفيه ما تقدم في كتاب الطهارة .
And reciting poetry, and from guffawing, and wudu on wudu for washing the deceased. End quote. Because this is a ruling on wudu itself that it is obligatory, not that there is an obligatory part in it. The apparent restriction to obligatory prayer indicates that wudu for supererogatory prayer is not fard even if it is a condition. Apparently it is fard at the time of decisive intention to pray as previously established in the explanation of the cause. And his meaning of wudu for sleep is wudu at the time of intending sleep, for it is recommended. As for wudu from sleep that nullifies wudu, it is fard. His statement: (and its sunnah) i.e., of wudu. Linguistically it is the customary way, even if bad. Terminologically, it is the path followed in religion. Thus in al-Inaya, and there is consideration in it because it includes fard and wajib. So he added in al-Kashf: 'other than fard and wajib', and there is consideration in it because it includes mustahabb and mandub. So it is better to say: it is the path followed in religion without obligation, in the manner of continuous practice, to exclude what is not delimited. And what is in Ghayat al-Thubut is zanni al-dalil, and ahad hadith are not like that. Reflect. His statement: (and reciting poetry). Sayyidi al-Arif billah Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi said in his commentary on Hadiyyat Ibn al-Imad: Know that poetry is three types: permissible, rewarded, and prohibited. Because it is either comprised of descriptions of good attributes of creatures like humans, animals, plants, minerals and the like, or of bad attributes in humans and similar, which is called satire, which repels a man's heart from his Muslim brother, and it is prohibited. If it is true, it falls into backbiting; if it is false, it falls into lying; so wudu from it is recommended. As for if it comprises good attributes like mentioning a specific or non-specific person, or a specific or non-specific flower or garden, then it revolves around intention and will. If by that he intends amusement, heedlessness, and delusion with the embellishments and pleasures of the world, then it is also prohibited. The Prophet said: 'Every amusement of the son of Adam is unlawful.' Hadith. And he praised what does not deserve praise, which is the ugly and foul worldly display; he is affected by that with a spiritual impurity, so it is recommended for him to renew wudu upon reciting it in the mentioned manner. But if by what we mentioned he intends to show the craft of Allah, His great wisdom, and the wonders that His power has manifested on the pages of the cosmos from amazing creations and strange artifacts, then he has his intention and his purpose. The Prophet said: 'Actions are by intentions, and each man gets what he intends.' This type of poetry is rewarded. As for the permissible, it is that he intends nothing of what we mentioned. So it becomes clear that poetry is like speech: its good is good, its bad is bad. Metaphors, similes, and exaggerations are not considered lies according to the detail we mentioned. The best exaggerations are those that contain something from the verbs of approximation. Allah said: 'Its oil would almost glow, even if no fire touched it.' [Quran 24:35]. There are numerous reports praising poetry, and likewise dispraising it. End quote, and its completion is in it. His statement: and his meaning of wudu for sleep is wudu at the time of intending sleep, this is what comes to mind. It is possible that he means wudu upon waking from it, so it would be on an implied addition. Both are correct, since wudu for sleep is upon purity, and wudu after waking is to hasten to purity and performance of worship as stated by al-Shurunbulali who deemed it recommended. As for the commentator's prohibition of that, it is not accepted, for obligatory wudu is only for prayer, i.e., when he intends it and is not in a state of wudu. An explanation that it (the sunnah) is something in whose doing is reward and in whose leaving is blame not punishment, so it is a definition by the ruling. What is in Sharh al-Nuqaya that it is something established by his statement or action and is not obligatory nor recommended, has consideration because it includes the permissible. What is in Fath al-Qadir and others that it is what the Prophet persisted in with occasional omission is refuted by the fard, because standing in prayer, for example, had persistence with occasional omission due to illness. So he added in al-Tahrir that the omission should be occasionally without excuse to necessitate it being without obligation. Its apparent meaning is that persistence without any omission does not indicate sunnah but obligation. The apparent meaning of al-Hidaya contradicts it, because in proving the sunnah of rinsing the mouth and sniffing water, he said: 'Because he did them persistently.' Likewise their proof for the sunnah of i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan that he persisted in i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan until Allah took him, as in the two Sahihs, indicates that it gives sunnah absolutely. Therefore Fath al-Qadir said: 'Thus this persistence coupled with never omitting, when coupled with no objection from the companions to one who does not do it, was evidence of sunnah; otherwise it would be evidence of obligation.' What appears to this weak servant is that sunnah is what the Prophet persisted in, but if it is without omission, it is evidence of emphasized sunnah; if with occasional omission, it is evidence of non-emphasized sunnah; if coupled with objection to one who does not do it, it is evidence of obligation. Understand this, for by it harmony is achieved. In some copies: 'and its sunnans in the plural, and he mentioned them in plural and singled out fard to indicate that the fards, though many, are in the rule of one thing due to the invalidation of some by omitting some, unlike the sunans, for some do not invalidate others by omitting them. The addition here is in the sense of 'lam' as is clear. And the author in al-Mustasfa made it an addition of the thing to its place because purification is the place for these sunans. In al-Nihaya it is in the sense of 'min', and there is what preceded in the chapter of purification.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the sunan (plural of sunnah) of wudu from the matn Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author, Ibn Nujaym, begins by clarifying that the ruling mentioned earlier—that wudu for certain acts is obligatory—refers to the obligation of wudu itself for obligatory prayer, not that it contains an obligatory element. He distinguishes between wudu for obligatory prayer (which is fard when intending it), for supererogatory prayer (not fard but a condition), and for sleep (recommended). He then defines sunnah both linguistically and technically, critiquing various definitions from other works. The sunnah is best defined as a path followed in religion without obligation but with continuous practice (muwazabah). He discusses the proof for sunnah being based on the Prophet's persistent practice without objection from companions, and resolves apparent contradictions between al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir by distinguishing between emphasized and non-emphasized sunnah. The passage then addresses specific sunan: reciting poetry and its effect on wudu. Quoting Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi, he explains that poetry is of three types—permissible, rewarded, and prohibited—and that wudu is recommended after reciting prohibited or heedless poetry due to the spiritual impurity it causes. The commentary also covers wudu for sleep, affirming it is recommended. Finally, he examines the grammatical construction of 'sunnah' in the matn, noting that the plural form indicates that omitting one sunnah does not invalidate the act, unlike fard.

Key Terms
السنة (al-sunnah): A path followed in religion without obligation, based on the Prophet's continuous practice without the companions objecting to its omission.
المواظبة (al-muwazabah): Continuous practice by the Prophet without abandoning it except rarely and without excuse.
الهجو (al-hijā'): Satirical poetry that repels a man from his Muslim brother, considered prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a sunnah from a wajib in Hanafi fiqh?
A) The Prophet's continuous practice without any omission
B) The Prophet's continuous practice coupled with the companions' lack of objection to those who omit it
C) The presence of a clear command in the Quran
D) The act being mentioned in the matn

Key Takeaway
Sunnah in wudu is defined by the Prophet's continuous practice without companion objection; it brings reward when done and is not blameworthy when omitted.

Washing Hands at Start of Wudu: Three Hanafi Opinions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on a wudu detail

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on washing hands to the wrists at the beginning of wudu and identify the mufta bihi position with its supporting evidence.

Core Idea
Washing hands to wrists at wudu's start is sunnah, though a minority view considers it fard.

The opening step of wudu—washing the hands to the wrists—is a detail where Hanafi scholars have three views. The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) clearly labels it sunnah, but earlier and later works debate whether it is fard or a non-emphasized sunnah. This lesson unpacks the three opinions and the evidence behind each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Matn's Ruling
The passage begins by citing the matn: 'washing his hands up to his wrists initially' and states it is sunnah. Note that the matn's authority is high—this is the core text being explained.
The matn does not use 'fard' or 'wajib'.
2
List the Three Opinions
The passage explicitly lists three opinions: 1) Washing is fard, doing it first is sunnah; 2) Sunnah ghayr mu'akkadah; 3) Sunnah mu'akkadah. Write them down in order.
The first opinion is supported by later Hanafi works like Fath al-Qadir.
3
Examine the Evidence for the Fard View
The passage cites Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Asl: after washing the face, one washes the forearms, not the hands. This implies the hands were already washed, so washing them initially is not a repetition—suggesting it is fard (since the Qurnic command is satisfied).
This is the strongest textual evidence for the first opinion.
4
Understand the Sunnah Position
The matn itself says it is sunnah. The other two opinions differ on emphasis. The mufta bihi (relied-upon view) is sunnah mu'akkadah, as the matn is the standard text.
The default in Hanafi works is to follow the matn unless strong evidence overrides.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence
If someone omits washing hands initially, wudu is still valid (no sajdah al-sahw because it's sunnah, not wajib). If it were fard, the wudu would be invalid.
This thamarah al-khilaf matters for daily practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (غسل يديه إلى رسغيه ابتداء يعني غسل اليدين ثلاثاً إلى رسغيه في ابتداء الوضوء سنة. والرسغ منتهى الكف عند المفصل. وفي ضياء الحلوم الرسغ بالغين المعجمة الكف في الذراع والقدم في الساق. اعلم أن في غسل اليدين ابتداء ثلاثة أقوال : قيل إنه فرض وتقديمه سنة واختاره في فتح القدير والمعراج والخبازية وإليه يشير قول محمد في الأصل بعد غسل الوجه ثم يغسل ذراعيه ولم يقل يديه فلا يجب غسلهما ثانياً. وقيل إنه سنة غير مؤكدة وقيل إنه من السنن المؤكدة.
His statement: (washing his hands up to his wrists initially) means washing the hands three times up to the wrists at the beginning of wudu is sunnah. The wrist (rusgh) is the end of the palm at the joint. In Diya' al-Huloom, the rusgh (with the dotted ghayn) is the palm in the forearm and the foot in the leg. Know that regarding washing the hands initially there are three statements: It is said that it is fard and its performance first is sunnah, and this was chosen in Fath al-Qadir, al-Mi'raj, and al-Khabbaziyyah. To it points the statement of Muhammad in al-Asl: after washing the face, then he washes his forearms, and he did not say 'his hands', so it is not obligatory to wash them a second time. And it is said that it is a non-emphasized sunnah. And it is said that it is from the emphasized sunnah.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage deals with the specific ruling of washing the hands to the wrists at the beginning of wudu. The author states that the matn declares it sunnah. He defines the wrist (rusgh) as the joint at the end of the palm. He then presents a three-opinion intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The first opinion holds that washing the hands initially is fard (obligatory), but performing it at the beginning (before the face) is sunnah. This is the view chosen by some later Hanafi works such as Fath al-Qadir, al-Mi'raj, and al-Khabbaziyyah. It is also supported by Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Asl that after washing the face, one washes the forearms, not the hands, implying that the hands were already washed and need not be washed again. The second opinion says it is a non-emphasized sunnah (ghayr mu'akkadah). The third says it is an emphasized sunnah (mu'akkadah). The author does not explicitly give tarjih here, but the matn itself states it is sunnah, and the default is that it is sunnah mu'akkadah unless evidence suggests otherwise. The passage illustrates how Hanafi scholars can differ on the precise classification of an act that is not explicitly in the Quran or mutawatir hadith.

Key Terms
الرسغ (al-rusgh): The wrist joint, the end of the palm where it meets the forearm.
غسل اليدين ابتداء (ghusl al-yadayn ibtida'): Washing the hands up to the wrists at the very beginning of wudu, before rinsing the mouth or washing the face.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling on washing the hands to the wrists at the beginning of wudu?
A) It is fard (obligatory)
B) It is sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized recommended)
C) It is sunnah ghayr mu'akkadah (non-emphasized recommended)
D) It is wajib (necessary)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that washing hands to wrists at the start of wudu is sunnah mu'akkadah, not fard, despite a minority view.

Defining Sunnah in Hanafi Fiqh: Levels and Legal Implications
Definition and levels of sunnah in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sunnah, distinguish between emphatic and non-emphatic sunnah, and apply the criteria to identify whether an action is sunnah or wajib.

Core Idea
Sunnah is defined by the Prophet's persistent practice and the absence of rebuke for omission.

The term 'sunnah' is central to Islamic jurisprudence, yet its precise definition varies. In the Hanafi school, critical distinctions determine whether an act is emphatically recommended (sunnah mu'akkadah) or lightly so (sunnah ghayr mu'akkadah), or even obligatory. This lesson dissects the Hanafi definition as presented by al-Bahr al-Raiq, clarifying how the Prophet's practice and the absence of rebuke shape legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act as sunnah or wajib
Check if the Prophet performed the act persistently and whether he ever rebuked those who omitted it. If he rebuked omission, the act is wajib; otherwise, it falls into the sunnah category.
Rebuke includes explicit scolding or strong disapproval.
2
Determine frequency of omission
If the Prophet never omitted the act, it is a candidate for emphatic sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah). If he sometimes omitted it (without excuse), it is non-emphatic (ghayr mu'akkadah).
Omission due to illness or travel does not affect the classification.
3
Apply the Hanafi reconciliation
According to the author, persistence without any omission indicates sunnah mu'akkadah, persistence with occasional omission indicates ghayr mu'akkadah, and persistence with rebuke of omission indicates obligation.
This resolves apparent contradictions between earlier Hanafi texts.
4
Understand implications for practice
Emphatic sunnah carries a stronger recommendation; omission is makruh tahrimi. Non-emphatic sunnah omission is makruh tanzih. Neither invalidates the act if omitted, unlike wajib.
Reward is greater for performing sunnah mu'akkadah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
البيان من أنها ما في فعله ثواب وفي تركه عتاب لا عقاب فهو تعريف بالحكم، وما في شرح النقاية من أنها ما ثبت بقوله أو فعله وليس بواجب ولا مستحب ففيه نظر لشموله المباح، وما في فتح القدير وغيره من أنها ما واظب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مع الترك أحياناً فمنتقض بالفرض فإن القيام في الصلاة مثلاً حصلت المواظبة عليه مع الترك أحياناً لعذر المرض، فلذا زاد في التحرير أن يكون الترك أحياناً بلا عذر ليلزم كونه بلا وجوب، وظاهره أن المواظبة بلا ترك أصلاً لا تفيد السنية بل الوجوب، وظاهر الهداية يخالفه فإنه في الاستدلال على سنية المضمضة والاستنشاق قال لأنه عليه السلام فعلهما على المواظبة، وكذا استدلالهم على سنية الاعتكاف في العشر الأخير من رمضان بأنه عليه السلام واظب على الاعتكاف في العشر الأخير من رمضان حتى توفاه الله تعالى كما في الصحيحين، يفيد أنها تفيد السنية مطلقاً، ولذا قال في فتح القدير: فهذه المواظبة المقرونة بعدم الترك مرة لما اقترنت بعدم الإنكار على من لم يفعله من الصحابة كانت دليل السنية وإلا كانت تكون دليل الوجوب انتهى. والذي ظهر للعبد الضعيف أن السنة ما واظب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لكن إن كانت لا مع الترك فهي دليل السنة المؤكدة وإن كانت مع الترك أحياناً فهي دليل غير المؤكدة، وإن اقترنت بالإنكار على من لم يفعله فهي دليل الوجوب فافهم هذا فإن به يحصل التوفيق. وفي بعض النسخ «وسننه بالجمع ونكته جمعها وأفراد الفرض الإشارة إلى أن الفروض وإن كثرت في حكم شيء واحد بدليل فساد البعض بترك البعض بخلاف السنن إذ لا يبطل بعضها بترك بعضها. والإضافة هنا بمعنى اللام كما لا يخفى وجعلها المصنف في المستصفى من إضافة الشيء إلى محله لأن الطهارة محل لهذه السنن، وفي النهاية أنها بمعنى «من» وفيه ما تقدم في كتاب الطهارة .
موصل
The Book of Taharah: Al-Bayan [defines sunnah] as something whose performance brings reward and whose omission brings reproach without punishment—this is a definition by [its] ruling. What is in Sharh al-Niqayah that it is something established by his statement or action, and is not obligatory nor recommended, has a problem because it includes the permissible. And what is in Fath al-Qadir and others that it is what the Prophet (peace be upon him) persisted in while sometimes leaving it, is refuted by the obligatory, for standing in prayer, for example, was persisted in while sometimes leaving it due to the excuse of illness. Therefore, in al-Tahrir, he added that the leaving should be sometimes without excuse so that it necessarily be without obligation. Its apparent meaning is that persistence without any leaving does not indicate sunnah but obligation. The apparent meaning of al-Hidayah contradicts this, for in its deduction of the sunnah of rinsing the mouth and sniffing water, it said: 'Because he (peace be upon him) did them persistently.' Similarly, their deduction of the sunnah of i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan: 'Because he (peace be upon him) persisted in i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan until Allah took him,' as in the two Sahihs, indicates that it indicates sunnah absolutely. Therefore, it was said in Fath al-Qadir: 'This persistence, accompanied by not leaving once, when coupled with the fact that he did not rebuke those who did not do it among the Companions, was evidence for sunnah; otherwise it would have been evidence for obligation.' End. What appears to this weak servant is that sunnah is what the Prophet (peace be upon him) persisted in, but if it is without leaving, it is evidence for emphatic sunnah; if it is with leaving sometimes, it is evidence for non-emphatic sunnah; and if it is accompanied by rebuke of the one who does not do it, it is evidence for obligation. Understand this, for through it reconciliation is achieved. In some copies: 'And its sunan (plural), and the point of putting it in the plural and singling out the obligatory is to indicate that the obligatory acts, though many, are like a single thing, as proven by the invalidation of one part by omitting another, unlike the sunan, since omitting some does not nullify others. The genitive construction here is in the sense of lam, as is not hidden. The author in al-Mustasfa made it the genitive of a thing to its place because taharah is the place for these sunan. In al-Nihayah, it is in the sense of 'min'. And there is what preceded in the Book of Taharah. Mawsil.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq defines the term 'sunnah' as used in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by citing al-Bayan's definition: an action whose performance brings reward and whose omission brings blame but not punishment. Then it critiques the definition in Sharh al-Niqayah because it would include mubah (permissible) actions. Next, it examines the definition in Fath al-Qadir that sunnah is what the Prophet persistently did while sometimes leaving it, pointing out that this would also apply to obligatory acts like standing in prayer, which the Prophet sometimes omitted due to illness. Thus, al-Tahrir refined this by adding that the omission must be without excuse. However, the passage notes that even persistence without any omission, according to al-Hidayah, still indicates sunnah, as in the case of rinsing the mouth and i'tikaf. Fath al-Qadir reconciles this by noting that persistence without omission indicates sunnah only when the Prophet did not rebuke those who omitted it; otherwise it would indicate obligation. The author then offers his own resolution: if the Prophet persisted in an act without ever omitting it, it is an emphatic sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah); if he sometimes omitted it, it is a non-emphatic sunnah; and if he rebuked those who omitted it, it is obligatory. The passage also discusses a textual variant in the matn where 'sunan' is plural, explaining that the plural indicates that the sunan are independent, unlike the obligatory acts which are interdependent.

Key Terms
سنة (sunnah): An action that the Prophet consistently performed, sometimes leaving it, without rebuke for omission. Performance earns reward, omission earns blame but not punishment.
السنة المؤكدة (al-sunnah al-mu'akkadah): Emphatic sunnah: what the Prophet consistently performed without ever leaving it (e.g., the two rak'ahs of Fajr).
السنة غير المؤكدة (al-sunnah ghayr al-mu'akkadah): Non-emphatic sunnah: what the Prophet sometimes omitted (e.g., the sunnah before Zuhr after the first two rak'ahs).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's reconciliation, what indicates that an action is an emphatic sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah)?
A) The Prophet consistently performed it without ever leaving it.
B) The Prophet performed it sometimes and left it other times.
C) The Prophet rebuked those who did not perform it.
D) The action is mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of sunnah hinges on the Prophet's persistent practice and the absence of rebuke; this determines the level of emphasis and the ruling on its omission.

The Three Hanafi Positions on Washing Hands at the Start of Wudu
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ruling and timing of initial hand washing in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on washing hands at the start of wudu and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Washing hands at wudu's start: intra-Hanafi dispute on whether it is fard, sunnah that suffices, or sunnah that does not.

In this lesson, we explore a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the first step of wudu—washing the hands up to the wrists. The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents three distinct positions among Hanafi scholars, each based on different understandings of the primary texts. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to navigate ikhtilaf within the madhhab and to identify the operative ruling for practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the passage carefully and list the three views: (1) it is fard, (2) it is sunnah that suffices for the fard, (3) it is sunnah that does not suffice. Note the key scholars associated with each view.
The author mentions Fath al-Qadir, al-Asl, al-Kafi, and al-Sarakhsi.
2
Examine the evidence for each view
For each position, find the proof or reasoning provided. The fard view relies on the general sunnah practice and a statement from Imam Muhammad. The sunnah-that-suffices view analogizes to Fatihah. The third view emphasizes that the act of washing is separate from washing the forearms.
Note the objection from al-Dhakhirah: purification is the goal, so any washing suffices.
3
Analyze the timing issue
The passage also discusses whether this washing is before or after istinja. Identify the three sub-positions and the majority ruling from al-Mujtaba and Qadi Khan.
The majority view: sunnah both before and after istinja.
4
Understand the ruling based on impurity
The author distinguishes three scenarios: (a) actual impurity on hands → wajib, (b) suspected impurity (e.g., waking from sleep) → emphatic sunnah, (c) no impurity → regular sunnah. Note the critique of the restrictive view in Sharh al-Majma'.
The condition of sleep is incidental; the general practice of the Prophet is the basis.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Based on the author's discussion, determine which position is relied upon for fatwa. The text suggests the majority of mashayikh hold the first view (fard), but the operative ruling in later Hanafi practice is sunnah, and the hands must be washed again when washing the forearms.
The author does not explicitly give tarjih, but the weight of the mashayikh is mentioned.

قوله : (غسل يديه إلى رسغيه ابتداء يعني غسل اليدين ثلاثاً إلى رسغيه في ابتداء الوضوء سنة. والرسغ منتهى الكف عند المفصل. وفي ضياء الحلوم الرسغ بالغين المعجمة الكف في الذراع والقدم في الساق. اعلم أن في الواقع في الهداية وغيرها اتفاقي لأن من حكى وضوء رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كحمران مولى عثمان بن عفان وغيره قدّم فيه البداءة بغسل اليدين من غير تقييد بكونه عن نوم، وعلل له في الهداية بأن اليدين آلة حصول المقصود وهو تنظيف الآلة. وعلم بما قررناه أيضاً أن ما في شرح المجمع من أن السنة غسل اليدين للمستيقظ مقيدة بأن يكون نام غير مستنج أو كان على بدنه نجاسة حتى لو لم يكن كذلك لا يسنّ في حقه ضعيف، أو His statement: (He washes his hands up to his wrists at the beginning) means washing the hands three times up to the wrists at the beginning of wudu is sunnah. The wrist (rasgh) is the end of the palm at the joint. In Diya' al-Huloom, the rasgh with the dotted ghayn is the palm of the forearm and the foot of the leg. Know that concerning washing the hands at the beginning, there are three sayings: It is said that it is fard, and its being performed before [the face] is sunnah, and this was chosen in Fath al-Qadir, al-Mi'raj, and al-Khabbaziyyah, and to it points the statement of Muhammad in al-Asl: 'After washing the face, then he washes his forearms,' and he did not say 'hands,' so it is not obligatory to wash them a second time. It is said that it is sunnah that suffices for the fard, like al-Fatihah, which is wajib and suffices for the fard, and this was chosen in al-Kafi. And al-Sarakhsi said: It is sunnah that does not suffice for the fard, so he repeats washing them, their outer and inner parts. He said: This is the correct view according to me. Al-Dhakhirah objected, saying that the purpose is purification, and by whichever means it is achieved, the purpose is achieved. The apparent statement of the mashayikh is that the first madhhab is [the relied-upon]. There is a difference of opinion whether this washing is before or after istinja'; it is said it is sunnah only before, it is said only after, and it is said both before and after, and the majority went to this, as stated in al-Mujtaba, and Qadi Khan in al-Fatawa and al-Nihayah validated it. The evidence for this is that all who described the wudu of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) mentioned washing the hands first. As for its being sunnah before [istinja], in the hadith of Maymunah describing his ghusl, she narrated that he washed his hands before istinja'. The wisdom behind doing it before is to be thorough in removing the odor that may affect them. An objection was raised that the affected hand is the left, so it should be limited to it and restricted to cases of defecation. The answer is as in usul: wisdom is considered in the general type, and it is not necessary that it be present in every individual. Then know that the initial washing of the hands is obligatory if there is actual impurity on them, and it is sunnah at the beginning of wudu as we mentioned, and it is emphatic sunnah when there is suspected impurity, such as when waking from sleep. From this it is known that the condition of waking up mentioned in al-Hidayah and others is incidental, because those who described the wudu of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), such as Hamran, the freed slave of Uthman ibn Affan and others, started with washing the hands without restricting it to being from sleep. It was explained in al-Hidayah that the hands are the instrument of purification, so one begins by cleaning them. An objection was raised that this implies obligation, because that without which an obligatory act cannot be accomplished is itself obligatory. The answer is that there is a preventer from saying it is obligatory, namely that they are pure in reality and in ruling, so the washing up to the wrists is enough to achieve the purpose, which is cleaning the instrument. From what we have established, it is also known that what is in Sharh al-Majma', that washing the hands is sunnah for one who wakes up, restricted to one who slept without having performed istinja' or had impurity on his body, such that if it is not so, it is not sunnah for him, is weak. Rather, the intent is to negate the emphatic sunnah, not the sunnah itself. The manner of washing them is as mentioned in the commentaries: if the vessel is small such that it can be lifted, he does not put his hand in it but lifts it...
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the ruling on washing the hands up to the wrists at the very beginning of wudu. The author explains that this act is generally considered sunnah. However, there are three distinct Hanafi positions: (1) It is actually fard (obligatory) and its performance before the face is sunnah—this view is chosen by al-Kasani in Fath al-Qadir and others, supported by Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Asl. (2) It is sunnah but suffices for the fard obligation of washing the hands later, analogous to how al-Fatihah in prayer suffices for the fard of recitation. (3) It is sunnah that does not suffice, so one must repeat washing the hands when washing the forearms—this is the view of al-Sarakhsi, who claims it as the most correct. The author notes that al-Dhakhirah finds this problematic, as the purpose is purification achieved by any means. The passage then addresses the timing: should this washing be before or after istinja'? The majority say it is sunnah both before and after, based on the practice reported in hadith. The narration of Maymunah mentions washing hands before istinja' in ghusl, but the general description of wudu by Hamran shows washing hands first regardless. The ruling is categorized: obligatory if actual impurity is on the hands; emphatic sunnah if there is suspected impurity (e.g., after sleep); and regular sunnah otherwise. The author criticizes the view in Sharh al-Majma' that restricts the sunnah to those who slept and had impurity, deeming it weak. Finally, the manner is described: if the vessel can be lifted, one should not dip hands in.

Key Terms
الرسغ (al-rasgh): The wrist joint where the hand meets the forearm.
الاستنجاء (al-istinja'): Cleaning the private parts after using the toilet.
السنة المؤكدة (al-sunnah al-mu'akkadah): Emphatic sunnah: the Prophet never omitted it without excuse. Here, it applies to washing hands when waking from sleep (suspected impurity).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on washing the hands up to the wrists at the beginning of wudu when there is actual impurity on the hands?
A) It is sunnah.
B) It is wajib.
C) It is makruh.
D) It is fard.

Key Takeaway
The initial washing of hands in wudu is sunnah, but becomes wajib with actual impurity, and emphatic sunnah with suspected impurity; it does not suffice for washing the hands again within the forearms.

The Ruling on Washing Hands Before Wudu
Intra-Hanafi dispute with reconciliation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on washing hands before wudu, explain the evidence for each, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Washing hands before wudu is sunnah, not fard, but it can substitute for the obligatory wash.

At first glance, the rulings on washing hands before wudu may seem contradictory: some texts call it sunnah, others fard, and still others wajib. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute, showing that the apparent conflict is largely terminological. By the end, you will understand the preferred position and why it matters for your wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three views: (1) sunnah that substitutes for fard, (2) sunnah without substitution (al-Sarakhsi), (3) wajib when impurity is certain. State which is mufta bihi.
The text also mentions a view that it is fard, but that is reconciled with the sunnah view.
2
Examine the evidence for each position
The primary evidence is the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as narrated by Maimunah and Humran. Al-Sarakhsi's argument rests on the Quranic command to wash hands to the elbows, requiring a separate wash for reward.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The commentator explains that all agree the obligatory wash is fulfilled. The difference is whether the sunnah wash counts for reward. The preferred view is that it is sunnah and suffices for the fard.
The key is the Hanafi concept of 'sunnah that substitutes for fard'.
4
Apply the ruling to timing and special cases
Washing hands is sunnah before istinja (preferred), and it is mu'akkadah for one waking from sleep because of possible impurity. The restriction to sleep is incidental, not essential.
The hadith evidence does not restrict to sleeper; the wisdom is general cleanliness.
