⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قوله: (واستشكله في الذخيرة (الخ) قال العلامة الشيخ إسماعيل : الذي ينبغي حمل كلام السرخسي عليه هو وعدم النيابة من حيث ثواب الفرض لو أتى به مستقلاً قصداً إذا السنة لا تؤديه، ويؤيده اتفاقهم على سقوط يديه ثلاثاً أيضاً .اهـ.) واختلف في أن غسلهما قبل الاستنجاء أو بعده؛ فقيل سنة قبله فقط، وقيل بعده فقط ، وقيل قبله وبعده وإليه ذهب الأكثر كما صرح به في المجتبى، وصححه قاضي خان في الفتاوى وفي النهاية. ويستدل له بأن جميع من حكى وضوء رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قدم غسل اليدين، وأما سنيته قبله فيما رواه الجماعة من حديث ميمونة في صفة غسله وفيه أنها حكت غسل اليدين قبل الاستنجاء وحكمته قبله المبالغة في اليدين واجب إذا كانت النجاسة محققة فيهما وسنة عند ابتداء الوضوء كما ذكرنا وسنة مؤكدة عند توهم النجاسة كما استيقظ من النوم، فعلم بهذا أن قيد الاستيقاظ الواقع في الهداية وغيرها اتفاقي لأن من حكى وضوء الوجوب، لأنه ما لا يتوصل إلى الواجب إلا به فهو واجب وأجيب بأن هنا مانعاً . من القول بالوجوب وهو طهارتهما حقيقة وحكماً فكأن الغسل إلى الرسغين لأنه يكفي في حصول المقصود وهو تنظيف الآلة. وعلم بما قررناه أيضاً أن ما في شرح المجمع من أن السنة غسل اليدين للمستيقظ مقيدة بأن يكون نام غير مستنج أو كان على بدنه نجاسة حتى لو لم يكن كذلك لا يسنّ في حقه ضعيف، أو المراد نفي السنة المؤكدة لا أصلها.
It is a sunnah that substitutes for the fard, like al-Fatihah, for it is wajib and substitutes for the fard, and this was chosen in al-Kafi. Al-Sarakhsi said: It is a sunnah that does not substitute for the fard, so he washes them (the hands) again, their apparent and inner parts; he said: this is the most correct according to me. It was objected in al-Dhakhirah that the purpose is purification, so by whichever means it is achieved, the purpose is achieved. And the apparent opinion of the mashayikh is the first madhhab. (His saying: 'And he objected in al-Dhakhirah...' said al-Allamah al-Shaykh Isma'il: What should be understood from al-Sarakhsi's speech is the absence of substitution in terms of the reward of the fard if one performs it independently and intentionally, for the sunnah does not fulfill it. He is supported by their agreement on the removal of hadath without intention. I say: Based on this, apparently there is no conflict between the three opinions. The one who said it is fard meant it suffices for the fard, and performing this washing which suffices for the fard before wudu is sunnah—this is the meaning of the saying that it is a sunnah that substitutes for the fard. Al-Sarakhsi, a great imam with precise insight, when he saw the verse commanding washing the hands to the elbows, said: He should repeat washing the palms when washing the forearms so that he intentionally performs the commanded act and thus obtains the reward of the fard. Even if the fard was fulfilled by the first wash, it was fulfilled within the sunnah wash, so it is like its fulfillment by washing without intention, so it does not substitute for the complete wash in every aspect. Thus it is sunnah to repeat washing them for what we have said. For this reason, it is transmitted in al-Nahr from al-Dhakha'ir al-Ashrafiyyah that the sunnah when washing the forearms is to wash the hands three times as well. End quote.) And there is a difference of opinion regarding washing them before or after istinja; some say it is sunnah only before, some say only after, and some say before and after—the majority went with this as stated in al-Mujtaba, and Qadi Khan authenticated it in al-Fatawa and al-Nihayah. It is used as evidence that everyone who narrated the wudu of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) mentioned beginning with washing the hands. As for its being sunnah before istinja, it is based on the hadith narrated by the group from Maimunah describing his ghusl, in which she narrated washing the hands before istinja. The wisdom of it being before istinja is thoroughness in removing the odour of what touches them. It was objected that what touches is the left hand, so it should be confined to it and restricted to when one has defecated. The answer was given based on usul: wisdom is considered in the genus, and it is not necessary that it exist in every individual. Then know that beginning with washing the hands is wajib when impurity is confirmed on them, sunnah at the start of wudu as we mentioned, and mu'akkadah sunnah when impurity is suspected, such as when one wakes from sleep. Thus, by this, it is known that the restriction of waking mentioned in al-Hidayah and others is incidental, because everyone who narrated the wudu of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), like Humran the freed slave of Uthman ibn Affan and others, placed beginning with washing hands without restricting it to waking from sleep. He gave the reason in al-Hidayah: because the hands are the instrument of purification, so one begins by cleaning them. It was objected that this requires wujub, because that without which the wajib cannot be reached is itself wajib. The answer was given that there is a preventer from saying wujub, which is their actual and legal purity, so the washing is to the wrists because that suffices to achieve the purpose, which is cleaning the instrument. And from what we have established, it is also known that what is in Sharh al-Majma'—that the sunnah of washing the hands for the one who is awake is restricted to if he slept without performing istinja or if there was impurity on his body, so that if it is not like that, it is not sunnah for him—is weak, or the intended meaning is the negation of mu'akkadah sunnah, not the original sunnah.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on washing the hands at the beginning of wudu. The text presents multiple opinions: some say it is sunnah that substitutes for the required wash (like al-Fatihah in prayer), others say it is a sunnah that does not substitute, and yet others consider it wajib or fard. The commentator reconciles these by stating that the difference is merely in perspective—all agree that the obligatory washing is fulfilled, but the sunnah wash before wudu is recommended and does not replace the fard wash in terms of reward. The passage also discusses the timing relative to istinja (cleaning after relieving oneself) and whether it is specifically for the one waking from sleep. The preferred view is that washing hands is sunnah in general, and is mu'akkadah for the one who wakes from sleep due to the possibility of impurity. The hadith evidence from Maimunah and others is cited to support this. The commentator concludes that the restriction of 'waking' in al-Hidayah is incidental, not essential.

Key Terms
سنة تنوب عن الفرض (sunnah tanubu 'an al-fard): A recommended act that fulfills the obligatory requirement if performed with the proper intention, such as washing the hands before wudu.
الابتداء بغسل اليدين (al-ibtida' bi-ghusl al-yadayn): The act of washing the hands at the very beginning of wudu, before rinsing the mouth or nose, as a recommended practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on washing the hands at the beginning of wudu?
A) It is fard (obligatory).
B) It is sunnah, and a mu'akkadah sunnah for one waking from sleep.
C) It is wajib (necessary).
D) It is makruh (disliked).

Key Takeaway
Washing the hands before wudu is a sunnah that suffices for the obligatory wash, and it is especially recommended for those waking from sleep.

Washing Hands and Musta'mal Water Before Wudu
Explains method of washing hands and rulings on water becoming musta'mal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on washing hands before wudu, explain why inserting the hand is makruh tanzih, and apply the Hanafi distinction that musta'mal water removes khabath but not hadath.

Core Idea
Inserting a clean hand before washing is makruh tanzih; musta'mal water cleans impurity but not ritual impurity.

Before beginning wudu, the Sunnah is to wash hands three times. But what if you need to scoop water from a large vessel? The hadith advises caution because your hand might have touched impurity overnight. This lesson unpacks the rulings on hand washing, the disliked nature of inserting the hand, and the status of water that becomes musta'mal—a key Hanafi distinction between removing actual impurity (khabath) and ritual impurity (hadath).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled: washing hands before wudu (sunnah muakkadah) and inserting the hand into the water vessel (makruh tanzih). Also, the water becomes musta'mal only when it contacts the hand and separates.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof for the dislike is the hadith 'He does not know where his hand spent the night.' This implies a possibility of najasa, leading to tanzih, not haram.
The hadith is ahad, with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'does not know' indicates uncertainty, so the prohibition is for caution (sadd al-dhara'i'), making it makruh tanzih, not haram.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school, there is a dispute on using water taken by mouth: Abu Yusuf allows for washing najasa but not wudu; Muhammad holds it invalid because saliva makes it non-mutlaq. The relied-upon view is that it removes khabath.
The text presents the differences without explicit tarjih but states 'the correct is that it becomes musta'mal and removes khabath.'
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi is that musta'mal water removes khabath (actual impurity) but not hadath. This is the majority Hanafi position, followed by al-Bahr al-Raiq.

مستعملاً بشماله ويصبه على كفه اليمنى ويغسلها ثلاثاً، ثم يأخذ الإناء بيمينه ويصبه على كفه اليسرى ويغسلها ثلاثاً. وإن كان الإناء كبيراً لا يمكن رفعه؛ فإن كان معه أناء صغير يفعل كما ذكرنا، وإن لم يكن If the vessel is large and cannot be lifted, then if he has a small vessel he does as we mentioned, and if not, he inserts the fingers of his left hand together into the vessel and pours onto his right palm, then inserts his right hand into the vessel and washes the left. Al-Muhit justifies that bringing the two hands together each time is not sunnah, but al-Halabi objects that bringing together is sunnah as the hadiths indicate. The apparent view is that preferring the right hand over the left is for the sake of tayammun (starting with right), not for what is in al-Muhit, as is not hidden. They said: He should not insert the palm, such that if he inserts it, the water becomes musta'mal as stated in al-Mubtagha, meaning the water that contacts the palm when separated, not all the water in the vessel, as we will verify in the discussion on musta'mal. They said: It is makruh to insert the hand into the vessel before washing, due to the hadith, and it is makruh tanzih, because the prohibition is diverted from being haram by his saying 'for he does not know where his hand spent the night'. The prohibition applies to a small vessel or a large vessel if he has a small vessel, so he should not insert his hand into it at all; for a large vessel, it applies to inserting the palm. So in al-Mustasfa and others, while it is transmitted in al-Khaniyya that a person in minor impurity or major impurity, if he inserts his hand into the vessel for scooping and there is no impurity on it, does not spoil the water, and likewise if a pitcher falls into a well and he inserts his hand up to the elbow, it does not become musta'mal. In Sharh al-Aqta': It is makruh to perform wudu with water into which the awoken person inserted his hand, due to the possibility of najasa, just as it is makruh to perform wudu with water into which a child inserted his hand. In al-Mudmarat: If he has nothing to scoop with and both his hands are najis, he should order someone else to scoop with his hands to pour onto his hands to wash them; if he does not find, he sends a handkerchief into the water and takes its end with his hand, then takes it out of the well, washes his hand with its drops, then washes the other hand; or he takes a cloth with his teeth and washes his hands with the water that drips three times; if he does not find, he takes water with his mouth to wash his hands; if he cannot, he performs tayammum and prays, and no repetition is upon him. The end. And on the issue of taking with the mouth, there is difference, and the correct is that it becomes musta'mal and it removes khabath. The water becomes ... the two forearms that he washes his hands three times also. The end. His statement: 'The apparent view that preferring the right hand over the left is for the sake of tayammun' – it would have been apparent to say for the sake of necessity. Consider his statement: 'and it removes khabath' meaning he takes water with his mouth and washes his hands from najasa, even if the water becomes musta'mal, because musta'mal removes khabath, then he inserts his fingers into the vessel to remove hadath. In al-Dhakhira, al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned in al-Muntaqa from Abu Yusuf concerning a man who took water with his mouth from a vessel and washed his body or performed wudu with it: it is not permitted; but if he washed najasa from his body, it suffices. In Mufarriqat al-Faqih Abi Ja'far: A person with minor impurity had a little water and najasa on his body, so he took water with his mouth without intending to rinse his mouth, then washed his hands with it. He said: According to Muhammad's opinion, his hand does not become pure, and this is one of the two narrations from Abu Yusuf, because the water he took with his mouth mixed with saliva and ceased to be mutlaq water, thus it becomes like other liquids besides water, such as vinegar, broth, oil, and rosewater. Regarding washing the hands with other liquids besides mutlaq water, there are two narrations from Abu Yusuf: in one narration, it purifies like a garment; in another, it does not purify, unlike a garment. From Muhammad, one narration: the body is not purified, unlike a garment, for it is purified by consensus. The end.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage contains rulings on the initial washing of hands before wudu and the water used. It begins by describing the method: one takes the water vessel with the left hand, pours onto the right palm, washes three times, then takes with the right and pours onto the left. If the vessel is large, one uses a smaller vessel if available, otherwise inserts left fingers into the water to pour on right hand, then inserts right hand to wash left. The commentator al-Halabi notes that bringing hands together is sunnah, contrary to what al-Muhit says. The preference for the right hand is for tayammun (auspiciousness). Then the text discusses inserting the hand into the vessel: it is makruh tanzih before washing due to the hadith about not knowing where the hand spent the night. The prohibition applies to small vessels, and for large vessels, only inserting the palm is disliked. However, if one needs to scoop water, it is permissible and the water does not become musta'mal unless it separates from the hand. The passage then covers cases when both hands are najis: one may request another to pour, or use a handkerchief or cloth, or even use mouth to take water to wash hands, though this makes the water musta'mal but it still removes khabath (impurity). Finally, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute on using mouth water for washing: Abu Yusuf permits for washing najasa but not for wudu; Muhammad holds the water becomes non-mutlaq due to saliva, and there are two narrations on purifying with other liquids. The relied-upon ruling is that musta'mal water removes khabath and can be used for that purpose.

Key Terms
مستعمل (musta'mal): Water that has been used for a minor act of purification (like washing a limb) and has separated from the limb. It is pure but not purifying for ritual impurity.
كراهة تنزيه (karahah tanzih): A disliked act that is not sinful but better avoided; its opposite is makruh tahrimi.
خبث (khabath): Actual impurity (e.g., urine, blood) as opposed to hadath (ritual impurity).
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity requiring wudu or ghusl to remove; distinct from khabath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on inserting the hand into a large vessel of water before washing the hands for wudu if one has no other vessel?
A) It is haram because the hand might be najis.
B) It is makruh tanzih, but only if the palm is inserted; inserting fingers is allowed.
C) It is permissible without any dislike.
D) It makes all water musta'mal and invalid for wudu.

Key Takeaway
Musta'mal water removes actual impurity (khabath) but not ritual impurity (hadath); inserting hand before washing is makruh tanzih, not haram.

Ruling on Tasmiyah (Basmalah) at the Start of Wudu
Discusses whether saying Bismillah is wajib or sunnah at the beginning of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tasmiyah at the start of wudu, explain the evidence and reconciliation of hadiths, and distinguish the Shafi'i position with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
Tasmiyah is sunnah in wudu, not wajib; the Prophet's actions show it is not obligatory.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses whether saying 'Bismillah' at the start of wudu is obligatory or recommended. The Hanafi school holds it is sunnah (mustahabb), while the Shafi'i school considers it wajib. Understanding the evidence and reconciliation is crucial to grasp the Hanafi methodology.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules for tasmiyah at the start of wudu and its status.
The position is sunnah (mustahabb), not wajib.
2
Identify the opposing school
Note which school holds a different ruling and what it is.
Imam al-Shafi'i holds tasmiyah is wajib.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith used by the Shafi'i school and its apparent meaning.
Hadith: 'لا وضوء لمن لم يذكر اسم الله عليه'
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand how Hanafi scholars reconcile this hadith with other evidence.
They interpret it as negating virtue, not validity, using the Prophet's own actions.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference in rulings between the two schools.
Hanafi: wudu valid without tasmiyah. Shafi'i: wudu must be repeated if tasmiyah omitted.

وإن ضعف ارتقى إلى الحسن بكثرة طرقه وأجاب عنه الطحاوي في شرح الآثار بمعارضته في الصحيحين أنه عليه السلام لم يردّ السلام حين سلم عليه رجل حتى أقبل على الجدار فتيمم ثم ردّ السلام وما رواه أبو داود وغيره من حديث المهاجرين قنفد لما سلم على النبي عليه السلام وهو يتوضأ فلم يردّ عليه، فلما فرغ قال : إنه لم يمنعني أن أرد عليك إلا أني كنت على غير وضوء. فهذه تفيد عدم ذكره عليه السلام اسمه تعالى على غير طهارة، ومقتضاه انتفاؤه في أول الوضوء فيحمل الأول على نفي الفضيلة جمعاً بين الأحاديث وتعقبه في معراج الدراية وشرح المجمع بأنه يلزم منه أن لا تكون التسمية أفضل في ابتداء الوضوء، وأن يكون وضوءه عليه السلام خالياً عن التسمية ولا يجوز نسبة ترك الأفضل له عليه السلام وقد يدفع بأنه يجوز ترك الأفضل له تعليماً للجواز كوضوئه مرة مرة تعليماً لجوازه وهو واجب عليه وهو أعلى من المستحب، لكن يمكن الجمع بين اللسان به إلا على طهارة، ويدخل في التخصيص الأذكار المنقولة على بخلاف الثوب فإنه يطهر بالإجماع اهـ . قوله : (وقد يدفع أي يدفع قوله ولا يجوز نسبة ترك الأفضل له عليه السلام، والظاهر أن المراد به الترك to his wrists, at the beginning like the tasmiyah, siwak, washing his mouth and nose, combing his beard and fingers, and three times. His statement: 'to his wrists' etc., meaning washing the hands to the wrists is sunnah at the beginning etc., meaning all that he mentioned of washing to the wrists at the beginning of wudu is sunnah, not wajib, as stated in other texts. His statement: 'at the beginning' meaning at the start of wudu, not during it, as stated in al-Misbah that washing hands to wrists is sunnah at the start of wudu, not during it. His statement: 'like tasmiyah' meaning just as tasmiyah is sunnah at the beginning absolutely, so is washing the hands sunnah at the beginning absolutely, i.e., whether the wudu is from sleep or otherwise. Its transmitted wording is as transmitted from the Salaf: 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim, al-Hamdulillah ala din al-Islam'. From al-Webari, he seeks refuge then says bismillah. Al-Zahidi mentioned that if he precedes with something and then says bismillah, it is good. In al-Muhit: It is sunnah to make any dhikr, like 'al-Hamdulillah' or 'La ilaha illallah'. What the author mentioned that it is sunnah is the choice of al-Quduri. In al-Hidaya, the correct view is that it is mustahabb; it is said that this is the zahir al-riwayah. He says tasmiyah before istinja' and after it, that is the correct view except when uncovering and at the place of najasa, as in al-Khaniyya. Proof for obligation of tasmiyah is cited from the hadith of Abu Dawud: 'No wudu for one who does not mention the name of Allah upon it', and although weak, it becomes hasan due to many chains. Al-Tahawi in Sharh al-Athar replied by countering with the authentic hadith in the Sahihayn that he (peace be upon him) did not return the greeting when a man greeted him until he turned to the wall and performed tayammum, then returned the greeting, and the hadith of Muhajir Qunfudh who greeted the Prophet (peace be upon him) while he was performing wudu, and he did not respond until after finishing, saying 'Nothing prevented me from responding to you except that I was in a state without wudu'. These indicate that he did not mention Allah's name without purity, and its implication is its non-existence at the start of wudu, so the first hadith is interpreted as negating virtue to reconcile between them. This was criticized in Mi'raj al-Diraya and Sharh al-Majma' because it would imply that tasmiyah is not better at the start of wudu, and that his wudu was devoid of tasmiyah, and it is not permissible to attribute leaving the better to him. It may be refuted by saying that he may leave the better to teach permissibility, like his wudu with one washing each to teach its permissibility, and it is wajib upon him and it is higher than mustahabb. However, reconciliation can be made between the hadiths by saying that tasmiyah is from the requirements of its completion, so its mention is part of its perfection, and one who remembers it before wudu is compelled to mention it to establish this sunnah that completes the obligatory act, so it is specified from the general dhikr, and absolute dhikr is not a necessity of wudu. It is mustahabb not to utter it except with purity, and into the specification enter the narrated adhkar. Unlike the garment, for it is purified by consensus. The end. His statement: 'it may be refuted' means refuting his statement 'it is not permissible to attribute leaving the better to him', and the apparent meaning is leaving it habitually, by the context of the speech, so the aforementioned refutation does not apply. Consider. His statement: 'is specified from the general dhikr' meaning that for which purity is mustahabb, and it is specified only because absolute dhikr is not a necessity of wudu. Yes, the rest of the adhkar of wudu enter into the specified. There remains something: when tasmiyah is specified from what was mentioned, then the opposition mentioned by Tahawi is negated, so the hadith remains beneficial. (Narrated by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, Ahmad). And it is.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage concerns the sunan (recommended acts) of wudu, specifically the tasmiyah (saying 'Bismillah') at the start. The matn lists 'to the wrists' and others as sunnah. The commentary then focuses on tasmiyah: it is sunnah, not wajib, according to the Hanafi school. There is a dispute: some argue it is obligatory based on a hadith from Abu Dawud. The Hanafi response, via al-Tahawi, reconciles the hadiths: the Prophet (ﷺ) did not say tasmiyah at the beginning of wudu in some weak narrations, but the hadith of Abu Dawud is interpreted to mean losing virtue, not an absolute negation. The counter-argument from Mi'raj al-Diraya is that this would imply the Prophet (ﷺ) omitted a superior act, which is not permissible. The resolution is that the Prophet (ﷺ) sometimes omitted a superior act to teach its permissibility, but tasmiyah is actually a completion of the wudu, so it is distinct. Therefore, the tasmiyah is sunnah and part of the perfection, not wajib. The commentary also notes that the tasmiyah is said before and after istinja', but not when exposed or at the place of najasa. The preferred Hanafi position is that it is mustahabb (or sunnah), as in al-Hidaya.

Key Terms
تسمية (tasmiyah): Uttering 'Bismillah' at the start of an act, specifically at the beginning of wudu.
مستحب (mustahabb): A recommended act; its omission is not sinful but is disliked. In this context, tasmiyah is mustahabb according to al-Hidaya.
سنة (sunnah): A recommended act that the Prophet regularly performed; its omission is not sinful but is blameworthy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on saying 'Bismillah' at the start of wudu?
A) It is wajib, if omitted wudu is invalid.
B) It is sunnah (mustahabb), and wudu is valid without it.
C) It is haram to say it.
D) It is fard, like washing the face.

Key Takeaway
Tasmiyah at the start of wudu is sunnah, not wajib, and wudu is valid without it; the Prophet's actions and hadith reconciliation support this.

Ruling of Tasmiyah in Wudu: Wajib or Sunnah?
Discussion of tasmiyah ruling with evidence and intra-Hanafi debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on tasmiyah in wudu, citing the key hadith and the principle of nafy al-kamal, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Tasmiyah is sunnah mu'akkadah; the hadith 'لا وضوء لمن لم يذكر اسم الله عليه' negates perfection, not validity.

The ruling on saying 'Bismillah' before wudu is a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. The primary evidence is a hadith whose apparent meaning suggests obligation, but the Hanafi school interprets it as recommending the act. This lesson unpacks the evidence, the arguments for and against wujub, and the final mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key proof text
Locate the hadith cited in the passage: 'لا وضوء لمن لم يذكر اسم الله عليه'. Note its transmission status (ahad) and apparent meaning (negation of wudu).
The hadith is zanni al-thubut (single-narrator).
2
Analyze the meaning: negation of existence vs. perfection
The Hanafis argue that the negation (لا) could be either negating the existence (batil) or negating perfection (nafy al-kamal). Supporting evidence from other hadiths shows that wudu without tasmiyah is still accepted.
Nafy al-kamal is a key usul concept.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafis (e.g., in Fath al-Qadir) initially argued for wujub based on the apparent meaning. The passage refutes their arguments using three main points: weakness of a counter-hadith, logical inconsistency, and lack of companion transmission.
This is an example of ikhtilaf within the madhhab.
4
Understand the repellers (sawarif) that shift the ruling to sunnah
The passage mentions several repellers: the hadith about wudu with and without tasmiyah, absence of transmission from Uthman and Ali, and the bedouin hadith. Even though these repellers are criticized, the consensus tilts towards sunnah.
Tarjih authority: al-Marghinani and later scholars.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that tasmiyah is sunnah mu'akkadah. Wudu is valid without it, but it is recommended to say Bismillah. Forgetting: saying it later does not retroactively complete the sunnah for missed parts.
Affects teaching of reinforcement vs. invalidation.

The book of purification. The limbs of wudu are among its completions. Likewise in Miraj al-Diraya, and it is based on that what is meant by it is negation of virtue, and it is apparent in negation of permissibility, but it is a single-narrator report, so nothing can be added to the Book by it, so its requirement is obligation except there be a repeller. Some mentioned that the repeller is his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever performs wudu and Allah is named, it is purification for all his limbs, and whoever performs wudu and does not name Allah, it is purification for the limbs of his wudu' for it implies the existence of wudu without naming. But it is refuted from three aspects: first, weakness of the hadith as clarified in Fath al-Qadir. Second, leaving an obligation does not negate existence, it only causes deficiency. Third, it implies division of purification, which in our view is indivisible. Likewise in al-Mi'raj, and al-Akmal refuted it in his report by saying that if someone performs wudu and washes some of his limbs, purification is limited to what he washed. Yes, the human body in terms of what comes out of it is indivisible. And it was said the repeller is the lack of narration from Uthman and Ali regarding it when they narrated his wudu (peace be upon him). It was refuted in Fath al-Qadir by saying that lack of narration does not negate existence; how can it be after proof by another means? Do you not see that they did not narrate the khilal and siwak? There is no doubt that they are sunan. And it was mentioned in al-Mabsut that the repeller is that he did not teach it to the bedouin when he taught him wudu. It was refuted in Fath al-Qadir by saying that the hadith of the bedouin, though al-Tirmidhi deemed it good, Ibn al-Qattan declared it weak. He said: So consideration led to its obligation, but the validity of wudu does not depend on it because the pillar is only established by qat'i, and adding to the Book by a single-narrator report is not necessary unless we say binding. And he answered their statement 'there is no obligation in wudu' by saying that this hadith, being zanni in transmission but qat'i in meaning, and no repeller repelled it, gave obligation, and there is no preventing that. Whoever says it is zanni is prevented: if by zanni they mean its common meaning, then our case is not from it because the apparent negation is directed at the wudu and the ruling which is validity, and negation of perfection is a probability. If by zanni they mean something with probability even if preponderant, we do not accept that obligation is not established by it because zann is obligatory to follow even if it has probability. And one may say: his statement 'lack of narration does not negate existence' etc. is not complete in obligation because teaching cannot omit any obligation. If tasmiyah were obligatory, they would have mentioned it due to need to clarify it, unlike sunan. So this is a repeller safe from refutation. For obligation the difficulty returns. Ponder his statement: 'it is based on that what is meant by it is negation of virtue' i.e., what was mentioned in Miraj al-Diraya or what is based on that what is meant by the hadith 'no wudu for the one who does not mention the name of Allah upon it' is negation of virtue, although its apparent is negation of permissibility, so it would indicate it is a fard, but because it is ahad, it does not indicate that, so it is carried on obligation unless there is a repeller, then it is carried on sunniyya. His statement: 'and if by zanni what has probability even if preponderant' i.e., includes the probability of negation of perfection. His statement: 'and one may say that his statement' i.e., the statement of the author of Fath al-Qadir. His statement: 'and there is no doubt it is shared' in claiming commonality between the real and metaphorical meaning; ponder, ponder. His statement: 'I do not know of a hadith on it that is authentic' means concerning it specifically; otherwise it is understood from the authentic hadith 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with...' and the statement of evidence. And what they mean by zanni al-dalala is its common meaning, as the usuliyyun state, and there is no doubt it is a shared Sharia term, sometimes used to negate reality like 'no prayer for a menstruating woman except with a khimar' and 'no marriage except with witnesses', and sometimes used to negate perfection like 'no prayer for a runaway slave' and 'no prayer for a neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque'. So negation of reality is determined in the first by consensus, and in the second because it is well-known and accepted by the umma, so addition by its like to absolute texts is permissible, so shahada is a condition. When there is no preponderance for one of the two meanings, the hadith is zanni and by it sunna is established, and from it is the hadith of tasmiyah. And amazing is Kamal Ibn al-Humam: in this place he said that in the zanni dalala of the hadith of tasmiyah by its common meaning, and he established it in the chapter of conditions of prayer with the strongest wording by saying: there is no doubt in that because the probability of negation of perfection exists. So the truth is what our scholars are upon, that it is mustahab, how can it be otherwise when Imam Ahmad said: I do not know of a hadith on it that is authentic? And Allah knows best. And if one forgets tasmiyah at the beginning of wudu then remembers during it and says it, the sunna is not attained, unlike its like in eating. So in al-Tabyin, with the reasoning that wudu is a single act, unlike eating where each morsel is a new act. Therefore in al-Khaniyya: if one says 'every time I eat I will give in charity a dirham', he is obligated for each because each morsel he ate. But the verifier Ibn al-Humam said: this entails attainment of the sunna in the remainder, not rectifying what was missed. So its apparent, along with what precedes, is that if he forgets tasmiyah, his coming with it and not coming with it are equal, although the apparent of what is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj is that coming with it is desired, and his wording: if he forgets tasmiyah at the beginning of purification, he says it when he remembers before finishing so that wudu is not empty of it.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion probe discussing the ruling of tasmiyah (saying Bismillah) before wudu. The author examines the hadith 'لا وضوء لمن لم يذكر اسم الله عليه' and its implications. The apparent meaning negates the validity of wudu without tasmiyah, but because the hadith is ahad (single-narrator), it cannot impose an obligation beyond the Quran. Therefore, the majority of Hanafi scholars interpret it as negation of perfection (nafy al-kamal) rather than negation of existence, making tasmiyah sunnah mu'akkadah, not wajib. The author refutes arguments for wujub, including a counter-hadith, the lack of transmission from companions, and the teaching of the bedouin. The relied-upon mufta bihi position is that tasmiyah is mustahab/sunnah, and wudu is valid without it. The passage also addresses the issue of forgetting tasmiyah: if one remembers during wudu, saying it does not retroactively fulfill the sunnah for the earlier parts, because wudu is a single continuous act, unlike eating where each bite is separate.

Key Terms
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): Uttering the phrase 'Bismillah' (In the name of Allah) before beginning an act.
ظني الدلالة (zanni al-dalalah): A text that has a probable meaning, not definitive; it can be interpreted in more than one way.
نفي الكمال (nafy al-kamal): Negation of perfection; a phrase that denies the perfect form or complete reward, but not the essential validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi ruling regarding saying 'Bismillah' before wudu?
A) It is wajib and its omission invalidates wudu.
B) It is sunnah mu'akkadah and its omission does not invalidate wudu.
C) It is haram to say it.
D) It is fard and wudu is invalid without it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that tasmiyah is sunnah mu'akkadah; wudu is valid without it, but it is a recommended act of reinforcement.

Ruling of Siwak in Wudu: Sunnah Mu'akkadah
Ruling of siwak in wudu and evidence for its sunniyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on siwak during wudu and identify its classification as sunnah mu'akkadah.

Core Idea
Siwak is a sunnah mu'akkadah in wudu, not obligatory, but highly recommended.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on using the siwak (toothstick) during wudu. While some may assume it is obligatory due to prophetic practice, the Hanafi school classifies it as an emphasized sunnah. We will explore the textual evidence and the reliance of the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is using siwak during wudu. The Hanafi ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah, meaning highly recommended but not obligatory.
Sunnah mu'akkadah is a category in Hanafi fiqh between wajib and recommended.
2
Locate the evidence
The evidence comes from hadith reports that siwak is from the practices of the prophets. Though some chains are weak, they are strengthened by multiple narrations, making the practice hasan.
The source text mentions hadith from Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, and Ahmad.
3
Understand the classification
Within the Hanafi school, siwak is considered sunnah mu'akkadah. This means it is regularly practiced by the Prophet and the Companions, and neglect is disliked but not sinful.
There is no difference among Hanafis on this ruling.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, one should use siwak at the beginning of wudu or any time during. If omitted, wudu is still valid but the reward of following this sunnah is missed.
Some scholars recommend using siwak before wudu, but it is permissible at any time.

قوله (والسواك) أي استعماله لأنه اسم للخشبة. كذا في الشروح. ولا حاجة إليه لأن السواك يأتي بمعنى المصدر أيضاً كما ذكره ابن فارس في كتابه المسمى بمقياس اللغة، ولهذا قال في فتح القدير: أي الاستياك والجمع سوك ككتاب وكتب ويجوز رفعه وجره وهو ببسم الله اقطع ويروى أبتر) ويروى اجذم) وأدنى ما فيه الدلالة على السنية وهو المعتمد من المذهب الذي يعوّل عليه ويذهب قوله : فإتيانه بها وعدمه سواء قال الرملي : أي من أنه لا يكون آتياً بالسنة، أما أنه يأتي بها بعد غسل بعض أعضاء الوضوء فما في كلام الكمال والزيلعي ما يمنعه تأمله اهـ. مقدسي . قول المصنف : (والسواك) قال الرملي : السواك من الشرائع القديمة لحديث فيه ضعيف ومجهول قال النووي : فلعله اعتضد بطرق أخر فصار حسناً أربع من سنن المرسلين»(۲) وعد منها السواك اهـ. ذكره ابن قاسم العبادي في شرحه على أبي شجاع الشافعي رحمه الله . قوله: (ويجوز رفعه وجره (۱) روه ابن ماجه في كتاب الطهارة باب ۱۳۲ الترمذي في كتاب الصلاة باب ١٦٠. أحمد في مسنده (10./7) (۲) رواه الترمذي في كتاب النكاح باب .١ أحمد في مسنده (٤٢١/٥).
His statement (and the siwak): i.e., its use because it is a noun for the stick. Likewise in the commentaries. There is no need for it because siwak also comes with the meaning of the verbal noun, as Ibn Faris mentioned in his book called Maqayis al-Lugha. Therefore it was said in Fath al-Qadir: i.e., al-istiyak (the act of using siwak). The plural is suwak like kitab and kutub. It is permissible to raise it (nominative) or lower it (genitive). It is said 'bismillah aqta' (I cut with bismillah) and it is narrated 'abtar' and 'ajdham'. The least it contains is indication of sunniyya, which is the relied-upon view of the madhhab that is relied upon and followed. His statement: 'so his coming with it and not coming with it are equal' al-Ramli said: meaning that he is not coming with the sunna. As for his coming with it after washing some of the limbs of wudu, what is in the speech of al-Kamal and al-Zayla'i does not prevent it. Ponder it. End of al-Maqdisi. The statement of the author: (and the siwak) al-Ramli said: the siwak is from the old sharia due to a hadith in which there is weakness and unknown chains, al-Nawawi said: perhaps it is strengthened by other chains so it becomes hasan. 'Four from the sunan of the messengers' and among them siwak was counted. End. Mentioned by Ibn Qasim al-Abbadi in his commentary on Abu Shuja' al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. His statement: (and it is permissible to raise it and lower it) (1) narrated by Ibn Majah in the book of purification, chapter 132, al-Tirmidhi in the book of prayer, chapter 160, Ahmad in his Musnad (10/7). (2) narrated by al-Tirmidhi in the book of marriage, chapter 1, Ahmad in his Musnad (421/5).
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This section from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the author's statement about siwak (toothstick). The commentator clarifies that siwak can mean the stick itself or the act of using it. The Hanafi position is that siwak is sunnah during wudu, as indicated by hadith. Some hadith may be weak but are supported by other narrations. The relied-upon view is that siwak is sunnah mu'akkadah. The text also addresses a grammatical point about the word's declension and references prophetic traditions that siwak is from the practices of the prophets.

Key Terms
السواك (al-siwak): The toothstick or the act of cleaning teeth with it; a recommended practice in wudu and at other times.
السنة (al-sunnah): A recommended act that is not obligatory; omission does not invalidate the act but is disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on using siwak during wudu?
A) It is wajib.
B) It is sunnah mu'akkadah.
C) It is fard.
D) It is mubah (permissible) with no reward.

Key Takeaway
Siwak during wudu is an emphasized sunnah, not an obligation, and its practice is meritorious.

Ruling of Saying Bismillah at the Start of Wudu
Establishes Hanafi position that basmalah is mustahab, not wajib, using hadith and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on saying Bismillah at the start of wudu, explain why it is mustahab not wajib, and apply the rule regarding forgetting it initially.

Core Idea
The basmalah is mustahab because its hadith is zanni; if forgotten, saying it later does not redeem the sunnah.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on saying Bismillah at the beginning of wudu. We will see how the school uses usul al-fiqh concepts like zanni ad-dalalah and mushtarak to derive the ruling. We will also explore the qiyas comparing wudu to eating when the basmalah is forgotten.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Uttering 'Bismillah' at the start of wudu. What is the hukm level according to the Hanafi school?
The text labels it mustahab (recommended), not fard or wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
What evidence is cited? A hadith about basmalah (an ahad hadith). Recognize that this is a dalil naqli.
The hadith is not explicitly quoted but referenced generically as 'حديث التسمية'.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? It uses a mushtarak term that could negate either reality (haqiqah) or perfection (kamal). The Hanafis interpret it as negating perfection, thus establishing sunnah, not fard.
The term 'zanni ad-dalalah' means the meaning is probable, not definitive.
4
Understand the qiyas on forgetting
If the basmalah is forgotten initially and remembered during wudu, what should one do? The text compares wudu to eating: wudu is a single continuous act, unlike eating where each morsel is a separate act. Therefore, saying basmalah later does not redeem the sunnah for the completed parts.
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Ibn al-Humam questions the benefit of saying it later, but al-Siraj al-Wahhaj suggests it is still desirable.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Based on the majority of Hanafi scholars ('alayna'), the operative ruling is that basmalah is mustahab at the start. If forgotten, it is still recommended to say it during wudu so that the wudu is not entirely devoid of it.
This reflects the position in al-Tabyin and al-Khaniyyah, with the nuance from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.

ومرادهم من ظني الدلالة مشتركها كما صرح به الأصوليون، ولا شك أنه مشترك شرعي أطلق تارة وأريد به نفي الحقيقة نحو لا صلاة لحائض إلا بخمار ولا نكاح إلا بشهود، وأطلق تارة مراداً به نفي الكمال نحو لا صلاة للعبد الآبق ولا صلاة لجار المسجد إلا في المسجد فتعين نفي الحقيقة في الأول بالإجماع، وفي الثاني لأنه مشهور تلقته الأمة بالقبول، فتجوز الزيادة بمثله على النصوص المطلقة فكانت الشهادة شرطاً فعند عدم المرجح لأحد المعنيين كان الحديث ظنياً وبه تثبت السنة ومنه حديث التسمية، والعجب من الكمال ابن الهمام أنه في هذا الموضع ففي ظنية الدلالة عن حديث التسمية بمعنى مشتركها وأثبتها له في باب شروط الصلاة بأبلغ وجوه الإثبات بأن قال : ولا شك في ذلك لأن احتمال نفي الكمال قائم . فالحق ما عليه علماؤنا من أنها مستحبة كيف وقد قال الإمام أحمد : لا أعلم فيها حديثاً ثابتاً والله تعالى أعلم. ولو نسي التسمية في ابتداء الوضوء ثم ذكرها في خلاله فسمى لا تحصل السنة بخلاف نحوه في الأكل. كذا في التبيين معللاً بأن الوضوء عمل واحد بخلاف الأكل فإن كل لقمة فعل مبتدأ اهـ. ولهذا ذكر في الخانية : لو قال كلما أكلت أن أتصدق بدرهم فعليه بكل لأن كل لقمة أكل . لكن قال المحقق ابن الهمام هو إنما يستلزم في الأكل تحصيل السنة في الباقي لا استدراك ما فات .اهـ. وظاهره مع ما قبله أنه إذا نسي التسمية فإتيانه بها وعدمه سواء، مع أن ظاهر ما في السراج الوهاج أن الإتيان بها مطلوب ولفظة : فإن نسي التسمية في أول الطهارة أتى بها إذا ذكرها قبل الفراغ حتى لا يخلو الوضوء منها.
And their intent from 'zanni ad-dalalah' is its common meaning as the usuliyyun stated, and there is no doubt that it is a legal homonym used sometimes to negate reality, like 'no prayer for a menstruating woman except with a khimar' and 'no marriage except with witnesses', and sometimes used to negate perfection, like 'no prayer for a runaway slave' and 'no prayer for a neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque'. So negating reality is determined in the first by consensus, and in the second because it is well-known and accepted by the ummah, so addition by the like of it to absolute texts is permissible, thus testimony became a condition. So in the absence of a preferred meaning, the hadith is zanni, and by it the sunnah is established, and from that is the hadith of basmalah. And amazing is al-Kamal Ibn al-Humam that he, in this place, regarding the zanniyyah of the hadith of basmalah in the sense of its common meaning, he established it in the chapter on conditions of prayer with the strongest form of establishment by saying: 'There is no doubt in that because the probability of negating perfection is present.' So the truth is what our scholars hold: that it is recommended. How indeed, since Imam Ahmad said: 'I know no authentic hadith about it.' And Allah knows best. If one forgets the basmalah at the beginning of wudu then remembers during it, so he says it, the sunnah is not attained, unlike the similar case in eating. This is stated in al-Tabyin, reasoned that wudu is one action, unlike eating, for each morsel is a new act. End quote. For this reason, it is mentioned in al-Khaniyyah: If he says, 'Whenever I eat, I will give a dirham in charity,' then he is obligated for each morsel because each morsel is eaten. But the muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam said: 'It [the analogy] only entails, in eating, attaining the sunnah in the remainder, not making up for what was missed.' End quote. And its apparent meaning, with what preceded it, is that when he forgets the basmalah, his doing it or not doing it is the same, even though the apparent meaning of al-Siraj al-Wahhaj is that doing it is sought; and its wording: 'For if he forgets the basmalah at the beginning of taharah, he performs it when he remembers before finishing so that the wudu is not devoid of it.'
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding saying 'Bismillah' at the start of wudu. The author clarifies that the hadith about basmalah is zanni ad-dalalah (probable in meaning) because it uses a shared term (mushtarak) that can mean either negation of reality or negation of perfection. By consensus, certain well-known hadiths indicate negation of reality, but basmalah falls under negation of perfection, making it sunnah, not fard. The Hanafi position is that it is mustahab (recommended), not obligatory. The text then addresses forgetting the basmalah: if one forgets initially and remembers during wudu, saying it at that point does not count as the sunnah, because wudu is a single continuous act, unlike eating where each morsel is a separate act. However, there is a difference of opinion: Ibn al-Humam holds that saying it later is futile, while al-Siraj al-Wahhaj suggests it is still desirable. The preferred Hanafi view is that the basmalah is mustahab and should be said at the beginning.

Key Terms
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): Uttering 'Bismillah' at the start of an action, specifically wudu in this passage
ظني الدلالة (zanni al-dalalah): A textual evidence whose meaning is probable, not definitive, due to being open to interpretation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must stop and repeat the wudu.
B) He should say it then, and the sunnah is attained for the entire wudu.
C) He should say it then, but the sunnah is not attained for the parts already washed.
D) He should not say it, as it would be a bid'ah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that saying Bismillah at the start of wudu is mustahab (recommended), not wajib, due to the probability of the hadith; if forgotten, saying it later does not compensate for the missing sunnah.

Ruling and Etiquette of Siwak
Ruling and proof regarding siwak's status, timing, method, and inter-madhhab difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on siwak, explain the preferred time and method of use, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i position regarding siwak for prayer.

Core Idea
Siwak is a recommended sunnah for wudu, not for each prayer; one siwak suffices for all prayers with the same wudu.

Siwak (toothstick) is a highly recommended act in Islam, with a rich textual basis. In this lesson, we examine its legal status according to the Hanafi school, the evidence from hadith, the optimal time during wudu, and a key point of difference with the Shafi'i school. Understanding this will help you apply the sunnah correctly in your daily worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal status of siwak
From the text, determine whether siwak is fard, wajib, sunnah, or mustahab. Note the evidence and the reasoning behind the Hanafi classification.
The text explicitly says it is mustahab (recommended).
2
Locate the evidence (dalil)
Find the primary hadith cited and understand why, despite being weak, it is considered acceptable due to multiple supporting chains.
This shows the Hanafi approach to hadith grading.
3
Identify the preferred time during wudu
The text mentions a difference of opinion: at madmadah (mouth rinse) or before wudu. Determine which view is preferred and why.
The majority prefer madmadah for thoroughness.
4
Note the inter-madhhab dispute with Shafi'i
Understand that the Shafi'i school considers siwak sunnah for every prayer, while Hanafis consider it for wudu. Follow the Hanafi reasoning and the practical consequence.
The blood argument is used to refute the Shafi'i position.
5
Review the method of using siwak
Learn the recommended technique: starting from the right, cleaning crosswise, minimum three strokes, using a soft twig, holding it in the right hand.
These details ensure the maximum benefit and sunnah.

The author's statement: 'And the siwak.' Al-Ramli said: Siwak is from the ancient rites due to a hadith that is weak and unknown; al-Nawawi said: Perhaps it is strengthened by other chains, so it becomes good. 'Four from the sunan of the messengers' and from them is siwak. End quote. Mentioned by Ibn Qasim al-Abbadi in his commentary on Abi Shuja' al-Shafi'i. His statement: 'And it is permissible to raise it and lower it.' He corrected the more apparent to indicate that beginning with it is also a sunnah. And he used as evidence in al-Kafi for its being sunnah that he (peace be upon him) persisted in it while sometimes leaving it. And this was refuted in Fath al-Qadir by saying his persistence regarding wudu is not known. As for what has come regarding the superiority of prayer performed with siwak over others, it indicates recommendation, and that is the truth. Therefore al-Sharih and others chose recommendation. And there is disagreement about its time: in al-Nihayah and Fath al-Qadir it is at the time of rinsing the mouth; in al-Bada'i' and al-Mujtaba it is before wudu. The majority are on the first, and it is preffered because it is more complete in cleansing. And it is not specific to wudu, but is recommended in situations: for yellowing of teeth, change of odor, rising from sleep, standing for prayer, upon first entering the house, when meeting people, and when reciting the Quran. This is in Fath al-Qadir and others. But their statement that it is recommended when standing for prayer contradicts what they narrated: that with us it is for wudu, not for prayer, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And al-Siraj al-Hindi reasoned in Sharh al-Hidayah that if he uses siwak for prayer and blood comes out, it is najis by consensus, even if it is not a nullifier according to al-Shafi'i. And they said: The practical consequence of the disagreement appears in the case of one who prays multiple prayers with one wudu: our view is that one siwak for the wudu suffices; according to al-Shafi'i, he should use siwak for each prayer. Its method: he should clean the upper teeth, lower teeth, and the palate, and start from the right side; the minimum is three times on the upper and three on the lower with three waters. It is recommended that it be soft, without knots, about the thickness of the little finger and a span in length, from the known bitter trees. He should clean crosswise, not lengthwise, as that harms the gums. Al-Ghaznawi said: clean lengthwise and crosswise, but the majority are on the first. It is recommended to hold it in the right hand, and the sunnah in how to take it is to put the little finger of your right hand under the siwak.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the siwak. It covers its legal status, timing, method, and a dispute with the Shafi'i school. The author begins by clarifying that siwak is from the sunan (recommended acts), based on a hadith that, though weak, is strengthened by multiple chains. The Hanafi school holds that siwak is mustahab (recommended), not wajib. The strongest evidence is the hadith that prayer with siwak is superior. There is a difference of opinion on when to use siwak during wudu: most scholars say during mouth-rinsing (madmadah), while others say before starting wudu. The first is preferred for thoroughness. Siwak is not limited to wudu; it is recommended in many situations. A significant point of difference with al-Shafi'i is that Hanafis consider siwak to be for wudu, not for each prayer. If one prays multiple prayers with one wudu, one siwak suffices, whereas Shafi'is recommend siwak for each prayer. The passage then details the proper method: cleaning upper and lower teeth, starting from the right, minimum three strokes each, using a soft twig about a span long, cleaning crosswise, and holding it in the right hand.

Key Terms
السواك (al-siwak): A twig used for cleaning teeth, also the act of cleaning with it
الاستياك (al-istiyak): The verbal noun of using siwak
المضمضة (al-madmadah): Rinsing the mouth during wudu

Siwak: Legal Status, Proof, and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Commentary defining siwak's ruling, proof, and madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on siwak (mustahab), justify it with the dalil, and explain the practical difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions regarding wudu and prayer.

Core Idea
Siwak is mustahab, not sunnah; its recommendation is linked to wudu, not prayer.

Siwak (tooth-stick) is a highly recommended practice in the Hanafi school, but its precise legal status and occasion have been debated. The commentary of Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that siwak is mustahab (recommended), not a sunnah mu'akkada, based on the evidence and the analysis of prophetic practice. Additionally, a key inter-madhhab difference emerges: Hanafis attach siwak to wudu, while Shafi'is attach it to prayer, affecting practical rulings. This lesson will guide you through the evidence, the dispute, and the tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled on? What is the hukm level according to the Hanafi school? Is it sunnah, mustahab, or wajib? Note that the passage begins by correcting a previous view and establishes istihbab as the correct position.
The author explicitly says 'الاستحباب وهو الحق' – recommendation is the truth.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof used to support istihbab. The passage cites a hadith about the superiority of prayer with siwak over seventy-five prayers without it. How does this hadith lead to the ruling of mustahab?
The hadith is 'صلاة بسواك أفضل من خمس وسبعين صلاة بغير سواك'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith indicates recommendation. The term 'afdal' (better) shows superiority, which in Hanafi usul indicates mustahab, not obligation (wajib). Also, the passage refutes the claim of muwazaba (consistency) from the Prophet regarding wudu.
The commentator argues that consistent practice is not proven for wudu, so the hadith only gives recommendation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafis say siwak is for wudu, Shafi'is say it is for prayer. What is the practical consequence (thamarah)? The passage clarifies: one who prays multiple prayers with one wudu needs only one siwak for Hanafis, but Shafi'is require siwak for each prayer.
The dispute arises from whether the hadith is understood as attached to prayer or to wudu.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Which position is preferred? The Hanafi school follows the view that siwak is for wudu, and the reason given is that if one uses siwak for prayer and blood comes out, it is impure but does not nullify wudu, so siwak is linked to wudu. The mufta bihi is istihbab.
The tarjih is supported by al-Siraj al-Hindi and the majority of Hanafi scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صحح الأظهر ليفيد أن الابتداء به سنة أيضاً واستدل في الكافي للسنية بأنه عليه السلام واظب عليه مع الترك، وتعقبه في فتح القدير بأنه لم تعلم المواظبة منه على الوضوء. وأما ما ورد من أفضلية الصلاة التي بسواك على غيرها فيدل على الاستحباب وهو الحق، ولذا الشارخ وغيره الاستحباب واختلف في وقته ففي النهاية وفتح القدير أنه عند المضمضة، وفي البدائع والمجتبى قبل الوضوء، والأكثر على الأول وهو الأولى لأنه الأكمل في الإنقاء. وليس هو من خصائص الوضوء بل يستحب في مواضع لاصفرار السن وتغير الرائحة والقيام من النوم والقيام إلى الصلاة وأول ما يدخل البيت وعند اجتماع الناس وعند قراءة القرآن . كذا في فتح القدير وغيره لكن قولهم يستحب عند القيام إلى الصلاة ينافي ما نقلوه من أنه عندنا للوضوء لا للصلاة خلافاً للشافعي وعلله السراج الهندي في شرح الهداية بأنه إذا استاك للصلاة بما يخرج منه دم وهو نجس بالإجماع وإن لم يكن ناقضاً عند الشافعي وقالوا : فائدة الخلاف تظهر فيمن صلى بوضوء واحد صلوات يكفيه السواك للوضوء عندنا، وعند الشافعي يستاك لكل صلاة. وكيفيته أن يستاك أعالي الأسنان وأسافلها والحنك ويبتدىء من الجانب الأيمن، وأقله ثلاث في الأعالي وثلاث في الأسافل بثلاث مياه. واستحب أن يكون ليناً من غير عقد في غلط الأصبع وطول شبر من الأشجار المرة المعروفة. ويستاك عرضاً لا طولاً لأنه يخرج لحم الأسنان. وقال الغزنوي : يستاك طولاً وعرضاً والأكثر على الأول. ويستحب إمساكه باليد اليمنى والسنة في كيفية أخذه أن تجعل الخنصر من بمينك أسفل السواك تحته والبنصر والوسطى والسبابة فوقه واجعل الإبهام أسفل رأسه تحته كما وراه ابن مسعود، ولا يقبض القبضة على السواك فإن ذلك يورث الباسور، ويبدأ بالأسنان العليا من الجانب الأيمن ثم الأيسر ثم السفلى كذلك، كذا في شرح منية المصلي، وتقوم الأصبع أو الخرقة الخشنة مقامه عند فقده أو عدم أسنانه في تحصيل الثواب لا عند وجوده .. والأفضل أن يبدأ بالسبابة اليسرى ثم باليمنى، والعلك يقوم مقامه للمرأة لكون المواظبة عليه تضعف أسنانها فيستحب لها فعله، ومنافعه كثيرة منها أنه يرضي الرب ويسخط الشيطان ومن خشي من السواك القيء تركه . ويكره أن يستاك مضطجعاً فإنه يورث كبر الطحال. كذا في السراج الوهاج.
The most apparent (opinion) is corrected to benefit that beginning with it (siwak) is also a sunnah, and it was argued in al-Kafi for it being sunnah because he (the Prophet) consistently performed it with occasional omission, but al-Fath al-Qadir refuted that it is not known that he consistently performed wudu. As for what has been reported about the superiority of prayer with siwak over others, it indicates recommendation (istihbab), and that is the truth. Hence the commentator and others (preferred) recommendation. There is disagreement about its time: in al-Nihayah and Fath al-Qadir it is at the time of rinsing (maddmada); in al-Bada'i' and al-Mujtaba it is before wudu. The majority hold the former, and it is more appropriate because it is more complete in cleansing. It is not specific to wudu; it is recommended in places: for yellowing of teeth, change of odor, waking from sleep, standing for prayer, first entering the house, at gatherings of people, and when reciting Quran. This is in Fath al-Qadir etc. But their statement that it is recommended when standing for prayer contradicts what they transmitted that according to us (Hanafis) it is for wudu, not for prayer, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Al-Siraj al-Hindi in Sharh al-Hidayah explained it: if one uses siwak for prayer and blood comes out, it is impure by consensus, even if not a nullifier according to al-Shafi'i. They said: the benefit of the difference appears in one who prays multiple prayers with one wudu; for us, one siwak suffices for wudu; according to al-Shafi'i, he uses siwak for each prayer. Its method: he uses siwak on the upper teeth, lower teeth, and palate, starting from the right side. Minimum is three strokes on upper and three on lower with three waters. It is recommended that it be soft, without knots, about a finger's breadth in thickness, a handspan in length, from the known bitter trees. It is used widthwise, not lengthwise, because that pulls out the flesh of the gums. Al-Ghaznawi said: use lengthwise and widthwise, but most hold the former. It is recommended to hold it with the right hand. The sunnah in how to hold it is to place the little finger below the siwak, with the ring, middle, and index fingers above, and the thumb below its head, as reported by Ibn Mas'ud. One should not grip tightly on the siwak, as that causes hemorrhoids. He begins with the upper teeth on the right side, then left, then lower similarly, as in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. A finger or a rough cloth suffices in its place when it is absent or when there are no teeth, for obtaining reward, not when it is present. It is better to start with the left index finger then right. Chewing gum takes its place for women, because constant use weakens their teeth; so it is recommended for them to use it. Its benefits are many: it pleases the Lord, angers Satan. Whoever fears vomiting from siwak should leave it. It is disliked to use siwak while lying down, as it causes enlargement of the spleen. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the rulings of siwak (tooth-stick) as part of the sunan of wudu. The author, Ibn Nujaym, engages in a critical refinement of earlier positions. He first addresses the disagreement on whether siwak is a sunnah (emphasized) or mustahab (recommended). The correct view is that it is mustahab, not a sunnah mu'akkada, because consistent practice (muwazaba) is not proven from the Prophet regarding wudu. The time for siwak is disputed: the majority hold it is at the time of rinsing the mouth (maddmada), which is more complete for cleansing. Siwak is not specific to wudu but recommended in various situations, including for prayer. However, a key Hanafi principle is that siwak is for wudu, not for prayer per se, unlike the Shafi'i school where it is recommended for each prayer. The practical difference is that if one prays multiple prayers with one wudu, one siwak suffices for the Hanafis, while Shafi'is would use siwak for each prayer. The passage then details the proper method of using siwak: number of strokes, material, direction, and hand position, as well as substitutes and disliked practices.

Key Terms
السواك (al-siwak): A stick or similar object used to clean teeth and mouth, recommended in wudu and other occasions.
المضمضة (al-madmada): Rinsing the mouth with water, one of the sunan of wudu.
الاستحباب (al-istihbab): Legal recommendation, less than sunnah mu'akkada; doing it is rewarded, leaving it is not sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) For each prayer
B) Once at the time of wudu
C) Only when teeth are yellow
D) At the beginning of each prayer

Key Takeaway
Siwak is mustahab, and the Hanafi school attaches it to wudu, not to each prayer, affecting how many times it is used.

Siwak: Method, Substitutes, and Etiquettes
Practical rulings on how to use siwak, its substitutes, and etiquettes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the recommended method of using siwak, identify substitutes when unavailable, and list the disliked actions and benefits.

Core Idea
Siwak must be used widthwise on teeth, with specific hand position, and substitutes like finger or cloth suffice for reward when siwak is absent.

Beyond its legal status, siwak has a detailed prescribed method: how to hold it, which teeth to start with, the number of strokes, and the proper material. The passage also covers substitutes for those without teeth or unable to use siwak, and makes exceptions for women and those who fear vomiting. Additionally, it mentions the disliked action of using siwak while lying down. This lesson focuses on the practical execution and associated rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended method
The passage details the physical method: using siwak on upper and lower teeth and palate, starting from the right side. Minimum strokes are three on top and three on bottom with three waters. Use widthwise (not lengthwise) to avoid gum injury.
The majority hold widthwise; al-Ghaznawi also allowed lengthwise but most prefer the former.
2
Learn the hand position
The sunnah way to hold the siwak: little finger below, ring, middle, and index fingers above, thumb below the head. Avoid gripping tightly as it may cause hemorrhoids.
This comes from a narration of Ibn Mas'ud.
3
Understand substitutes
When siwak is not available, or for those without teeth, a finger or rough cloth can substitute to obtain reward, but only when siwak is absent. Not when it is present.
For women, chewing gum is recommended because the constant use of a stick may weaken their teeth.
4
Note disliked and recommended occasions
Siwak is recommended in many situations (yellow teeth, bad odor, waking, prayer, entering home, gatherings, reciting Quran). It is disliked to use siwak while lying down, as it may cause spleen enlargement. Also, if one fears vomiting, it should be left.
Benefits include pleasing Allah and angering Satan.

Madmada and Istinshaq: Rulings and Method
Commentary defining rulings and method of madmada and istinshaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on madmada and istinshaq, explain the proof from the hadith, and apply the method of mubalagha correctly.

Core Idea
Madmada and istinshaq are sunnah mu'akkadah, not fard; the command 'exaggerate' proves emphasis, and the exception for fasting confirms they are not obligatory.

This lesson unpacks the definitions, rulings, and method of rinsing the mouth (madmada) and nose (istinshaq) during wudu. Based on the commentary of Bahr al-Raiq and the hadith from the four Sunan, we will see why these acts are strongly recommended but not obligatory, and how to perform them properly. The discussion also clarifies a common practical question: what happens if you swallow the water after rinsing?

Guided Path
1
Define the Acts Linguistically and Technically
Identify the linguistic and technical meanings of madmada (thoroughly rinsing the entire mouth) and istinshaq (reaching water to the soft part of the nose). This terminological foundation is critical for understanding their ruling.
The author distinguishes between linguistic and technical definitions to clarify the scope of each act.
2
Determine the Ruling and Its Evidence (Dalil)
Establish that madmada and istinshaq are sunnah mu'akkadah in the Hanafi school, not fard. Cite the hadith 'Exaggerate in rinsing mouth and nose unless you are fasting' from Sunan al-Arba'a. Analyze the wajh al-dalalah: the imperative 'baligh' indicates emphasis, and the exception proves the act is not obligatory (wajib/fard).
The cross-madhhab difference: Shafi'is consider them fard; Hanafis rely on the exception to show non-obligation.
3
Explain the Method of Mubalagha (Exaggeration)
Describe the recommended method: for madmada, gargling (gharghara); for istinshaq, sniffling and expelling (istinthar). Note that mubalagha is sunnah only for non-fasting persons; fasting persons should avoid it to prevent water entering the stomach or lungs.
There is a minor intra-Hanafi difference on the extent of istinshaq: some say to the hard part, others to the bone.
4
Address Practical Issues (Swallowing and Method of Taking Water)
Analyze the rulings: if one swallows the water after madmada, it suffices (the act is valid), but it is better to expel the water because it becomes used water. Also, the method of taking water: using one handful for both madmada and istinshaq is permissible, but some authorities prohibit using the same handful for istinshaq alone.
These details reflect the thoroughness of Hanafi fiqh in deriving sub-rulings from the primary ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ( وغسل فمه وأنفه عدل عن المضمضة والاستنشاق المذكورين في أصله الوافي للاختصار، وما في الشرح من أن الغسل يشعر بالاستيعاب فكان أولى فيه نظر، فإن المضمضة كذلك فإنها اصطلاحاً استيعاب الماء جميع الفم كما في الخلاصة، وفي اللغة التحريك والاستنشاق لغة من النشق وهو جذب الماء ونحوه بريح الأنف إلى داخله، واصطلاحاً إيصال الماء إلى مارن الأنف. كذا في الخلاصة والمارن مالان من والمبالغة سنة فيهما أيضاً كذا في الوافي لحديث أصحاب السنن الأربعة «بالغ في المضمضة والاستنشاق إلا أن تكون صائماً» (١) وهي في المضمضة بالغرغرة وفي الاستنشاق بالاستنثار كذا في الكافي والاستنثاء دفع الماء ونحوه للخروج من الأنف، وقد وافقه في فتح القدير على الأول. وقال في الثاني كما في الخلاصة إلى ما اشتدّ من الأنف. وفي الخلاصة : هي في المضمضة أن يصل إلى رأس الحلق. وقال شمس الأئمة هي في المضمضة أن يدير الماء في فيه من جانب إلى جانب، والأولى ما في فتح القدير ذكره بعضهم. ولو تمضمض وابتلع أجزأه لأن المج ليس من حقيقتها، والأفضل أن يلقيه لأنه ماء مستعمل. وفي الظهيرية وإذا أخذ الماء بكفه فمضمض ببعضه واستنشق بالباقي جاز وبخلاف ذلك لا يجوز وفي المجتبى : لو رفع الماء من كف واحدة للمضمضة جاز وللاستنشاق لا يجوز ينافي ذلك كما لا ينافي استحبابه عند غيره مما مر على أنه يبعد استحبابه في الصلاة التي هي مناجاة للرب تعالى سيما عند بعد العهد من الوضوء مع ما فيها من قراءة القرآن التي يستحب استعماله عندها وحضور الملائكة عندها مع أنهم استحبوه عند مجامع الناس، فبالأولى مع الملائكة . قال في هدية ابن العماد روى جابر رضي الله تعالى عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «إذا قام أحدكم يصلي من الليل فليستك فإن أحدكم إذا قرأ في صلاته وضع ملك فاه على فيه لا يخرج من فيه شيء إلا دخل فم الملك أسنده البيهقي في شعب الإيمان .اهـ. قوله: (وبخلاف ذلك لا يجوز) الماء У ولم يمجه عدم حضور
His statement (And he washes his mouth and nose). He turned from the terms 'madmada' and 'istinshaq' mentioned in its original (al-Wafi) for brevity. What is in the commentary that 'ghusl' implies thoroughness and so it is better is questionable, because madmada is likewise; technically it is the thorough rinsing of all the mouth as in al-Khulasa, while linguistically it is moving. Istinshaq linguistically is from 'nashq' which is drawing water etc. with the breath into the nose; technically it is reaching water to the soft part of the nose. Thus in al-Khulasa, and al-marun is what is soft. Exaggeration (mubalagha) is sunnah in both, as in al-Wafi due to the hadith from the four Sunan: 'Exaggerate in rinsing mouth and nose unless you are fasting.' Exaggeration in madmada is by gargling, and in istinshaq by sniffling (istinthar), as in al-Kafi. Istinthar is expelling water etc. from the nose. He agreed with him in Fath al-Qadir on the first. And he said regarding the second as in al-Khulasa 'to the hard part of the nose.' In al-Khulasa: 'In madmada, it is to reach the back of the throat.' Shams al-A'imma said: 'In madmada, he moves water around in his mouth from side to side.' The best is what is in Fath al-Qadir mentioned by some. If he rinses and swallows, it suffices because gurgling (majj) is not part of its reality; but it is better to expel it because it is used water. In al-Zahiriyya: 'If he takes water in his hand and rinses part of it and sniffs the remainder, it is permissible; otherwise not.' In al-Mujtaba: 'If he lifts water from one hand for madmada, it is permissible; for istinshaq, it is not permissible.' This contradicts, as it does not contradict its recommendation elsewhere as has passed; but it seems far-fetched to recommend it in prayer, which is intimate conversation with the Almighty, especially after a long period from wudu along with the recitation of Quran for which siwak is recommended, and the presence of angels, while they recommended it at gatherings of people, so even more so with angels. In Hadiyyat Ibn al-'Imad: Jabir reported that the Prophet said: 'When one of you stands to pray at night, let him use siwak, for when one recites in his prayer, an angel places his mouth upon his mouth; nothing comes out of his mouth except it enters the angel's mouth' (al-Bayhaqi in Shu'ab al-Iman). His statement: (And otherwise it is not permissible). The water? And he did not spit it out, absence of presence.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'he washes his mouth and nose' from the base text. The author notes that the base text used 'ghusl' (washing) instead of the more specific terms 'madmada' and 'istinshaq' for brevity. He rejects the argument that 'ghusl' implies thoroughness, as 'madmada' technically also implies thorough rinsing of the entire mouth. He defines the technical terms: madmada is to thoroughly rinse the mouth; istinshaq is to draw water into the nose reaching the soft part. The sunnah of mubalagha (exaggeration) is confirmed by a hadith from the four Sunan, except for a fasting person. The method of mubalagha in madmada is gargling (gharghara), and in istinshaq is istinthar (sniffling and expelling). There is a difference of opinion on the extent of mubalagha in istinshaq: some say to the hard part of the nose, others to the bone. If one rinses and swallows the water, it suffices because spitting out (majj) is not part of the reality, but it is better to spit out used water. Regarding the method of taking water: if one takes water in one hand and uses part for madmada and the rest for istinshaq, it is permissible, but some say it is not permissible for istinshaq from the same hand. The text then transitions to discussing the recommendation of siwak again, connecting it to prayer and the presence of angels, citing a hadith. The end of the passage is corrupt and unclear.

Key Terms
المضمضة (al-madmada): Technically: thoroughly rinsing the entire mouth. Linguistically: moving water in the mouth.
الاستنشاق (al-istinshaq): Technically: reaching water to the soft part of the nose (marun). Linguistically: drawing water into the nose by breath.
المبالغة (al-mubalagha): Exaggeration in rinsing; for madmada by gargling, for istinshaq by sniffling and expelling (istinthar).

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to this passage, if a person rinses their mouth (madmada) and then swallows the water, what is the ruling?
A) It invalidates the wudu
B) It suffices, but spitting out is better
C) It is fard to spit it out
D) It is haram to swallow

Key Takeaway
Madmada and istinshaq are sunnah mu'akkadah in wudu, proven by the hadith commanding exaggeration except for fasting, and their method includes gargling and sniffling with expulsion.

Rulings and Etiquettes of the Siwak
Describes the correct way to use siwak, its substitutes, and disliked actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the proper etiquettes of using the siwak, identify which actions are Sunnah, makruh, or substitute, and explain the Hanafi ruling on siwak.

Core Idea
Siwak is a confirmed Sunnah — its etiquettes carry reward, but using it lying down is makruh.

The siwak is a beloved practice of the Prophet ﷺ, and Hanafi fiqh details its proper use. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, outlines the recommended method, substitutes when unavailable, and actions to avoid. Understanding these rulings ensures that we practice this Sunnah correctly, reaping its spiritual and physical benefits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage rules about siwak: it is a Sunnah (confirmed Sunnah). Note that it is not wajib.
Hanafi: Sunnah, others: wajib – but we follow the Hanafi position.
2
List the recommended etiquettes
Extract the specific recommended actions: proper grip (fingers above, thumb below), starting with upper right teeth, then upper left, then lower, etc.
These are based on the narration of Ibn Mas'ud.
3
Identify the disliked action
Find the action that is makruh: using siwak while lying down, as it causes enlargement of the spleen.
Makruh tanzihi – not sinful but best avoided.
4
Understand substitutes
When siwak is unavailable or one has no teeth, a finger or rough cloth can substitute for reward. For women, gum is recommended because siwak may weaken teeth.
Substitutes only apply when siwak is absent.
5
Recognize the benefits and exceptions
The passage mentions benefits (pleasing Allah, enraging Satan) and an exception: if one fears vomiting, he should abandon siwak.
This shows fiqh balances benefit and harm.

Source Text
